CAUTION.—The large sale for
“Eumenthol JUJUBES” has led to a
number of imitations being manufac-
tured under the name of menthol and
other jujubes. It is important, there-
fore, when buying to be sure and get
HUDSON’S Eumenthol Jujubes. You
may be asked to buy other jujubes,
and be told they are “just as good” as
Hudson’s Eumenthol Jujubes. This
proves that these imitations and sub-
stitutes have no reputation of their
own, and can only be sold on the good
repute and high character of Hud-
son’s Eumenthol Jujubes.

The Red Page
Th e Repertory Theatre
Ihe fact that a member of the Adelaide
Literary Theatre dubbed the Melbourne
Repertory scheme, though in a qualified and
no unkindly sense, “commercial,” leads Gre-
gan McMahon to explain:
The basic idea of the Adelaide scheme
seems to be the elimination of all “commer-
cialism m the managerial department, and
o all professionalism in the stage depart-
ment. I must confess that I do not sympa-
thise with either of these ideas when carried
to. extremes. A repertory theatre should cer-
tainly beware of the “commercialism” that
sets large profits before everything else, but
L C !r binab f n of an ? atem ‘ business managers,
amateur actors, and— worst of all —amateur
producer cannot hope to attain to what is
best in the presentation of these plays
it an art scheme cannot hold its own
commercially within its own sphere, and if
the few enthusiasts who profess to support
it won t pay a fair price to see the ‘ -o, ood
play and so enable it to be produced in a
dignified way, then I think it best to give up
the idea altogether until the Labor party has
ne . a ..RtRe more for “the finer attributes
of civilisation.” It is an undignified proce-
dure, I think, to seek escape from the pay-
ment of authors fees. The unfortunate
nnS 101 ' wi t lG St , man who should be
paid. Why should art always go hat in
kaad asking for charity? If J ,hire is a?
audience for these plays in the Common-
wealth and I think there is a small one—it
should be made t<o pay fairly for their proper
production. This is equally a matter in which
the public needs education. My idea is that
the thing should be put on a business basis
from the very start-
Regarding “professionalism” in the stage
department much confusion of terms exists.
All pxofessionals are not ipso facto bad
actors, and all amateurs are not ipso facto
good actors. Let us delete these terms, and
use the word actors.’’ A good actor is one
who, having natural ability and artistic per-
ception, has acquired in addition the tech-
nique and ease that experience alone can
give. My endeavor in Melbourne is to get
together a company that has the first two
gifts, and to give such of them as lack ex-
perience the teaching and experience without
which their ‘sense of artistic beauty and
moyement can never “ring true.” If inex-
perienced actors and inexperienced producers
whether amateur or professional—conspire
to give performances of repertory plays, they
are doing more harm than good to the cause
they seek to help. In my opinion the audi-
ence that exists already will never attain to
larger proportions, or take the repertory
moyement seriously in this country until this
habit of asking for charity, and producing
, a l ‘T s , wl * :a ae of well meaning but un-
skilled labor, is dropped.
The English repertory theatres are now
bolding their own with the large commercial
enterprises, because from the jump they were
run by business men with experienced produ-
cers and experienced actors. I certainly
started with some amateurs—l call them
actors now—but I made sure that, firstly,
they were amateurs of natural ability and
artistic perception, and I didn’t let them
act. at all until they had gained a lot of ex-
perience under my direction (I am assuming
that I possess sufficient culture and artistic
sense and. experience myself to constitute
myself their director). I have from time to
time discreetly mixed them with professionals
of equal artistic perception and experience,
and the result has been a good and evenly
balanced performance.
My “pupils” do not “reflect” or imitate
me. I endeavor to get an effect from them
as a body, and I see that I get it. If I find
I can’t, I don’t produce that particular play.
.Regarding scenery—the use of draperies
with a symbolic suggestion” is very effective
m certain plays—the classic and poetic
drama—but I don’t see that this method of
symbolic, suggestion” can be used in modern
conversational drama or comedy, as Adelaide
suggests. You can’t escape the use of scen-
ery, and payment for same.
Lastly, damn all “committees, boards and
advisers”! How can a. committee of laymen
cast plays? It is essentially one man’s
work, an expert’s work. A good producer cafi
visualise a whole play, and the interworking
of every character in it. If he can’t do this
before he starts rehearsals, he is no good as a
producer. A producer must work indepen-
dently of everybody. Again, the practice of
fixing on plays a year ahead is unworkable.
With the limited supply of actors on hand,
the play must be ehosen to suit the people
available, otherwise many square pegs will be
found in round holes. I used to try to make
out a programme of plays for a year’s use,
but soon realised the futility of it. Now
eight weeks before the date of a season, I
ascertain what people are available, go
through all the plays available, and fix on
a play to suit those particular people. At
this moment I have got casts together that
can give convincing performances of my
July series of plays; but I wouldn’t dare
say what I shall do the season after. I don’t
try actors on the plays; I try the plays on
them, until I find one that sits easiiy on
them. When in the future I shall have got
two. complete working companies, each con-
taining a type or personality suited to any
casting exigency, then I shall perhaps ven-
ture to *»ap out programmes in advance.
The only use for a committee is ticket selling
and propaganda.
Adelaide dubs my enterprise “commercial.”
•It is—in the sense that I pay author’s roy-
alties, even Australian authors; theatre
rents; union wages to stage hands; a scenic
artist of simple but artistic views; a goodly
whack to the daily and weekly press; and if
there is anything over after that I have no
compunction in paying it to my butcher and
Greengrocer a>s a recompense for my daily
2 hours’ toil.

grapmeal and Economic (Whitcombe and
Tombs, Melb.; 4s. 6d.), edited' by Prof.
a , nd wn I tt ™ b / A - W. Jose, T. Grif-
fith laylor and W. G. Woolnough, is, as a
geography of N.S.W., a very admirable geo-
graphy, though its appearance at this date
has prevented the inclusion of the statistics
available from the latest census, and the
tuple editorship has the defect of occasional
overlapping, compensated for, of course, by
the advantage of expert knowledge for the
separate sections, hardly likely to be coitn-
wuw ln 011 tI. man—except a man like

elucidative. But then, who wants a
geography of N.S.W.? A section of Aus-

has no geography separate from the con-

is an inalienable part. The physical condi-

sidered as, part of the general conditions of
the Commonwealth ’Tis pity that all this
learning, meant for the Commonwealth,
should be given to Ma. Also, it is a pitv
If Work 1 t he sch ° ol children

a suburb of N.S.W. The work itself will
not foster this delusion, but its mere exist-

G. H. Souter state? the obvious:—
You may see her in the Summer, as she strolls
around “The Block,”
In her funny hat and gossamer, and latest model
frock.
And suppose you want a smile from her, you’ll get
it, as you pass;
For she’s not a bit afraid of you—nor any other ass
You may see her in the “Office,” at her “Reming-
ton” or “Bliek,”
With her cheery word for Harry, and her pensive
look for Dick.
You can meet her at the “Oval,” when the football
season’s on,
Where she “barracks” most unblushingly for Jimmie,
Joe or John.
And you’ll find her, any Sunday, where the surf’s
a-rolling free,
For she knows she takes the “biscuit” in her natty
neck-to-knee”!
When you watch her in the “Panto,” dressed in
pretty things that fit,
DanC /<j|„ acrobatic dances—well—she’s absolutely
But you’ll also find her, sometimes, with a baby
on her knee,
Waiting patiently for Percy, when he’s out upon the
spree;
Making light of silent sorrow, and the, carking care
that grips—
With a dagger in her bosom, and ft smile 1 upon her
lips!
But no matter where you see her you will find her
bright and brave—
Whether whirling gaily down the days, or toiling
like a slave.
And she’s not a sylph; she’s not a saint, she’s neither
peach” nor “pearl,”
For she’s something better than the lot—a true Aus-
tralian Girl!

just worth print:—
THE WATTLE BLOSSOM GIRL.
With flying locks, azure eyes, or maybe brown,
Deft hand upon the bridle rein
(Perhaps, it differs not, astride).
Incarnate poetry of Life’s Spring.
Over dry and dusty saltbush plain
Reviving e’en the “whaler’s” weary eye,
Leaves with the zephyrs of her transit
Echoes of laughter.
Sunburned lass, felt hatted, bare feet.
Jogging cherfully ’tween the gnarled trees
Gold splashed to match the tangled hair.
Herding mournful cattle to the byre.
Singing snatches of some ancient rime
(Relic of her mother’s youthful day) ;
Chaffing jestingly the dawdling laggards
With peals of honest laughter.
Pegtop skirt, dainty footwear,
Stockings of cobweb mesh, hat of rare design,
Claiming the moving tram with expert ease,
Satchel in hand. Disdaining with a look
The seat possessed by boorish male.
Passing the latest quip with every friend.
Gauging the prospects of a Sunday trip.
Punctuations—laughter.
Office, farmhouse, dairy, station, factory;
Working, idling, battling with a mate,
Know always then the Wattle Blossom Girl
Brimful of happy humor. And quaintly
Like silver flakes dropping on a velvet bed
Tinkles her laugh.

“Love’s Pilgrlmage”
Under the title, Love's Pilgrimage
(Heinemann, per Sydney Book Club), the
author of The Jungle has written his auto-
biography. It is the story of a genius who mar-
ried. Not that Upton Sinclair is a genius ; he
is a fine writer, with an understanding of the
type he depicts, a Socialist, an advocate of
fasting as a cure for all evils, except the
evil of being born a genius or the evil of
having married a genius. In this book, un-
fortunately in its English dress disfigured
by a crass eulogy by a person of the name
of Hall Caine, Sinclair tells with frank-
ness the story of the genius who was snared
by marriage. It is a terrible narrative, done
with fine art —one of the few real books that
have recently come from the United States.
Into it the author has poured all his fierce
convictions —his Socialism, his ideal of free
love, even his Fletcherism; but these, to
him so important matters, are but subsidi-
ary in the reader’s eyes to his portrayal
of the creative mind of his hero. In most
works of fiction a genius must be taken on
no evidence but the author’s word. The
author tells you that his hero is a genius;
the other characters assume , that he is a
genius; but neither his conversation nor
his actions convince you. But in Love's
Pilgrimage, Sinclair buttresses his assertion
by actually printing the plots of the books
and plays his hero wrote or planned, and
quoting pages of his poetry—from which
the reader will learn that Thyrsis was no
poet, but assuredly a great and original
writer. ,
Jt
Thyrsis had the drunken father necessary
for a genius; and he carried right through
the section of his life here detailed his over-
powering conviction that he was not as
other men, his paralysing frankness, his
devotion to his art irrespective of the scorn
of the world, his naive obsession with each
new child of his brain. The psychology of
the creative force is here admirably and ex-
haustively done. The struggles of this
modern genius, set in America, for recogni-
tion and for the wherewithal of existence
are faithfully set forth; but the theme is
not the apathy of the world, but the influ-
ence of marriage upon this sort of mind.
For Thyrsis married. This book is that
tragedy.
Corydon came to him, dominated by him,
willing that they should live as bro-
ther and sister. But poverty—the
poverty that dominated all his life —’
interposed. Their forced propinquity made
the inevitable happen, and then there
was the baby. And Thyrsis, infatuated only
with his art, has the dreadful feeling of
having been caught in a snare. Suppose
he, the genius, should become domestic!
J*
It was a conspiracy of Nature, a con-
spiracy of all the world! It was a Snare!
He had been the sport of cosmic forces,
of the blind arid irresistible reproductive
impulse of Nature. Step by step he had been
driven, be had played his part according
to the plan. He had hesitated and debated
and resolved and decided—thinking that he
had something to do with it all! But now
he looked back, and saw himself as a leaf
swept along by a torrent. And all the while
the torrent had known its destination! lie
had had many plans and mhny purposes,
but always Nature had had but one plan
and one purpose—"which was the Child !
Dismayed as he was, Thyrsis could not
but smile as he realised it. The artist in
him appreciated the technique of the per-
formance. How cunningly it had all been
hidden, how shrewdly the bait had been
selected !•.'.,.
He marvelled at the prodigality that
Nature had displayed. Sh£ would create a,
million eggs to make one salmon; and she
had created a million emotions to make one
baby! What poems she had written for
them—-what songs she had composed for
them! She had strewn the pathway with
roses before them, she had filled their mouths
with honey, and their ears with the sound
of sweet music ; she had blinded them, she
had stunned them, she had sent them
drunken and reeling to their fate !. ~.
Those very powers and supremacies that
he had thought were Iris protection—were
they not, also, a part of the Snare? His
culture and his artistry, his visions and his
exaltations—what had they been but a lure
for the female? Even his genius, his mirac-
lulous, ineffable had been the
plume of the partridge, the crowning glorv
before which his mate had capitulated!...'.
He saw himself in new and grotesque lights ;
he was the young rooster, strutting before
his mate, and thrilling with the knowledge
of his own importance ! He was each of the
barnyard creatures by turn, and Corydon
was each of the fascinated females. And
somewhere, perhaps, stood the farmer, smil-
ing' complacently—for should there not be
somewhere a farmer in this universal barn-
yard ?....
And so he must stand by Corydon—he
must bring her food, that she might give her
child life! And for that purpose she would
hold him.... She would not know this, of
course; she would do it blindly and instinct-
ively, as she had done everything so far.
She would do it by those same generous
and beautiful qualities that had made him
hers! Therein lay the deadly nature of the
Snare. The cords of it were woven out of
love and tenderness, and they were wound
about his very heart-strings, so that it would
kill him to pull them loose. And he would
never pull them loose And the end would
be simply the withering and shrivelling of
those radiant qualities which he called his
genius—qualities which were so precious to
him, but about which Nature knew nothing!
_ So as an undercurrent to Thyrsis’ con-
tinual struggle for recognition, his incessant
poverty, his utter inability to compromise,
runs the sordid story of the worries of
domesticity, the increasing call upon the
artist to sink his individuality in the hus-
band. We see, and he sees, the effect of his
devotion to his ideal upon his wife and child
we see his attempts to break from the mesh
of marriage, his escape into the solitary life
in the woods, his wife’s attempt to adapt
her needs and her child’s needs to their grim
necessities. Thyrsis was no abnormal super-
man ; he loved, his wife, as she loved him.
despite the quarrels that came inevitably
from the disparity of their temperaments.
He had to make his wife and child suffer,
but was human enough to grieve that he had
made them suffer. The author has no brief
for the genius: the wife’s point of view gets
equal prominence in this long record of suf-
fering. The trouble lay in the, mere fact that
these two separate souls were “mashed toge-
ther in the stewpot of domesticity.” “Each
was in some way a handicap and a torment
to the other; and as ever, they had in their
hours of anguish no resource save to sit
down and reason it out together, and absolve
each other from blame.”
It is impossible in a review to adumbrate
all that the author has put into this loosely
sprawling book. But mention must be made
of the steps that led to Thyrsis’ conversion
to Socialism, with a full precis of the books
that led him to this new religion; and of the
final incident of Corydon’s love affair, and
on what rational principles her husband
dealt with it. There are, too, scathing criti-
cisms of the life of the idle rich, character
studies of magazine folk, descriptions of
farming life; and a fat section of love
letters. A book of absorbing interest, real-
istically frank. Almost a great book.
A book of 78 Australian Bungalow anc
Cottage Home Designs (N.S.W. Bookstall
Co.; iis. (id.) is a step in the evolution of
national architecture suitable for this climat
and this people. The object has beer
to provide home-builders with plans anc
elevations, from which a selection ma;
be made or a suggestion borrowed
lhe need is fairly catered for, thougl
the fact that all the designs are from
the pen of one architect (Reginald A. Pre
vost) militates against its variety. It should
be worth this publisher’s while to produce
another such book to which a dozen of the
leading Australian architects might contri
bute their characteristic conceptions of the
rational and artistic Australian dwelling
The present work needlessly antagonises the
reader by a colored cover design, showing
cottage decorated in four garish colors—this
however, is probably merely the artist’s poly-
chromatic fancy. The designs happily show
the almost complete elimination of that
abode of cold horror —the drawing-room, anc
the substitution of the large living room, and
ample provision of verandah space for sleep-
ing out. But in many cases the narrow hall,
or tunnel, is still retained. The designs are
plain, and will be easily understood by the
amateur house-builder.
A word for Thomas Mosher, the benevo-
lent pirate of Portland, Maine, U.S.A. The
Bibelot, ’a reprint of poetry and prose for
book-lovers, chosen in part from scarce edi-
tions_ and sources not generally known,” is
a dainty little monthly publication, sold by
subscription only, a dollar a year. This
year, for instance, the reprints include: A
Frelude to Life, by Arthur Symons; The
Dying of Francis Donne, by Ernest Dow-
son; L nder an Elm Tree, by William Morris :
With an Essay by W. B. Yeats, and a selec-
tion fjom the poems of Whitman. Moshers
Catalogue is the most artistic and interest-
ing catalogue of books published.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Last Lap!
Berlin, Wednesday.—The German Reichstag has
voted, en bloc, the Army and Navy Bills providing
for substantial increases in both services.-
—Cheering Cable.
Great Britain, population, ,000,000.
Germany, population, 60,000,000.
—Latest Estimates.
What a beautiful thing is Peace, my friends!
A beautiful thing, indeed!
By the Lord’s good Grace it gives us space
To furnish the things we need.
And what are the things we need, my friends,
Ere our preparations cease ?. ...
While we double our Fleet I will just repeat
What a wonderful thing is Peace! f
What a horrible thing is War, my friends!
A horrible thing, indeed!
Its very mention implies intention
Of arming with frenzied speed !
We’ve only four million folk, my friends,
To keep the foe from our d00r!....
Though the thought gives pain, I must say again
What a horri le thing is War!
What a marvellous thing is Trade, my friends!
A marvellous thing, indeed !
Though old John Bull had a mighty pull,
We’re fast reducing his lead !
We’re only a yard behind, my friends,
And I think he’s half-afraid
That he’ll lose the race —it’s a killing pace!
What a marvellous thing is Trade!
What a capital thing is Cash, my friends!
A capital thing, indeed!
By the tariff’s aid we have got the trade,
And the sequel all may read.
And what will the sequel be, my friends?
Well, the coming years will show.
We’ve the Cash,
We’ve the Trade,
We’ve the new keels laid,
And there’s only a lap to go !
N.S.W.
R. McGEDDON.
Ballad of Exile.
God’s flag’s unfurled! His banner of the stars
Hangs down the shining, wind-swept vault of ever-
lasting Heaven;
And here, shut in as by a prison’s bars,
My heart becomes a singing loom for thoughts of
thee to weave in!
O little one, whose rose-red trembling mouth
Breathes kisses sweet across the leagues that lie so
grim between us ;
It weaves for thee —far in the distant south —
Thoughts like the thoughts of lonely Mars when reft
of his dear Venus!
That goddess fair, born of the silver foam,
Had eyes like thine—lit with the rays of love’s star-
blazing splendor ;
And, like a ceaseless, swinging metronome,
My heart athwart the measured miles throbs forth its
homage tender !
My far-off love! from some Oythera’s isle
Thou art in these new Pagan days like young Astarte
risen ;
And all the gods, to win thy blessed smile,
Would leave their proud Olympic home to make
thine arms their prison!
To know thine amorous arms and close-pressed
lips—
To know thy deep, fire-pulsing love and all its name-
less glory ;
Were cause enough to launch the fighting ships,
And light a blood-red battle-flame on each sea pro-
montory !
Some call them fools —who fought for ancient
Troy—
But I, who know thine honeyed kiss —I know far
better ;
To spend with thee one hour of fervent joy
Were worth all else —worth death's eclipse and pain’s
Promethean fetter !
We who have swooned with love’s most sweet
excess —■
Whose hearts have heard the singing stars lift up
their chant elysian :
We know why Troy was sacked —yea, I confess
I’d fight the leaguered world for thee, my goddess
neo-Grecian !
My life I’d give—a thing of little worth —
To win you back, O star-eyed love, were you from
me thief-taken ;
Red war I’d wage against the blazing earth —
For hearts run o’er with bitterness when lips are
lip-forsaken 1
Remember, love, the dear undying days—
The days when heart cried out to heart and soul on
soul lay beating;
These are the things that set the Troys ablaze —
That lift on high the gleaming blades and set the
steel flesh-eating!
Oh, woman’s love! Oh, strange and mystic spell,
What fighting thoughts thou stirrest up—red flame
each brain-cell flashes ;
O bond electric, Time’s strong manacle—*
When thou art broken, hope decays and life’s but
grief and ashes!
In exile here and far, O love, from thee—
Far from the breast whereon my head in days of
yore found haven ;
I breathe a prayer, a strong man’s litany—
No moan of pale, ascetic priest with cowled forehead
shaven;
It is the song of fierce, tempestuous love—
Of hope and fear that shake my soul as with the
storm’s dread thunder ;
Hear it, ye stars that shine like eyes above—
Are all her heart’s dear thoughts of me? Ah, gods
of mine, I wonder 1
For we were born to fare forth hand in hand —
Were born, O love, to walk through life and on to
death together;
Unlike that long-dead Venus —fickle, bland—
Thou art not swayed as sways the wind the light, in-
constant feather !
Safe in thy soul I know I have my place—
I know that for thine exile’s kiss thy heart, dear one,
is lonely;
Oh, but to see thy lovely, tear-wet face,
As here beneath the stars I stand and swear I love
You Only!
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.

THE £5 BABY BOBUS.
We do not say that the birth rate of Australia is as high as it should be.... The practical way to set about remedying
the want of natural increase is by a policy of immigration.—Daily paper.
“Of course it’s nice and human enough in its way, my dear; but you must admit that such a business is considered
somewhat vulgar and low-class amongst our set, who know that everything this awful country requires may
be obtained from ’ome—and certainly of a much more matured and superior quality.”


The Bulletin

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
Vie Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official alivays is.

cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, JUNE 27, 1912.
THE COOL, DAMP CENTRE OF
AUSTRALIA.
The beginning of the new Federal session
finds the Australian Government, as usual,
carefully shirking the biggest questions of
the day lest it should lose its job. Just now
the two problems which overshadow all
others are the tariff, and the construction
of the great railway which is required to
promote settlement in the Northern Terri-
tory. These are matters of some difficulty
to the weak Fisher and the weak
Fisher combination, because they, to
a great extent, throw the Cabinet on
its own responsibility. Almost every other
point that is large enough to be worth
calling a point was settled for it in advance
by the party organisation. It inherits its
instructions, sailing directions, chart, or
whatever the thing may be called. All it
has to do is to be honest, sober, respectable,
not too mad in a financial sense, not too
wild in its language, not too obvious in its
pursuit of strange girls, and reasonably in-
telligent. On the tariff question, _ however,
the Parliamentary Labor party is left to
make up its own mind. And despite the fact
that the tariff is utterly inefficient, that the
influx of cheap labor goods has reached a
point at which even the, importers’ papers
begin to grow alarmed, and that the outflow
of gold is so great that the beginnings of a
panic are already discernible, it can’t make
up its mind. Not having any clear direc-
tions or any hereditary barometer, and hav-
ing a rooted dislike of responsibility, it lets
things drift. In the matter of the Northern
Territory it is in the same situation. It
has taken over the Territory, partly because
its predecessors carried the matter so far
that it could hardly do otherwise. It knows
that the place must be settled if the White
Australia policy is to have the semblance
of a chance. It is perfectly well aware that
the only hope of filling up the North with
population lies in the building of a railway
to make the great Federal property access-
ible. But the Fisher Government has no
clear instructions as to the route of that
essential railway. Owing to the fact that
every Labor member is just as anxious as
any other member to drag every possible
railway round by his own door and ash-
barrel and dog-kennel, the organisation
which draws up the Labor programme was
reluctant to give definite instructions. It
left the Ministry and the Federal Parlia-
mentary Labor party to do the best they
could, and in their dislike of responsibility
they do as little as possible.
In the matter of the railway to the West
it was easy to come to a decision. Westralia
is less isolated than the Territory, inasmuch
as it lies on the direct route to Europe and
to the headquarters of the Empire, while the
other place doesn’t. It is less of a danger
to Australia, and is less in need of defence,
because it lies further from the Brown and
Yellow Peril. It has less claim on the Com-
monwealth, because it isn’t Commonwealth
property, whereas the Territory is. There is
every reason why, on purely national prin-
ciples, the North should be considered first.
But the West, having more defenders than
the North, has also more voters, and more
prospective supporters of the Government
(provided fhey get their line early), and
more prospective enemies of the Government
(if they don’t get their line early). And in
an important respect, the line to the West
is the line of least resistance. It can only
go through S.A. and Westralia. No imag-
inable deviation can drag it through Victoria
and N.S.W. and Queensland; therefore it
isn’t a cause of dissension among those mem-
bers of the caucus who represent the biggest
voting power. The railway to Kalgoorlie
and Perth can be built without the Govern-
ment having to make up its mind, inasmuch
as it is the sort of work which, through its
mere situation, makes up its own mind. But
the really important road to the North is a
different proposition, so the Fisher Ministry
is wary and non-committal. For the present,
it proposes to survey a track from the exist-
ing northern terminus at Pine Creek to
Katherine Waters. By-and-bye, it will pro-
bably build the track to Katherine Waters.
That will amount to a very slight extension
of the present dead-end railway from Port
Darwin. It won’t connect the Territory
with any other State or any other centre of
civilisation. It won’t diminish its isolation
to any extent, or do much to help settlement,
or make the defence of the lonesome province
any easier. It will do nothing to make the
southern fragment of the transcontinental
line —the one from Port Augusta to Oodna-
datta—any less fragmentary or any more
profitable. But the Fisher Government
can extend the Port Darwin-Pine Creek
line to Katherine Waters or even a
little further without committing itself
or risking its job, and therein lies
the great merit of the scheme. Whether it
ultimately decides to build along the short,
straight track to Port Augusta and Adelaide,
or along the long, curly, circuitous one
through Western Queensland and Western
N.S.W. doesn’t matter. The little section
to Katherine Waters is on the road in either
case. This indefinite hanging up of about
the most urgent question in Australian poli-
tics may be a crime against the community,
but it postpones the final row among the
various State Fright elements in the caucus,
and it puts off the evil day when the Fisher
Cabinet will have to make up its mind.
Of late, the old question as to whether
the white man can be a permanent success
in warm regions has been raised again, and
the advocates of nigger labor have been
maintaining at the top of their voices that he
can’t. Judging by such evidence as is avail-
able in Queensland, the white man, under
civilised conditions, can do remarkably well
in the tropics, but it doesn’t follow that he
is at his best in a mixture of intense heat,
chronic drought, great evaporation, and
shifting sand. That is the description of a
great part of the region through which the
Australian transcontinental railway must go
if the State Frights crowd, which clamors
for the deviation through Western Queens-
land and N.S.W., has its way. There is in
the west of N.S.W-., the south-west of
Queensland and the north-east of S.A. a
huge area which is marked on the map as
“sea-level.” There is another such area
round the Gulf of Carpentaria—mostly : n
the Queensland section of the Gulf.
The first-named region represents the
burning depression or hot saucer-bottom
of Australia —the place where the
white man can’t live, if there are
any places in Australia where he can’t live.
It doesn’t, of course, absolutely represent
sea level, though Bourke, in N.S.W. is only
350 ft. above the ocean, and many other parts
of the basin are lower. In this dip lie the
Boulia, Birdsville, Beetoota, Windorah and
Jundah country in Queensland, and between
one-third and a half of N.S.W. The eastern
deviation party wants to add enormously
to the length of the Northern Territory rail-
way in order to drag it through the unprom-
ising hollow where, over a great area, the
extremes of heat, drought and evaporation
meet, so that the N.S.W. and Queensland
representatives may do their duty to their
constituents by hauling a costly public work
inside their State borders. The said hauling
won’t do them any special good, for a rail-
way that takes a devious course through
about the lowest and hottest and driest part
of Australia can do very little good to any-
thing or anybody. But the State borders
are there —so many streaks on the map; and
it is the duty of every good State Brighter
to drag everything he possibly can inside his
streak or perish in the effort.
* * * *
The other idea is to build the shortest and
cheapest possible line across the continent,
and to carry it over the great cool tableland
of Middle Australia. There may still be
room for eloquent liars and others to argue
as to the suitability of the white man for
settlement in the low. hot, damp country in
the Gulf region of North Queensland —the
area that includes Burketown, Normanton,
Croydon, and tfie region almost as far south
as Cloncurry. There may even be room for
argument as to his fitriiess or anybody s fit-
ness to dwell in the hot, saline, dried-up
sea-bottom region about south-western
Queensland. But concerning the. breezy up-
lands of Central Australia there is no doubt
whatsoever. Very few Australians take so
much interest in their own country as
to learn that there is an extensive group of
mountains in the middle of this continent,
just about half-way between Adelaide and
Port Darwin; that there is a region with a
fair rainfall; and that rivers—the Hugh,
the Finke, the Todd, the Waite, the Al-
berga and many more —flow down from it
and get lost, for want of attention and con-
servation works, in that same old dried-up-
sea country which the railway deviation party
wants the line to traverse. Even the baby
township of Alice Springs, at the foot of the
Central Australian mountains, is nearly
2000 ft. above ocean level. From there the
tableland slopes very gently north, with a
steadily increasing rainfall. At Daly Waters,
which is almost abreast of Cooktown, it is
still 700 ft. high, and the high lands con-
tinue north of that till they almost look
down on the coast. Port Darwin is cer-
tainly on a warm coastal belt, but the belt
is not nearly so wide there as the correspond-
ing locality in North Queensland.
For some unexplained reason, the worst,
form of Toryism and the worst form of
State Frights always go together. The viru-
lent Tory declares the Territory unfit for
white settlement, thereby meaning the
coastal scrap of the Territory which is low
and damp, though not apparently unhealthy.
That is the Tory in his Nigger Labor capa-
city. In his State Frights capacity he wants
to add hundreds of miles to the length of
the Northern Territory railway in order to
drag it through Queensland and N.S.W.,
where the big voting power lies—to drag it
round for a vast distance through a
low hot, inland region of a most un-
promising character. He proposes to
do this on the ground that the cool up-
lands, which would be traversed by the
straight line across the middle of Australia,
are uninhabitable. One way and another, he
seems to think that every place is uninhabit-
able, except for niggers and State Frights.
As a matter of fact, there is no need to argue
out the question of the fitness of the white
man for tropical settlement, so far as the
Territory is concerned —not for years to
come. If the railway is pushed- straight
through along the shortest route, from Port
Augusta in S.A. to Port Darwin, there are
only about 1000 miles to be built. Afid most
of this distance is lofty country, where the
white man can live and flourish beyond any
shadow of doubt, unless the weather is too
chilly in spots. (Gilbert White, Bishop
of Carpentaria, says in his “Across Aus-
tralia” that on the tableland near Alice
Springs, in the same latitude as Rockhamp-
ton, he had “six days of as cold travelling
as I ever experienced.”) If this line is
constructed, and the Halfway City or City
of Paradise (it would be Paradise
for those who like a cool climate)
is built in the middle of Australia,
then a great white population can be planted
along the railway and east and west of it,
till the end of the highlands is reached.
After that, if anybody can prove that the
fragment of low coastal country is unfit for
white settlement, the colored labor question
can be argued to a finish. But that problem
need not really turn up for quite a long
while. Even with the most vigorous settle-
ment that can be imagined it will be very
long before the white Australian in the Ter-
ritory need be more than a casual dweller in
the low shore lands, unless he wants to be a
constant dweller there. That is to say, it
will be a very long time before the pressure
of population drives him to the low lands.
The great Federal province has suffered
mostly from fools and State Frights. People
from cold climates, who judge things solely
by latitude as marked on the map, clamor
that the vast elevated country, where the
pious and holy Bishop of Carpentaria nearly
froze, must be hot, because it lies abreast of
Rockhampton and Townsville, and in their
inexperience they find these places hot.
Casual politicians from the south visit Port
Darwin and its immediate surroundings, and
find them unsuited to their constitutions —
debilitated, it may be, by southern winters.
They assume that Port Darwin is the Terri-
tory, just as the British Cockney takes a
day’s run over to Calais, and hands in his
verdict that foreign countries are a failure,
because he tried to buy twopenn’orth
of beer in a strange language and
couldn’t make himself understood. Local-
minded politicians from N.S.W. and
Queensland demand that the railway to
the North shall be built, for local reasons,
through the worst sections of their own
States, and judge the Territory by these sec-
tions, which are neither in it nor of it.
Whether the Federal province is approached
from Port Darwin, or via the Townsville-
Cloncurry railway and through the low-
lying north of Queensland, the explorer is
only looking at the fringe of the country, and
the fringe is no fair test. The middle of
Australia is a sanatorium, not a mangrove
swamp. If the railway is built as nearly as
possible due north and south, the Common-
wealth will discover a new asset. And in
doing so it will learn that it is a mistake to
judge the real interior of this country by
places like Bourke and Wentworth and Jun-
dah and Birdsville and Boulia, that grovel
down in holes. The real interior has a
future, and if the Fisher Government would
only take its courage in both hands and stake
its job on the fact, it might find the gamble
pay.
AUSTRALIAN PLACE NAMES.
Edward S. Sorenson writes: —
Count de Eleurieu, of the Socicte Geographique de
Paris, who has been touring Australia for the pur-
pose of making investigations into the old names
given to various localities by the French explorers,
says that he has found that these, in a great many
cases, have given place to others devoid of any his-
toric value, disregarding the law that the first desig-
nation given to a place should in* all cases be
retained.
Australians heartily agree with the Count, and
would be glad to see that law respected in all cases.
The first names, however, were not French, but
aboriginal. Places on our coast, and in the vicinity
of Sydney, that still honor the intrepid foreigner,
have far more euphonious aboriginal names, which
are not devoid of historic value to Australians.
Thev have a claim above all others; the very
earliest French navigators, as La Perouse, d’ENTßE-
casteaux, Daudin and Perron, had no right to
ignore these, and apply their own appellations in
tneir stead. ,
Personal names applied to places are always ob-
jectionable. The need of wiping out this practice
is brought home to us more forcibly when we hnd
obscure aldermen and politicians disfiguring our cit\
and country maps, often with harsh, uncouth expies-
sions that a conscientious man wouldn’t give to his
dog. However self-important these persons may have
been while honored with a little brief authority,
they had no claim to immortality by inflicting their
commonplace titles on all posterity. Even the people
who help by their votes to clothe them with that
authority know nothing about them in a year or two
afterwards. , , . . ,
We see the need also of restoring the aboriginal
names in numerous instances where places are
labelled with duplications borrowed from other
countries. This duplication is not only confusing,
but it is a form of flattery that is anything but
creditable to us, showing a deplorable lack of origin-
ality. One might even say that it shows a want of,
honor, if not altogether a laxity of morals, for the
business is a steal. Shandygaff names—New This,
and New That, and Native Something-else—are equ-
ally bad.
* * * *
The name of the Westralian capital, Perth, be-
longs to a town in Scotland, and is also duplicated
in Maoriland. Manilla, a town in N.S.W., has
caused no end of confusion, and was particularly
annoying at the time of the Spanish-Amerian war.
The aboriginal name of the place in Munila. Here
we see a corruption of the original through the
carelessness or the auricular dulness of some official,
with very bad results. Numerous instances of the
kind occur in our nomenclature. The name of the
Richmond River port-town, Ballina, which belongs
to a place in Ireland, should be Boollinah, meaning
“place of oysters.’ Louisa (N.S.W.) should be Illo-
e-jah; Breeza, Biridja; Woolloomooloo, Wulla-
mulla. Other Sydney names are: Rose Bay, Panner-
ong; North Shore, Cammeray; Pinchgut, Mattin-
wunye; Middle Head, Cubba Oubba; Sydney Cove,
Warrant-; Goat Island, Memel; Manly Beach, Can-
nae. The only consolation to be derived from Manly
is that the person who so named it didn’t call it
Womanly.
In almost every district throughout the country
one finds Sandy Creek, Deep Creek and Stony Creek.
There must be 10,000 Sandy Creeks in Australia.
When the watercourse is not sandy (it is sometimes
called MacPherson for a change), it is nearly sure
to be Stony or Rocky; if none of these answer, then
it will probably be Dead Man or Dead Dog Creek,
or Murdering Gully.
Emu, Dingo, Breakfast and Blackfellow are other
designations that occur frequently, not to forget
Smith, Jones and Jeremiah. Cooper’s Creek (the
one in Queensland, not the other in Victoria) should
be Koopa Creek.
The aborigine, whom the great white man looks
down upon, showed no such paucity of ideas. He
has an appropriate name for every place, for every
hill and waterway. The majority of them, when
properly pronounced, are sweet-sounding and often
full of sentiment.
In many places the names in a district have some
peculiar affix or prefix, which, to the aboriginal stu-
dent, would be often sufficient to locate them. About
the south-west border of Queensland, for instance,
many names begin with noc, as Nockatunga, Nocun-
dra, Nockaburara, Nocabareena, etc. In the south-
east corner many end with dilla, as Muckadilla,
Moomlilla, Burrandilla. In the big corner west of
the Darling River we have the termination cania and
the prefix yan, as in Wilcannia, Bancania, Yancania,
Yantara, Yandam, Yancowinna, Yanko. And in the
north and north-eastern parts of the State the ter-
minal bri (meaning running water) occurs fre-
quently, as Narrabri, Collarenebri, Boggabri, Cutta-
bri, Benalabri, Nogabri, Therabri. Billy, or billa,
and bony are familiar on the map of the same State,
the first —meaning river or creek —being applied to
streams in the south-east, as Tidbinbilly, Yarrango-
billy ( yarran is a well-known tree), Adjungbilly,
Wagrabilly ( wagra means tomahawk), and Bogabilla,
Bong signifies dead. Thus. Billabong is a blind
channel, or a channel with a dead end. Billa-
bong Creek (a tributary of the Edward River) is
therefore silly, as its meaning is Dead Creek. Creek.
In Victoria the terminal up, meaning watering place,
is common, as in Eurraminnup, Mulallyup, Wilinar-
rup, Quaggamirrup, Kooweerup; while in South Aus-
tralia, along the line from Adelaide to Broken Hill,
we come to a lot of places whose names end with
owie, as Terowie, Euriowie, Caltowie, Canowie, Yar-
cowie, Gumbowie, Condowie, Booboorowie. The
terminals or or ah, oo and ee are common over a
great part of Australia, while any, wang and ong
occur frequently in N.S.W. and Queensland; but the
prefixes are generally territorially distinctive, a mat-
ter overlooked by most writers when coining Aus-
tralian names.
Siiaiispeare asks, what is there in a name? There
is everything in a name. Take, for instance, New-
castle (a steal). The native name of the place is
Moorabinda. Had it been retained the poets would
have made it ring through the world, for the place
is one of the world’s greatest coal centres. But a
stodgy, stolen name like Newcastle, has no more
appeal or inspiration in it than a plate of cold
cabbage.
Take our rivers—Richmond, Clarence, Hunter
Hawkesbury, Hodgkinson, and so on. Who could do
anything with a monstrosity like Hodgkinson? It
could not have been worse if it had been Son-of-u-
Hog. Had it been so called it would have been
in keeping with some pet English names, such as
Pokestog-on-the-llike. The Clarence is one of the
noblest rivers that the traveller could wish to see.
It is dotted with 99 islands, on some of which are
towns and farms and mountains ; on others beautiful
gardens and recreation grounds. How many outside
the district know of its beauties? How few would
be ignorant of that charming river if it had a sweet-
sounding name?
There is no doubt that a name counts for a lot
with the people, and has a considerable influence on
settlement. A name that thev can take to their
hearts and love is a tie that binds and holds. Does
not the man whose childhood was passed in laughing
Riverina, or in snow-capped Monaro, look back to
that land with more satisfaction than the one who
grew up in New England looks back to his? (I am
referring to the New England in New South Wales,
not the New England in America —the country which
contains New Jersey, and New York, and New Bruns-
wick and New London and New Found Land, and
New Mexico, and numerous like horrors.) Does the
latter not feel that he has been robbed of somethin"
that is most dear to everyone who loves the place of
his birth? The great north-western corner has no
name at all, except Trans-Darling, which is lovely.
Con over the soft, lingering, mellifluous names
that are far-famed in Irish song.. Are these names
not loved for themselves ; do they not appeal to the
sentiments and the emotions, apart from all that
may be associated with them, of the sons and
daughters of Ireland ? The nearest approach to them
•in euphonv are our Australian aboriginal namrs.
And it must not be forgotten that there are a great
manv beautiful things in aboriginal legendary lore,
mueh of which has more or less bearing on native
place names. Every country should havp its distinct
nomenclature, and these are ours. We want no
hybrid titles on our maps, and it is certain that
future Australians will not tolerate such appellations
as New South Wales. South Australia fwhich is two-
thirds north and central), Western Australia and the
like. Australia will one day be divided into many
federated States, and it is to be honed that the
naming of them will be left to those of bush experi-
ence. who have some national pride in them, and
some artistic taste.
PLAIN ENOUGH.
What is a Place?
A little while ago one Morgan, of Mel-
bourne, was found running a two-up school
at Galatea Point, and was charged with
using a certain place for gambling purposes
< contrary to the provisions of the law. And
then the Victorian Courts and the Federal
High Court started to foam, just as they
have foamed for ever so many years, over
the definition of a place, and some lawyers
said that Galatea Point was a place, and


some said it wasn’t, and all the ancient con-
fusion and venerable Costs resumed. In
view of this trouble, The Bulletin draws
attention to the fact that six years ago
(31/5/’OG) it published a definition of a
place, and if its advice had been taken, and
its definition fixed by law, the present worry,
and many dozen similar worries, would have
been averted. The following was this paper’s
statement of the case: —
(1) A place is just a place, a situation, a posi-
tion, in fact, any old spot.
(2) A place is anywhere and everywhere in the
universe. When a man is standing, sitting, lying, roll-
ing, walking, or circumnavigating on a place, it is a
place. It is also a place if a man isn’t standing, sit-
ting, lying or rolling on or around it. It is just as
good a place if a cow is standing on it, or an ele-
phant. The South Pole will be a place when it is
found. It will also be a place if it isn’t found. It
is a place now —so is every other place. If there is
no South Pole, it is a place just the same.
(3) An imaginary spot is a place just as much as
if it wasn’t imaginary. If anyone is accused
of making bets in Atlantis, Utopia, or Barataria, the
Court will go right on and hear the evidence, whether
those places exist or not, and will therefore accumu-
late Costs. The Law believes in Costs. The locality
in which Bishop Pontoppidan’s legendary animals
existed was a place.
(4) If a man is up in a balloon the balloon is a
place, and is in a place. This clause applies equally
to a man who has fallen off a ship and not been re-
covered. Even if lie fell off Mark Antony’s galley
at Actium, and hasn’t been heard of since, he is in a
place, and can be prosecuted for making bets there,
provided satisfactory evidence is tendered with the
aid of a duly paid solicitor.
(5) Valhalla is a place. So are Nifleheim and Ge-
henna. For the purposes of this Act anybody who is
dead for 20 years shall be regarded as a place. He
is dust, and dust helps to make the earth, and the
earth is a place.
(6) It is not necessary for a spot or locality to
have any known name or address or latitude or
longitude, or to be approachable by any known or
definite road or other means, or to be located on any
map, or to be defined in any way. It is a place all
the same.
(7) A cockroach under ordinary conditions is a
cockroach. When it is stood upon it becomes a
place. A horse is a place when a man or any other
animal is riding it. So is any other animal. If a
man makes a bet while impaled on the horn of a cow
the cow is a place. When Captain William Jackson
Barry rode on the whale it was a place. So was the
turtle when Louis iie Rougemont was on it, or
wasn’t on it. It doesn’t matter how many animals
are piled on top of one another, each of them con-
stitutes a place for the purposes of this Act. So does
any vehicle, building, structure, object, or any solid,
liquid, fluid, gas, phantasm, chimera, rumor, delu-
sion, or sundry. This clause includes quicksands,
landslides, avalanches, earthquakes and volcanic erup-
tions. . , _.
(8) A place may be of any size or shape. It may
be tipped up at any known or unknown angle. It
may be raw pr fried or otherwise prepared. It may
be living or dead, or partly one or partly another.
Also, it may be wet, dry, or a little of both, with a
leaning either way. (Dust storms come under this
clause, man gets on t j ie shoulders of another
for the purpose of making a bet, the man underneath
shall be deemed a place, and shall be fined not less
than £lO for being one. The man on top shall be
fined double for trying to bring the Law into con-
tempt.
(10) Any solicitor or barrister who claims that
his client wasn’t in a place when the bet was made
shall be suspended for 12 months. Also, if the Judge
or magistrate entertains the claim thus advanced, he
shall lose three months’ salary. If the person on the
Bench is only a J.P. and gets no salary, he shall
cease to be a J.P., and shall be exiled to some
locality at least 100 miles distant, where he shall be
compelled to go on the land.
(11) The term “man” in this Act shall include
any woman or child who bets, also any automaton,
machine, performing dog, calculating pig, or any
other object that gambles, or looks as if it might
gamble.
(12) If, by the process of scientific discovery, and
the invention of flying men or airships, or the exten-
sion of wireless telegraphy, or by thought transfer-
ence or other phenomena, any method is devised
whereby a bet may be made, despite the definitions con-
tained in this Act, without both the parties being in
a place, they shall both be held to be in a place just
the same.
The Bulletin again recommends that these
views be embodied in an Act, and then three-
card men will be put to rout and two-uppers
scattered to the four winds.
An Eminently Successful Way of
Keeping Down Population.
While inquiring recently into the death of
a boarded-out infant, which had allegedly
been transferred when in a dying condition
from the East Melbourne Foundling Home,
Victorian Coroner Cole was handed some
figures that ought to have been made
public long ago. An inquiring constable
fished them out and informed the coroner
that they “staggered him.” They showed
that, between April 15, ’ll, and JMay,
5, ’l2, there had passed through this “home”
—run by a committee of ladies and main-
tained by subscriptions and a Government
grant of £IOO0 —252 children, of whom 132
had been discharged, 53 had died, 20 had left
and been readmitted, and 47 remained in the
institution. Of the 132 discharged, 97 were
still alive three months later, 21 could not
be found, and 1h had died. That is to say,
of 252 infants handled during the months
67, and probably more, had died! No won-
der the coroner, like the constable, professed
himself “staggered.” Next day the Boss
Benevolent Lady of the “Home” rushed
into print to say that their records were no
worse than those of similar institutions in
America (!), that the class and constitu-
tions of the children they received neces-
sarily entailed a high mortality rate, and
that the home existed for the purpose of
saving child life and rescuing the mothers
from further degradation. Also, Treasurer
Watt, who provides the £IOOO referred to,
said that he didn’t think the institution did
too badly considering everything, or words-
to that effect. John Weary, whose depart-
ment concerns itself with the inspection of
such institutions, had not yet made himself
sufficiently awake to say anything. As to
the president’s excuses or allegations, it is
more pertinent to compare the East Mel-
bourne Foundling Hospital, not with similar
institutions in America, but with one not 20
miles away—at Broadmeadows (Vic.). This
is managed by the Sisters of St. Joseph, and
receives infants of all denominations and
from all sources. The latest Victorian Year
Book gives these figures for. ’O9-’lO concern-
ing the two places
East Broad-
Melbourne. meadows.

Probably one reason for the high East Mel-
bourne mortality is the fact that that home
is a close-packed collection of none-too-
modern buildings on a small allotment, near
a congested suburb. Those buildings must
have a large and venerable collection of
germs. They get little air, and what they
do get is not the fresh country air on which
the infants thrive out in the wind-swept
paddocks at Broadmeadows. This conclu-
sion is confirmed by the fact that of the 132
infants sent to boarding-out homes, 14 or
more died. The mother of the deceased
child told the coroner that when it entered
the institution it weighed 16J1b.; when she
took it away and gave it to a nurse at Oak-
leigh it only weighed llilb. Of course, a
Chief Secretary who meant to honestly and
energetically carry out the work he is paid
to do would at once order a searching in-
quiry as to the management of this pre-
cious “home,” and would try and discover
whose fault it is that the undertaker is re-
quired so often; and if he couldn’t find out
lie would close the place as a menace to the
community, and have it fumigated and dis-
infected ; or, better still, razed to the ground
—but the man in charge isn’t that kind of a
Chief Sec. He is a weary old battler —at
least he would be if he battled, but he
doesn’t. t
A Full Explanation.
“Petronius Earbiter” tells how he was:
informed: —
Lively woman carries her quaint “contrariness”’
right into politics, as well as into dress and deport-
ment. For instance, N.S.W. Liberals are handi-
capped with a Women’s League, which lately
“passed,” in more or less solemn conference, the fol-
lowing resolutions: —
(1) To be very loyal to the Empire!
(■/) To cultivate the spirit of true Liberalism! !
(3) To raise the standard of public life ! ! !
(4) To raise the position of women in ail spheres
of hfe! ! ! ! .
(5) To support as candidates for Parliament onia
Liberal and patriotic citizens of high principles and
ability / ! ! ! !
(6) To" support all movements calculated to mini-
mise Social Evils!!!!!!
I absorbed this virtuous and edifying programme
for about three-quarters of an hour, and then I
humbly took it—with all my unresolved doubts—to
a lady who is the vice-something of our suburb s
branch of the said Wimmin’s League. She said: —
“I am so glad you came to me in your dilemma!
All you men have to come to us in the long run, don t
you? Yes! Now, of course, No. I—Loyalty1—Loyalty to the
Empire—Yes” (brightly). “Well, it isn’t necessary
to explain that, is it? No l I’m sure you re veiy
loyal, because you come from Scotland ! All Scots-

instance, who is, of course, half Scotch by his name.
Yes!—.you see?” ( Laughingly .) “Do you know, I
should love to see him wearing the kilts —the plaidecl
tartan of the Bruces, you.know”— —
“How about the tartan of Clan Smith? I inter-
rupted. >
“Oh, yes! And that, too, of course! Well, the
next thing is—oh, yes ! —the spirit of true, earnest
Liberalism. Of course, I couldn’t say exactly what
that—er—ah—implies, but it means fun —( wildly )
—“fundamentally sound Liberal principles, you
know!”.. ..(I did know; she was quoting from the
Sydney Telegraph!) “Then, No. 3—let me see
... .yes !....‘raise the standard of public life
Yes! That, of course, is obvious. It is most neces-
sary. These vulgar Labor persons —and oh! their
women! My dear Mr. Earbiter, you ought to see
them 1 Plaint Oh, positively hideous! And the
airs they give themselves because their party is in
power iust now! And” (with a shriek of laughter)
“the way they dress! They are positive frights I
Dear Lady Gummidge, who saw some of the creatures
at Government House last week, said that they were
dowdy —Fancy! at Government House, of all places!
No really nice person could put up with them! How
his Excellency could ever — But it can t last, Mr.
Earbiter” ( tragically ), “it can never last. Society
couldn’t stand it!
“Yes No. 4 resolution was enthusiastically
adopted. So much depends on it, you know! Really,
as I often say to Mrs. Hauton, ‘We must raise the
sex dear Mrs. llauton ——raise them at once, uplift
them, and improve them —er —generally ! They have
been too long under man and the domination of male

have known her go in her car to the poorest houses
and sit with a sick person for an hour, trying to win

oall it beautiful!. ...
“What is the next? Oh, yes! We cannot have
candidates, can we? who are not pure, good, stainless
men! The League is very, very particular in that
respect! All Liberals should be chosen for Parlia-
ment on the highest moral basis, and- —er —yes, only
patriotic, moral men should be elected. The League
considers that morality is indispensable in a politi-
cian, and —er —that it ranks before ability and mere
grasp of affairs. Only one thing should be allowed
to come before morality, and that is patriotism
loyalty, you know, and attachment to the Throne, and
—and so on! What is the last —oh, yes! social
evils. Yes” ( gravely) “the Conference deplored the
existence of so many dreadful evils among the people.
The spirit of pleasure—theatres —and those awful
picture-shows, Mr. Earbiter. And this wicked cry
for the Totalisator and fair betting, and all the de-
plorable Sunday trading we see around us to-day!
How can we ever be a Nation if we don t go regu-
larly to church and to Sunday-school, and stop buy-
ing cabbages on Sundays? Look at Japan, Mr. Ear-
biter! Do they allow such things? N0!.... We
women must put a stop to it at once, the making
of Australia is in our hands, and when only mce
people and good, religious people and respectable,
well-dressed people go to Parliament and to Govern-
ment House; and when only proper, clean-living men
are elected to the Liberal party, and the vulgar
Labor persons are sent back to the streets, or to pri-

always like to talk about our League, and our ob-

I left in a dream. _
The Only Way.
Last week Mayor Clarke, of Sydney, de-
livered a violent protest against the state
of affairs at Milson’s Point and Circular
Quay during certain hours of the day. An
extract: —
The travelling public are cooped up like sheep in
a pen. They are so huddled together that there is
not even elbow room for them. When the gates are
■opened they are simply pushed through by the force
of the crowd behind. There Is a great opportunity
for pickpockets, and I say that this state of affairs
■ought not to be allowed to continue any longer.
Having made bitter reference to the over-
crowded condition of the ferry-boats, he pro-
ceeded to suggest a remedy. It was, bneffy,
that a fffrther lot of ferry-boats should be let
loose on Sydney’s already-congested harbor.
The present position of affairs is hideously
inconvenient. Inoffensive citizens are liable
to get pneumonia while standing round wait-
ing for a chance to crowd on to one ot the
steamers, and if they get on they do the
journey under utterly uncivilised condmons.
But with more boats the constant dread of
■death would be super-imposed upon their
other discomforts. The only relief, as vari-
ous folks have long been pointing out, is a
bridge or a tunnel, or both, or several of
each. Even 40 years ago the need was ap-
parent in the mild fashion according to
which an immature Skye terrier is visible;
to-day it is the biggest thing in sight, a re-
quirement so vast in i.s urgency that it
■dominates the entire Sydney landscape.
Meanwhile, both the bridge and the tunnel
are as far off completion as ever. The
factor which blocks them, and always has,
and apparently always will, is, of course,
■our demented system of Party Government.
Most of the northern suburbs are solidly
Tory. A Tory Government won’t supply
them with a bridge in order to buy their
votes, because it has got their votes already,
and a Labor Government doesn’t worry about
them because it reckons that it couldn’t
buy their votes even with 10 bridges. There
are no suffrages to be gained, but there are
a whole heap to be lost. C. G. Wade, rep-
resentative though he is of a constituency
which is fairly languishing for rational
means of communication with the city, did
not hesitate, the other day, while orating,
away in the Back of Beyond, to denounce the
McGowen Government for its stated inten-
tion of getting busy with this grey-whisk-
ered old proposition. Wade described the
alleged intention, in effect, as a gross wrong
to the country constituencies, which would
Tiave to pay their share towards the cost of
the venture without getting any of the
benefits. The argument went down well
with the bucolics, _ as indeed it always does
whoever exploits it. Which is why every
•Government, under whatever name it mas-
querades, has sbudderinalv side-stepped the
big work from the beginning of time. Let
it be granted (1) that no State Government,
however earnestly it blithers on. the subject,
will have the nerve to start this costly but
vitally necessary job; and (2) that Private
Enterprise will not be given tlie chance to do
it. The question arises : Who is to take it
on? The answer is the City Council, the
body presided over by the painfully futile
old gentleman whose idea of grappling with
one of the most tragic transport muddles on
record appears above. Under a Greater
Sydney, this work would probably have been
started years ago. It would pay the city
well, if only by making easier the housing
problem, and opening up a vast area of rate-
able property along the shores of Middle
Harbor and elsewhere. In any case, from
the Sydney standpoint, the thing is a neces-
sity, like up-to-date fire-fighting appliances
and an improved water supply, and so Syd-
ney might as well resign itself to beginning
the venture, without further qualms. A con-
dition precedent is, of course, the institution
of a Greater Sydney, and for that ideal
every citizen who is inconvenienced by the
existing state of affairs would do well to
battle hard and tirelessly.
The Mono-Kail.
It is reported that Louis Brennan is only
waiting till all his patents are issued to offer
his mono-rail contrivance to the British Gov-
ernment and to the human race at large, lie
is working a full-sized line at Gilling-
ham (England), where for years he has
labored at perfecting his invention, lie is
now satisfied with if, and reckons that it
has really arrived. The report goes that the
mono-car has shown that it can travel 130
miles an hour on a single rail, bedded on
sleepers 3ft. Gin. long, placed 2ft. apart. It
can turn round curves that no ordinary loco-
motive will navigate. It can face grades
that the old loco, finds impossible. It is less
likely to capsize in a high wind than the
ordinary style of vehicle, for the gyroscope
resists any attempt to overturn it. It keeps
its perpendicular just as wtffl standing as
running. On the evidence of its trials this
concern could jog easily from Port Augusta
in the South of Australia, to Port Darwin in
the North, in 20 hours, and it might do bet-
ter if it put on a spurt. It seems a pity
that Australia’s voluble and hungry High
Commissioner can’t take his lunch with him
one day, and go out to make a thorough in-
spection of the new wonder. The Fisher
Government, if it could be a little less ab-
sorbed than usual, might set to work in haste
to see if the Australian rights are worth
buying, as The Bulletin urged it to do
more than a year ago. It certainly should
have got on the traclt of Brennan’s inven-
tion, and arranged for a series of trials in
Australia, before it committed itself to build-
ing a transcontinental railway on an old,
slow, costly principle, which is probably just
about ready for the scrap heap. The world
is suspected of being on the verge of a
revolution in land transit. It is quite pos-
sible that a considerable number of the mil-
lions now being expended on new railways
are just as much lost as if they were thrown
into the deep, deep sea, and (hat the old
“permanent and reproductive” lines have
suddenly depreciated about one-half. And it
is on the cards that, while the Government
lingers, good old Private Enterprise may buy
up the Australian rights of what may easily
prove an essenlial device, and bleed this
country remorselessly for the use of it. Of
course there is nothing in the Labor pro-
gramme about keening an eye on new me-
chanical devices. But it isn’t necessary to
keep always inside the four corners of the
nrogramme. In this case the Government’s
lack of interest may cost Australia such a
heap of money that a departure from the or-
dinary rut and routine would be excusable.
If the suspicions of many engineers are jus-
tified, the Brennan mono-rail is 1000 times
more important to Australia than evert the
Brisbane strike.
Alfred Deakin’s waggon is hitched, these
days, not to a star, but to the mudhole of
Joseph Cook, and for that reason his at-
tack on the Fisher Government last week
was unconvincing. He had to leave out the
most effective part of his indictment. He
might have preached High Protection. He
might have pointed out to the Hughes sec-
tion of the Labor party that it is really the
Cheap Labor party ; that it is the agent and
advocate of the Tory importer; that *t en-
courages the use in Australia of sweated
labor goods and “scab” labor goods and
colored labor goods; and that it has man-
aged to make this country, in proportion to
its population, the greatest employer of low-
priced misery on earth. He could have told
the Real Labor men that when they Fused
with the Freetraders of the Cheap Labor
crowd they had sold their souls for office.
He could have based his whole indictment
on the tariff, and challenged the Real Lab-
crites to declare that they approved of the
Cheap Laborites by voting against liis
motion. And a lot of the Real Laborites
would have hesitated to do it even at the
bidding of Fisher and Hughes. But he
couldn’t strike this note without throwing
the disastrous Cook overboard, so he let
the chance pass. Fisher and Deakin are
in much the same situation. Each is at the
head of a Fusion of Protectionists and Cheap
Laborites, and the Cheap Labor minority is
allowed to prevail, partly, it may be, be-
cause its policy is a policy of doing nothing,
and it is much easier to do nothing than
to do anything.
The very Fattest rib of the Anti-Socialistic
quadruped met its friend Private Enterprise
a few weeks ago in S. Aus., and promptly
howled for help. Private Enterprise in this
case consisted of the Port Adelaide wharf-
owners, who had just announced an increase
in rates “amounting to 50 per cent, on bales’
of wool and sheepskins.” Now, Private
Enterprise wouldn’t, of course, demand this
increase unless it was just and proper ; yet
the fuss made about it by the Fat Anti-Sosh
people who are interested in wool and sheep-
skins was deafening. They met and “pro-’
tested,” and thanked Providence that, any-
how, there were a few Government wharves
at which this daylight robbery surely
wouldn’t be practised. E. 11. Bakewell,
the chief spokesman for the Pastoralists’
Association —the concern which is quite sure
that State Socialism is the Devil—wrung
his hands over the infamy of the private
wharf-owners: —
These extra imposts would make it hard for the
primary producing interests of the State, more especi-
ally as' the great proportion of wool shipped to Ade-
laide had already to pay three wharfages. The
wharves were essentially a public convenience, and
should be acquired by tire Government at the earliest
possible date. The wharfage rates should be only
The Law and the Profits.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Yesterday the ease of Rex v. The Colonial Sugar
Refining Company was continued at the Water Police
Court.... .
The hearing of the information against Henry
Normand MacLaurin was gone on with....
The hearing was adjourned till next day at
11.15 a.m. — Lines kept in type by Sydney papers dur-
ing the last six weeks.
I turned into the familiar Court —into the
Court I went, but I recognised it not!
Aforetime, the Court had been the trysting-
place of misguided strangers, muddled
citizens and magnificent constables.
(Were they not magnificent, earner ados?)
There, Justice and Retribution held their
sway—there, Vice was more or less van-
quished, and Virtue let off with a
caution.
Aforetime, Minos, P.M., sat there in awful
state, and judged many who had been
haled before him ofttimes in an awful
There, Sergeant Antinous drew with
bright, compelling eye the unwilling
glance of froward men.
Yea, and erring women he regarded with the
sorrowful look of an Ancient Horse-
Marine to whom all probable tales have
been told !
There, the Scribes and Pharisees, the Ex-
pounders of the Law, the Publicans and
the Sinners gathered daily—
And when they were entered in, Sergeant
Antinous gazed steadfastly at the ceil-
ing and cried aloud —-
“Take off your hats, for this is holy ground!”
And, again, he cried with a loud voice:
# #
And, lo! the Court was opened by Minos,
P.M. —and generally closed by 1 p.m.
Disturbers of traffic, thieves, forgers, harlots,
bad lots, smugglers, abandoned wine-
bibbers, and even-more-abandoned bib-
whiners, received their admonition, or
correction, or compensation.
The assessments of iniquity and of obliquity
were declared and exacted; what Justice
demanded was straightway paid, to the
uttermost sovereign, or to the last syl-
lable of the recorded time!
He who waxed familiar, or disputatious,
before Minos, P.M., was abased and
put down in his seat; and he whose uni-
form had been torn was exalted.
She that bore false witness against her
neighbor was brought to shame; and
such as had stood on the bridge at mid-
night found that reproof cometh in the
morning.
In all the Court were found none that could
withstand the sweet influences of the
wily “D’s,” or loosen the bands of Con-
stable O’Ryan !
And, anon, there was an end of the matter,
and the Court rose.
Then the Scribes and Pharisees, and the Ex-
pounders of the Law said: “Let us
adjourn!” And swift was the adjourn-
ment thereof.
And they went forth ; and with them divers
of the legionaries; and, relating sad
stories, one to the other, they mingled
their beers together.
A time and a time ago, thus were all things
done in the Court of the Water of the
City of the Beautiful Harbor,
Rut now doth it suffer a melancholy change
for the waters of strife have covered it.
Its pleasant voices are no more heard, and!
the story of the innocent pawnbroker is
departed !
Justice and Retribution hibernate without,
and Law reigneth triumphant!
Very ancient is Law, camerados; it abidetb.
even from B.c. to K.C.!
The Law stretches forth its immemorial.
hand, and the Court is not.
Minos, P.M., thunders no more; in his seat
he broods upon the flight of Time and
the Costs thereof!
Sergeant Antinous is become a mournful
bystander; his eyes are closed in con-
templation—or sleep.
The Welkin resounds with the learned voice
of Argument, breaking like the waves
of the sea upon the shores of Precedent.
(Alas! camerados, the Welkin is nearly
worn out! It will cost much money to’
replace it. Let the rulers see to it!)
They have leased the Court to the Law and
the Profits; and where the Sugar is
there the Legals are gathered together!
Oye Legals! How glorious is the uncer-
tainty of the Law! How glorious the
certainty of Costs!
Tacitus —our learned friend Tacitus —
hnth it that the Law aims at perfection ;
but we say that the Law is a poor
marksman, considering its limitless
practice!
Is it right that they should lease our pleas-
ant Court for ninety-nine years as a.
legal shooting ground?
Camerados, let us demand our Court again
—let us insist that the Legals cease to
kid, and that the Crown lie down with,
the Lamb !
And I salute you !
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

Then a committee was appointed to ask the
Government to provide the wool-gi'owers
with more of the thing that ruins the coun-
try and bursts up the happy home; and J. J.
Duncan, who leads the Anti-Ruin party in
the Fatmen’s House, was chosen as a mem-
ber of the committee that proposed to ask
for some of the ruin for themselves. Inci-
dentally, Duncan and the other portions of
the Fat rib didn't decide to ask the Govern-
ment for cheap bricks for warehouses, fac-
tories and workmen’s cottages. On the
contrary, though it is obviously the proper
thing for the Government to own the
wharves so that the Fat squatter may escape
the fleecing of Private Enterprise, it was just
as obviously a monstrous thing for Verran
to propose Government brickworks and tim-
ber-yards so that the Lean citizen might
escape the same fleecing. Wherein anyone
who likes can see the beautiful consistency
and sincerity and unselfishness of the Anti-
Sosh quadruped, whose other name is the
Liberal” party.
The Sydney Women’s Liberal League Con-
ference, having previously adopted a resolu-
tion for “The preservation of individual lib-
erty and enterprise,” wound up with this: —
The Conference records its deep conviction of the
essentially immoral and unjust principles underlying
proposals for legislative enactments on the lines of
the Contagious Diseases Acts, which were abolished
m England in the year 1885, and pledges itself to
use every possible means to prevent the enactment
of such legislation in the State of N.S.W., and also
to assist the other States of the Commonwealth to
resist any similar attempt within their borders.
The anxiety of the Women’s Liberal League
to preserve to Flossie the glorious privi-
lege of “individual liberty and private enter-
prise ” is touching. Flossie will probably
be grateful, and regard the Liberal ladies as
brother women, so to speak. Still, there are
other aspects of the subject. It may be in-
tolerable from the individual-liberty - stand-
point to wipe out Flossie’s syphilis-propa-
gating enterprise, but what about the indivi-
dual liberty of some other people? It isn’t
only, the immoral person who contracts
syphilis, although the Liberal ladies in their
ignorance may think it is. Very often the
Perfectly innocent person succumbs to it.
What about his or her individual liberty?
Doesn’t that count? And what about the
individual liberty of the absolutely unoffend-
ing babe, born maimed or idiotic, of parents
who are the guilty or innocent sufferers from
t* l ® hellish disease? Doesn’t that count,
either? Seemingly not. Seemingly nothing
counts but the criminal prudery of the
Womens Liberal League.
From an Adelaide critic:—
•<T7- A i? red Sinclair - widely known 20 years ago in a
Wadham-and-Sinclair partnership that covered acres
of canvas, chiefly in Maoriland, is back in Australia,
and the S. Aus. Society of Arts has lent him its
rooms for a one-man show. His work is neat, beau-
tifully finished and photographic in fidelity. In his
light and shade on hillsides he sometimes recalls
Peter Graham. What he lacks most are vigor and
variety. His subjects—all landscape—are drawn
mostly from Adelaide, or the hill townships of Mount
Barker and Strathalbyn; but there are one or two
from Healesville, and one or two more from North
Wales, with a fine canvas from Somerset, England.
Sinclair holds a “Royal British Colonial” diploma,
in which George V. addresses him familiarly as
“trusty and well-beloved,” and suggests that he
should keep on at his good work with the brush. He
intends to, and Sydney and Melbourne should have a
welcome for him soon.
Sydney Telegraph put it this way on the
morning of June 21: —
LABOR.
Cargo Broaching.
VALUABLE CONSIGNMENT PILLAGED.
Then followed a report of the pillaging of a
case of Parisian blouses aboard a French
mail steamer on the voyage from Marseilles
to Sydney. Probably the headline was an acci-
dent, but no apology has followed it. Yet.
if by any accident there appeared a set of
headings to this effect: —
LIBERAL PARTY.
At The Quarter Sessions.
HEAVY PENALTIES FOR WIFE BEATTNG.
the. apologies next morning would be as
striking as the paper’s quaint information
on common matters of history.
On the strength of a cable blunder, ,T.
Bull’s Imperialistic Daily Express lately
gave a heavy-headlined column to the “trag-
edy of Australia,” the horror lying in the
alleged fact that “the final census count
shows that in .10 years the population has
increased by 118,000.” The Express man-
aged, somehow, to get hold of figures declar-
ing that, in the same 10 years, 570,500
people had emigrated to the country. Where-
fore it assumed that, besides grilling all the
infants who had arrived in the 10 years, the
Labor party, or some other infamous tiger,
had consumed 457,500 adults. This last
act of the tragedy left the Express flooding
London with tears over “the steadily dwin-
dling population,” and the stupid old slan-
derer never once seemed to suspect the truth
—that the very respectable increase of
113,000 was for the one year 1911.
