“Kismet” made a first and highly success-
ful appearance in Sydney at the Royal on
Saturday night, with Oscar Asche as Hajj,
the picturesque beggar of Bagdad, and Miss
Lily Brayton as his daughter. A strong
company and a very fine collection of scen-
ery lent their support. At various times dur-
ing the season “Antony and Cleopatra,”
Midsummer Night’s Dream,” “The Merry
Wives ” and “The Taming of the Shrew”
will transpire.
*
The crowded state of the ferry-steamers is
as nothing to the congestion that has pre-
vailed during the past week at Her Maj-
esty’s, Sydney, where the craft of “Sinbad
the Sailor” rides the whirlwind of popular
approval, and defies “the bird” six nights a
week, to say nothing of matinges. This is
a vessel where youth and beauty are not
only at the prow and helm, but all over the
place, from the captain’s bridge to the water-
tight compartments. Hence its success.

The stage is taking its place as the chief
agent of reform. The subject of solitary con-
finement in prison was attacked by Gals-
worthy in his grim drama, “Justice.” Now
“Niglitbirds,” at the Criterion, points the
way to the transformation of our out-of-
“KISMET” AT SYDNEY ROYAL.
H a jj (Oscar Asche) tells his daughter (Lily Bray ton) to prepare to wed a stranger at sundown.
date methods of treatment of the criminal.
The New Age Prison shows what ameliora-
tive results can be got by simple kindliness
and comfort. For instance, nobody can call
the convict costume at Darlinghurst either
attractive or aesthetic; but the New' Age
Prison officials have designed costumes of
blue and scarlet, relieved by bold designs of
the broad arrow conventionally treated, that
must have their effect upon the convict’s
artistic sense. The happy device of differ-
entiating the cells by pictures of flowers
rather than numbers must also appeal to those
criminals in for making false income-tax
returns, who do not desire to be reminded
of figures. And the substitution for the
exercise of the prison yard of “the bunny-
hug” and “the turkey trot” is cordially re-
commended to the authorities at Darling-
burst. As for the characters who are pur-
suing the Nightbirds of Happiness, Florence
Young’s voice and vivacity, Sybil Arundale’s
ankles and gaiety, Jack Cannot’s escapades
and aplomb, W. S. Percy’s black eye and
humor, W. Talleur Andrews’s singing and
Hungarianism. Reginald Roberts’s voice and
acting, and Victor Prince’s rather obvious
humor and rather obvious nose prevent any
of the Birds from suffering a dull moment.

George Marlow requests the pleasure of
Sydney’s company at the “Marriages of
Mayfair,” to be celebrated at the Adelphi
Theatre on Saturday night, and thence every
evening until Mr. Mayfair deserts Mrs.
M. or gets a divorce or six months for big-
amy. No refreshments. R.S.V.P.
In the vaudeville business, there never
seems to be a shortage of people who can
throw lighted lamps, needles, cannon balls
and china plates into the air and catch them
again in their proper order as they come
down. Also, so far as the writer knows,
there has never been a dearth of people who
balance other people on their heads, or sup-
port them with teeth or ears while hanging
from a bar or strap. One sighs for the night
when the lighted lamps, etc., will stay up in
the air, and the sustaining bar or strap will
be invisible. These introductory remarks
are a sort of apology tendered bv the Reli-
gious Editor for his lack of excitement over
the arrival, at Sydney Tivoli, of Charles
Hera, juggler and equilibrist, and the Hans
Paxton Troupe of acrobats. Besides Bluett’s
songs and ventriloquist Fred Russell’s pat-
tei\ the second part contains the Four
Amaranths’ tempestuous turn, Blake and
Granby’s absurdities, and Ronald George’s
high-speed music.
£c %
Margaret Cooper, at the Palace, is still
explaining to the population of Sydney, in
clear, brisk fashion, how the cream puli' of
entertainment should be served up. As
usual, the managerial advertisement speaks
very highly of her each morning; but the
majority of those who attend the little show
also become her honorary advertising agents
in office, home and pie shop. For fear he
should be found guilty of painting the lily,
the present writer hurriedly closes his ink-
pot, and retires with a graceful bow.

Millie Doris returned to the National
Amphitheatre last week, and the populace
signified its approval in the usual manner.
The Mcßanns do some new and amazing
juggling. To see them hurl frail straw hats
far into the auditorium, in such a fashion as
to cause them to boomerang back to the
stage, is to realise that there are more possi-
bilities in David Storey’s trade than one had
dreamed of. Florence Wix is a fine and
sprightly lass, with a rotten song ; and Cecile
Eldred and Carr earn every farthing of
their money, whatever it is.

Perhaps the greatest free spread ever seen
in Sydney occurred on Saturday night at the
opening of the Greater J. D. Williams
Crystal Palace in George-street. The £70,000
structure- was packed. The crowd filled the
picture hall to the last seat, crowded the
Winter Garden to the last square inch of
room, and backwashed through ■ the Electric
Studio, the Hall of Athletic Exercises, and
the Pavilion of Automatic Devices. The
whole, system of Yankee ices, drinks, peanut
collations, popcorn and sundries was free. The
Crystal Palace has easily the handsomest
photo-play theatre in Sydney, and the plays are
shown by a greatly improved process. This
theatre is a symphony in dark green, with
pictures .painted on the walls, and framed
heavily in old gold. The screen is also
framed in old gold. The Winter Garden is
a pleasant haunt of high-priced refreshments
and iced drinks. Ladies “with encumbrance”
will be pleased to notice an enclosed space
filled with baby furniture and crowded with
dolls, teddy bears and toys, where the infant
can be deposited in charge of a nurse. A
careful labelling system makes the return of
the right infant fairly certain. The ventila-
tion is excellent. The place was filled with
smoke on Thursday before the opening, and
the atmosphere was completely purified fn
five minutes.

Adelaide has been in a whirl of shows, a
Baden-Powell lecture, embroidered with
Scout rallies and concerts, being added to
the usual list. Also, the. rival showmen,
Wirths, have arrived with their circus, and
dropped it at the Exhibition. Besides the
Polar bear, the Wirths have “The Globe
of Death” with them, though it now calls
itself the “Globe of Life.” Still, it looks
just as dangerous as ever. “Everywoman”
has come into the Royal, displacing “The
Blue Bird.” The latter show includes a
clever young Adelaide girl, Maud Plunkett.
Manager Harold Ashton, who seems to have
taken root in Adelaide, is still in command.
At the Tivoli, manager Marcus celebrates
its 12th year under the Rickards banner.
The Keeleys are quaintly funny in their
lightning-like ball-punches, and Burnetti is
a really good musician behind all his clever
acting. At the King’s are the reckless acro-
bats, the Siddons; also some fine vocalists,
and a lady who can comb her hair with her
foot. To West’s, and the Empire and Won-
dergraph, and the other picture shows, is
now added the Pavilion, which asks to be
called the “Pav.,” and for the sake of brevity
this paper doesn’t mind. It is a charmingly
appointed house in Rundle-street, and is
open all day.

Maeterlinck, at Melbourne Royal, last »atu: rc
The scenic strength and suggestive charm of the
panto-romance are accepted propositions. Herewith
few remarks upon its weakness. The could
appeals to the poetic sense of the P ub^ e at .
see beneath the surface of Be ter Pan, qhow
peal is not so logical, nor is its interest as a show,
so simply human as that of Barry s fairy ta •
tion seems subservient to fact in the awakeric
of Wendy Darling and Peter Fan, whereas Tyltyl and
Mytyl are merely borne to Shadow Land in the arms
of Morpheus. And the pauses in their journey are
frequent and long. The entertainment, which is un-
substantial fare for a winter night, is served in six
courses, with tedious intervals between them, no t
mention curtain breaks in two of the acts. 1
almost as much interval as action in a performance
of Maeterlinck’s fantasia on the Barry theme. it
deserves a great success, of course. Everything good
and sweet in intellectual dramatic work ought to
attract the million. “Peter Pan” should have been
a popular delight, and its sturdier eccentricity should
have smoothed the way to a great run for the present
production. Maybe that the somewhat mild applause
on Saturday night was only a faint indication of the
audience’s respect for a beautiful show.
The symbolism of the plot is often crudely definite
in form, and therefore its inconsistencies are obtru-
sive. “The Blue Bird” means Happiness, the advt.
states, while the play states that Mytyl and Tyltyl
don’t find it. They leave the real Maginnis perched
on a moonbeam in the Garden of Mrs. Night, a melan-
choly lady who seems anxious to keep Happiness flying
around her premises. Yet the children are vaguely'
understood to bring the Fowl of Joy back to their
home (in their dream), and it saves the life of Mrs.
Berlingot’s sick daughter, but, as soon as she has
got it, the incredible creature flies away, because
Happiness refuses to be caged. This is the very
lunacy of poetic imagery. Everybody knows that
Good Luck is a fleeting thing, whilst Bad Luck is a
devil to linger, but the Blue Bird, which brings Hap-
piness where it is not caught, and then flies from
where it never was, knocks spots off the Athanasian
Creed for incomprehensibility. The temporary return
to life of dead people and things in the Land of
Memory is very forcibly expressed. There is much
food for the sentimental in the incident of the two
unborn lovers, whose natural affinity is powerless to
get them both to town at the same time. The wrath
of the Oak against his hereditary enemy Man is
reasonable (although Man has done a lot for the Oak
in the way of planting acorns which might otherwise
have been devoured by swine). But how about the
birds which Man murders, the flowers he plucks and
wires, the fruits he eats, and the fish which he catches
and destroys by the ton, in order to keep up the
market price? Why are they not included in the con-
spiracy which the faithful Dog brings to naught?
As for Bread and Milk, they are absurdly out of
place in the fable. Bread is not a natural product
of earth, anyhow. It is created by bakers for Man’s
sustenance, and adulterated with alum. Cow’s Millc
is the very life of Man, if he is brought up on the
bottle. How can it become antagonistic to him at
the instance of its natural enemy the Cat? Bread
and Milk are introduced in the interests of huipor,
seemingly. Edward Rigby’s speaking of the first-
named part is supremely funny. Loaf gets the hear-
tiest and wholesomest laughs of the evening, the un-
born baby laughs being less wholesome. Miss Mcln-
tosh is impressive in the easy character of Night,
whilst Miss Bavley is only pretty and pleasing as
Light. The difficult Cat is well acted by Norman
Page, and the still more difficult Dog (E. Hendrie)
achieves the victory of the pathetic over the ridicu-
lous, when he is doomed to remain inarticulate in
his devotion to the boot that kicks him. The princi-
pal “boy” (Gertie Cremer) of this pious panto is a
great disappointment to the present commentator.
She lacks the naiveness of her quiet little sister,-Vera
Spaull, and is neither very winsome in personality
nor very earnest in her acting. If she is as interest-
ing as the cleverest of children could possibly be in
such a part, then the play is at fault in requiring" a
real youngster instead Of a mock infant for its central
figure.
The Quinlan co. is now in the third week
of a terribly busy season, at Her Majesty’s
(Melbourne). With the exception of
“Faust” and “Traviata,” the whole reper-
toire of 15 operas has been unrolled. “Mrs.
Butterfly” was to eventuate on Wednesday,
and Gounod’s version of the Man, the Girl
and the Devil comes due to-night (Thurs-
day). Changes in the cast of principals
usually mark the revival of an opera,
as was the case with the Melba
season, and the change usually repre-
sents a discount on the original effect,
but the orchestra is an invariable joy,
and the chorus never disappoints its ad-
mirers. Under this system of labor distribu-
tion the Co. thus far has preserved good
form all round. When “Tristan and Isolde”
comes to be revived next Tuesday, with,Mrs.
iNicholls and Coates again in the name parts,
it will doubtless find those trusty exponents
ot vehement musical drama in robust health,
lnree times last week the performance
bumped up against .a recent recollection, and
in (wo instances its bruises were severe.
Falla Miranda, .as Mimi in “Bohbme” was
hardly more satisfactory than Gregorio, the
Italian tenor who made her welcome to the
attic ot neglected genius, and lured her into
a long conversation. The gent was dramati-
cally uninteresting, and his small-sized voice
had streaks in it. The prima donna sang
only moderately well, whilst her acting
never distracted attention from the unneces-
?T r rl blonde , wig wherein she lived and died.
Melbourne hopes to see its long-lost daugh-
ter to better advantage in a more congenial
part. In this performance W. J. Samuell,
whose baritone is sympathetic to all music,
was a pleasing Marcel, and Miss Courtenay
had voice and vivacity enough and to spare
for the giddy goings on of Musetti.

Have you a little room in which you have wished
it were possible to place a Grand PIANO. We have
enabled you to do so. For a half-century we have
patiently experimented to reduce the Size and price
of Grand Pianos, to adapt them to the limited space
and purse of many music-lovers. In the STECK
“Boudoir” we have at last produced a Grand that
meets musically the requirements of the most exact-
ing tone critic. This dainty, beautiful instrument
charms all who hear and see it. Measuring but
sft. 7in. in length, it renders a Grand Piano of the
highest character available even for rooms of limited
space.
The STECK, more than any other Piano, possesses
a rich mellowness of tone, a sympathetic singing
quality, which makes it not only the ideal instru-
ment for the great artist in concert, but also the
best Piano for the home.

Enter Agnes Nicholls, as Elizabeth.
were the dusky Aida, the jealous Princess
and the gallant captain with the sausage
ringlets. Whereas the lovers voiced their emo-
tions with warmth and did themselves proud
throughout the proceedings, the contralto
(Miss Thornton) went one better in her
own particular scene. For the rest of the
performance the chorus was fine and pictur-
esque. High Priest Hinckley, of the rolling
bass, had the striking appearance of a pious
Egyptian, pierrot. Magrath was a Pharaoh
who commanded some reverence, and Sam-
uell presented the captured King of Ethopia
as somewhat like a nigger minstrel down od
his luck. “Aida” happens again next Sat-
urday, and will be in further demand. Also,
“Carmen” occurs again once only, next week.
Last Saturday’s performance of that hardy
annual \vas a picnic for some of the weaker
members of the co. Rosina Beynon, as the
raging Gipsy, was overweighted; ditto the
N. 3, or No. 4, tenor; whilst Robert Parker,
as the toreador, and Lalla Miranda, as
Micaela, helped to raise the average. On
Monday—the Wagner night—came an excel-
lent “Lohengrin” show. Here the chorus
counted for its full worth, and with Coates
as the knight in shining armor, Agnes Nich-
olls as a large, impressive Elsa, Edna Thorn-
ton as the villainess, and Parker, Hinckley
and Wynn in the men parts, the opera had
the best cast the co. can provide. The per-
formance in general was the finest this writer
has seen.

“Man’s Enemy” was uncorked at the
King’s Theatre on Saturday night, and the
odor of alcohol crept like a fog across the
footlights. All the trouble is caused because
Cyril Mackay meets Miss Eugenie Duggan,
the reigning belle of an ornate two-up school
or something of the sort, and falls hopelessly
in love with her elocution. They unani-
mously commit matrimony, and fly to Monte
Carlo in search of the Blue Bird of Happi-
ness. Here, in a high-class drinkery, fur-
nished in the chaste manner of an icecream
emporium, Cyril sadly pursues the nimble
fantod, while his giddier half is pursued by
an amorous Russian Count in a red sash,
presumably the badge of a Christian En-
deavor Society. The lady chants her _ wil-
lingness to fly with the Count to Siberia or
somewhere, but Cyril shoots him, and would
probably be lynched for his marksmanship
but for the timely intervention of the low
comedian. After Cyril has sounded all the
depths and shoals of alcohol, he is weaned
from the bottle by a pewer gyurl, who
dresses with the elaborate simplicity of a
musical comedy favorite. As a drawer of
corks and a slayer of fantods, Cyril Mackay
provides a vigorous performance—so vigor-
ous, in fact, that in the whirlwind of his in-
temperance on the first night he bumped
against a large statue, which crashed to the
ground like a dropped fan. Miss Duggan,
as the lady who nearly broke the seventh
commandment at Monte Carlo, wears some
new dresses, but her elocution is the same as
hitherto. As the Count who would a-wooing
go, Max Clifton talks like a Hebrew endeay-
oring to dispose of a brummy diamond. Rut-
land Beckett, a polished adventurer, is super-
cilious and well-tailored, and Charles Brown,
as Ikey 'Barnett, strikes this paper as a
sometimes tragic comedian.
** * *
The most notable circumstance about “The
Woman Pays,” which turned up _at the
Princess’s on Saturday night, is the industry
of the callous and incisive villain-in-chief.
Roger gets busy at 8.5 p.m., and by the time
the audience is feeling for its hat, he has
piled up a list of excesses ranging from
murder to common forgery, and including a
scheme of bigamous intent. Apart from the
capers of this more-than-usual miscreant, the
plot is wearisomely conventional. There
are the same old “props,” from the foolish
pa of the lovely heroine to the sodden pa of the
girl who has strayed, and they all dodder
when thev ought to cut it short and merely
do. The flood of talk allotted to the comic
relief” is particularly Amazonian, and
Frank Crossley, the alleged “relief” in
question, makes the most of his unfortunate
opportunities. Vivian Edwards is happy as
the evil Roger Marchant; though still
young, he is already efficient enough as a
villain to teach crime by correspondence on
the United States model. George Cross
plays a noble fisher-lad. Miss Nellie Bramley
is the nice girl who marries George, and
Miss Nellie Fergusson appears as a lady
with a casual infant that needs post-dating
to be respectable. Next Saturday the Plim-
mer-Denniston co. breaks out at the Prin-
cess’s in “A Woman of No Importance,”
and a cast including Geo. Titheradge, Mrs.
Brough, Miss Beatrice Day and Miss Lizette
Parkes will give voice to Wilde’s epigrams,
and look as aristocratic as possible.

Somehow the “living art productions” of
Henriette de Serris (who by any other name
would be of no less personal unimportance
to the audience) are not rushed after at the
Opera House. They are remarkably clever
arrangements of human beings in the guise
of statuary or oil paintings, but a public
that has outlived the novelty of picture
studies from art is apt to wonder what’s the
good of whitening a fellow creature in imi-
tation of a more finely outlined marble
figure. The public is not even judging the
De Serris pictures on their merits. The
most unartlike of the lot —which is “The
Angelus”—gets the only burst of applause
worth mentioning, because its sentiment
counterfeits piety. Max Laube, a human
Blue Bird, has been added to the Rickards
bill; and various ladies—solo, duo or trio —
are appearing as before. Also. Will Fox
repeats his former turn in his previous
gaudy suit of clothes, with hat to match.

Dr. Mirakel, the weird one (Charles Magrath).
Jansen, the elegant young man of Mys-

Town Hall. The demand for magic is never
dead. Sometimes it gets surfeited, and goes
to sleep, but when the hour is favorable and
a satisfactory wizard strikes it with his
wand, the crowd comes along. Jansen is the
right man. The Town Hjj.ll seems the wrong
place for a soft-voiced professor to practise
his black arts in, but its seating accommo-
dation is great, and the effect of the Phantom
Princess from afar off is immense. More-
over, the xylophone player revels in the vast-
ness of the auditorium.

Edgar Allan Poe, in the act of hatching
“The Raven,” that fowl that sat on the
pallid bust of Pallas and made dispirited
remarks, was cleverly shown in a picture
that came under the notice of this paper at
Melbourne Olympia last Saturday. The
film also tried to symbolise some of Poe’s
ideas, but in these weird tableaux the
achievement was smaller than the intention.
An unusual natural-history subject was the
otter hunting its dinner; while an edition
of Pathe’s foreign Gazette showed the last
stand of the motor bandits in Paris. “A
Stenographer Wanted” was the title of a
comedy that was really funny, and yet not
raving mad.
“In the Probate Division.—Emily Soldene.
Realty, £893; net personalty, £BlO. Probate
granted to her - granddaughter, Helen M. E. Simpson,
of Feltham, Middlesex.” Well, she no doubt enjoyed
the tens of thousands she made and spent better than
the few hundreds she made and hung on to.
“Blab”: When viewing the De Serris group of
Living Statues recently at Sydney Tivoli, I was re-
minded of a similar exhibition I saw at Paisley, near
Glasgow, in the ’6o’s. The Paisley statues mostly
represented members of the Grecian and Roman mvtho-
. logy. Two drag-loads of gods and goddesses left
Glasgow for the scene of action. The gods were
medical students, and the goddesses—well, they were
ladies anxious to display their shapely forms not
encumbered with much clothing. On emerging from
the undressing room, Bacchus supplied the sons and
daughters of Olympus with whisky from a hand
basin. After drinking heavily they mounted their
pedestals arranged on, tiers on a turnstile. Some of
them hiccupped. The curtain rose, and after the
first evolution of the turnstile the statues wobbled.
After a few more turns the mythology was leaning
up against itself at an acute angle. About the tenth
evolution all the deities fell with a crash and kicked
and cursed in a struggling mass on the stage floor.
The audience demanded its money back, and the hall
was cleared with police and difficulty. The whole
performance did not last more than a quarter of an
hour.
“0.K.”: The Bulletin, it seems, owes an apology
to Britain for having referred to Quinlan’s opera
ballet as crude dancers from a Gold Climate, or
words to that effect. Whereas the aforesaid limited
ballet (the word got changed to “unlimited” in tele-
graphic transmission) was assumed to be part of the
army that Quinlan landed on these shores, it is
actually an auxiliary force of local girl supplied by
the Combine. Therefore The Bulletin’s suggestion
that “If the frisky nymphs of Venusberg had been
borrowed from Melbourne dancing schools, the first
scene of ‘Tannhauser’ would have been an improve-
ment on previous productions” was like unto the
error of the man who shot at a haystack and killed
a crow, or of the proverbial dawg who barked up the
wrong tree. It was a pardonable and popular error,
all the same. Hundreds of spectators of that
“Tannhauser” ballet must have fallen into it. The
dancing seemed much too stiff and angular to be
Australian, nevertheless it was the only native-born
item in the performance. And the eight blessed
damosels who did the deed were selected from the
Combine’s own school —the eight students in this
case being apparently of the class that is coming on,
but has not yet arrived at panto, and comic-opera
proficiency. It is fairly up to them to shake their
legs like true daughters of the soil, and show the
imported opera co. what poetry of concerted motion
means in these latitudes. As a matter of course,
they are improving with each performance, but the
fact remains that those nymphs of Venusberg put
The Bulletin in a hole. Reading its comment on
his imported ballet Quinlan doubtless cried: “Gee!
but this is funny.” It’s a terrible thing for a hum-
orous paper to be truthfully stigmatised as funny.
Grant Hervey turned his Pegasus out to grass, the
other day, and wrote a play. The name of the work
is “A Sportsman and a Man,” and it deals with the
career of Adam Lindsay Gordon. It was produced at
Mount Gambier (9/6/’l2) amidst scenes of abso-
lutely blithering excitement, and at the finish the
members of the audience, as some small token of
their appreciation, doggedly insisted on standing the
author a champagne supper. Grant did not refuse.
“0.K.” : From a musical observer’s point of view
the most interesting item of the Quinlan opera season
in Melbourne, last week, was the producton of
“Aida.” The Egyptian opera is not built after the
antique Italian plan, which practically invited tumul-
tuous encores for the principals after each of their big
numbers, individual or collective. “Aida” was one of
Verdi’s earlier attempts in descriptive, consecutive
music writing—a compromise between sweetly tuneful
and strictly harmonious opera. Nevertheless, it has
semi-detached airs for solo and duet singers, and
when the loud Italians of a dozen years ago opened
their campaign in this Verdian work they never com-
pleted a big number without pausing for applause—
and getting it. In the last 12 years, seemingly,
Melbourne has learned to refrain from the old encore
interruptions, and the Quinlan performance of
“Aida,” which is beautifully smooth and subdued in
tone as compared with the fierce declamatory Italian
rendering, is allowed to pass without comment from
the audience until the curtain falls. In three of the
four acts, to be sure, there are two scenes, and the
curtain drops between them, which gives the prin-
cipals a show to get glory where glory is due. In
the last act, for instance, the contralto, as Pharaoh s
daughter, has a great scene all to herself, and numer-
ous “calls” result from it, a glory denied to Cis-
neros in the Melba season performance of “Aida,
because the “call” curtain was not provided. Still,
the silence of the audience, all the time the opera is
in view, bears dumb witness to the spread of what
may be called modern musical feeling among the
general public. Isolated indications of a desire to
start a furoTe whilst the curtain was up, or even
when the orchestra was finishing a finale after the
curtain had dropped, were promptly hushed the other
night. The majority were taking their Verdi as
seriously as they take their Wagner.
John Coates, the burly tenor, appears to be the
mainstay of the Quinlan opera co., whereof every
member is a more or less valuable support. Coates
is a Yorkshireinan by persuasion, carrying 17st.,
without a vast preponderance of stummick. A family
man in the 40’s, whose pretty little wife travels
with him. An artistic singer, of course, else he
wouldn’t have preserved his fine voice in full strength
through years of incessant work. And an actor
of such all-round ability that, if his operatic vocal-
ism failed him, he would be wanted for character
parts in a dramatic co. Big John is a Coates ol
many colors in histrionic work, whilst his tenor is
just as lyric or robustious as he likes to make it.
No sort of music has come amiss to him thus far in
the Melbourne season. He makes a rule, it is said,
of talking as little as possible by day when he has
to sing at night, and as he is mostly rehearsing his
next part on other days, he has not much time to
spare for afternoon tea discussion. Coates doubtless
makes for peace and goodwill in a touring grand opera
co. If there were much wrangle and ill-will in the
air he would silently knock the heads of the dissen-
tients together and restore harmony.
“Stargazer”: The Melbourne University students
have just enacted “Miles Gloriosus, a Latin comedy,
written by Plautus some 2000 years ago, and the
most interesting feature of the event was its demon-
stration that the difference between the broad comedy
of b.c. and that of d.b.y. (day before yesterday) is
scarcely discernible. Plautus chased his characters
about precisely as John Smith might, and proved in
a general way that it was just as comic to fall over
a banana skin on the Appian Way 100 8.C., as it is
in Collins-street a.d. 1912.
Rosina Buckman, the Maoriland singer who made
good during the last Melba opera season in Australia,
is likely to get a long-dreamed-of chance in London
shortly. A few days ago John McCormack —the
Irish singer with the look of dog-like fidelity in his
eyes—cabled to her to pack up and get a hurry on
at once to Covent Garden, where her prospects were
good. At time of writing the lady was trampling on
her best hat, to get it into her box in the least pos-
sible space of time.
“S.T.S.”: Maeterlinck always has had a keen eye
for the possibilities of a Closed Door, with something
unknown behind it; and in “The Blue Bird” he rides
his hobby almost to death. At the beginning, there
are mysterious knocks on the cottage door. It swings
open and discloses —nothing. Then, in drear silence,
enters what appears to be a witch. Even at the end,
the door is fast, for unknown reasons, until it opens
of itself, also for unknown reasons. But the dog is
left outside howling. In the Future scene, every-
thing leads up to the opening of the great gates at
the back. In the Memory scene, a fog takes the
place of the door, with two frightened children won-
dering what thev will see of the dead folk when it
lifts. In the Graveyard scene (generally omitted)
the idea is much the same. And the Palace of
Night is all closed doors, with Miss Madge Mclntosh
telling in her deepest melodrama tones of the un-
sneakable horrors attendant on opening them. With
his clever suggestions of doom, and his definite state-
ment that trees and animals and nearly everything
in Nature hate children and want to kill them,
Maeterlinck is a nice companion for any sensitive
youngster, I don’t think.
The Melbourne suburb of Brunswick will have two
picture theatres from this time forth. The Empire,
on the Svdney-road, is to be opened to-night (Thurs-
day) with much pomp and ceremony, and Brunswick
claims for this edifice that it is as good as the best
in the business, or even better.
AT POVERTY POINT.
COUP DE THEATRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sit in a curtained box and yawn
In lingering misery,
And the play goes on, and the play goes on,
And the hero struts on a garden lawn,
As the heroine enters L.U.E.
And there on the artificial grass
The plot has come to a pretty pass,
As anyone’s wife could see—
(My wife’s ignoring me!).
Her mouth is hid by a drifting fan
In elegant listlessness ;
Vet the gleam in her eyes would stir a man —
(They were still and chill when the play began)
But I don’t pretend at her moods to guess...
Now, all in a breath, intense, intent,
She flashes a glance that was not meant
For a husband’s eye to see.
(She has not looked at me!).
And why does her fan so misbehave,
And why does her hand shut tight?
(For ever so little a start she gave ;
Was somebody walking over a grave
When the heroine breathed, “Good-bye!
Good-night” ?)
While memories linger, memories stir,
And I have thoughts that are shut from her
Of a parting long ago.
(There are things she need not know.)
He’s playing his part, this actor-chap,
He’s playing his part too well!
My wife’s white fingers twist in her lap,
And I have lifted the door of the trap
That covers rriy personal hell.
(It’s raining in hell in my heart to-night,
For the past stands up as a mirage might,
And I watch my dream made whole
In the desert of my soul.)
Though far apart are the paths that crossed,
Yet a word has joined again
A heart to a heart that loved and lost,
And this is the way that it pays the cost,
The way that it hides its pain.,.
I steal a glance at my wife, and she
Through a film of tears looks back at me—
Pretence we have not guessed
The truth our eyes confessed!
Ah, well, what matter? The lie laid bare,
Since born of an impulse true,
May weld a bond for our hearts to share,
For each has knowledge that each played fair,
And the game begins anew.
Comrade, courage! “Good-bye, good-night”
Absolves us both—we shall start aright;
Clasp hands, dear heart... and yet
How shall our hearts forget ?
VICTORIA.

Edmund Fisher has prepared an assort-
ment of his Bulletin verses for publica-
tion, in book form, under the title of “The
Kiss of Dolly Day, and Other Cynical
Rhymes for Recitation.” E. W. Cole, of
Melbourne Book Arcade and elsewhere, is
the publisher, and the book will be on the
market next week. The author of the well-
known “Billiard Marker’s Yarn” has written
many, many pungent verses for The Bul-
letin during the past 25 years or more. His
selection includes the best cf them, and, as
compared with the ultimate cost of kisses in
general, “The Kiss of Dolly Day” will be
found ridiculously cheap at Is. and 2s. 6d.

Another report of an infant school in an
overcrowded and insanitary state was thrown
at Victoria’s Education Department the
other day. This time it is the Middle Park
infants’ penitentiary, and the council that
sent the information curtly intimated that if
repairs weren’t carried out in a fortnight
it would instruct its health officer to con-
demn the building. Perhaps the best thing
would be to condemn the whole insanitary
department in one act and be done with it.
The Bulletin receives from “William
Hughes, Town Clerk,” a copy of “a letter
sent by his Worship the Mayor, Alderman
Munger, to the secretary of the Mascot Em-
pire Celebration Committee,” and “as the
subject is of more than passing importance,”
William Hughes, town clerk, wants it kindly
published. Alderman Munger, it seems, has
a brilliant idea. “For future Empire Cele-
brati ms it is desirable to offer a prize for
an essay on Imperial problems, the prize to
be open for competition to every member of
the Empire, irrespective of- race or color.”
The prize—the Mayor thinks it might be a
10-guinea medal—“should be offered in true
Imperial spirit to the Red Indian of Canada,
the Chinese of Hongkong, the Hindus of
India, as well as the residents of Mascot.”
Having inspected Mascot for the first time
the other day, and found it about the most
slatternly, unkempt, ill-paved and ragged of
the 50 or so absurd little municipalities of
suburban Sydney, The Bulletin moves as
an amendment that the Mayor be urgently
requested to repair the streets in his own
squalid area, the same being now partly rut
and partly rubbish tip, and that the town
clerk be instructed to lock up the municipal
stationery until the job is completed.

“P.X.” goes exploring :
I found a one-pub town the other day, and now
I think less than I used to of the policy of pub-
reduction. It was a sizeable town, in spite of vicis-
situdes. At one time, quite lately, there were a
couple of thousand people or more in it, and they
had plenty of money to burn. But the local Hop was
strong in the land, and at first he said “no pubs at
all.” Afterwards he compromised, anil said “One
pub.” So tile one pub got its license, and has made,
I’m told, at least £.12,000 in five years.
The pub is a large barn of a place. When I was
there it didn’t have any floor-covering of any sort
in the hall, except dirt. I dragged my luggage to
my bedroom and fell over five cals. The girl ex-
plained that there were 1000 cats about the place,
but if there weren’t 1000 cats there would be
1,000,000 rats. I didn’t find any rats in the bed-
room, but I found plenty of other things. Evidently
the local cat won’t hunt small game. Or perhaps
that was the job of the 1001 th cat.
Where the place
wasn’t strewn with
eats it was strewn
with fowls. While the
cats explored the
kitchen table the fowls
fossicked on the kitch-
en safe. At the first
meal somebody drop-
ped half a loaf of
well-handled bread
and dived for it in-
stantly. He was an
old hand and knew
that some of the fowls
were waiting under
the more-or-less spot-
less damask.
I found the bath-
room the first morn-
ing. It was early, and
I was sorry to have to
disturb a hen and her
quarter-grown brood
that perched on the
edge of the bath. They
were mostly pullets, so
I had to ask the old
lady to take them out.
On considering the
bath, I went out too.
The girl was sorry
there wasn’t nobody to
clean the boots. Still,
she’d get a cup of tea
presently. It arrived
in half an hour, along
with her regrets that
the milkman hadn’t
come yet. I’m not an
expert in these mat-
ters, but I think she
tried to make up for
the deficiency by put-
ting in some tomato
sauce. It may, however, have, been something else.
It may, in fact, have been almost anything else.
When L went to ask for my bill the boss said it’d
do by-and-bye. : When I urged that I was leaving
directly, and pinned him down to the job, he fished
a package label out of a litter in the corner of the
room, and made some calculations in Arabic. Then
he groaned and said he reckoned it was about two-
poun’-’leven, and could I do a taste? I did a taste
and reckoned that, when the State makes up its
mind that there should be only one pub in a town,
it had better also make up its mind to run that pub
itself.

After he had heard Archbishop Clarke’s
story, John Norton’s legal representative
gave his libel fight best, and agreed to say
that he wasn’t justified in biting the episco-
nal gaiters, and would hand over a cheque
for £lOOO to cover the rip. Of course, there
will also be Costs, probably another couple
of thousand. All through the weariful
Gnash ruction the public was led to believe
that the trouble was due to the cleric’s mere
“indiscreet conduct” as regarded a lady

that the fuss arose in part through
Gnash’s philandering with the family
nursemaid, a damsel of 16, with what
the good man called an “angel face,”
And as that wasn’t the first philan-
dering, Duffy, on John’s behalf, decided that
there was monev to be saved by getting
down quickly. Still, Gnash had poor luck.
The case stopped after Archbishop Clarke
had told his story, which was mainly a story
about Gnash. And the accused cleric hadn’t
a ghost of a chance to tell his story.
TRAPELLERS’ TALES.

in the ancient land of the Pharaohs!”

“Ooray” : The Maoriland “red-raggers” are
on a real strike jag just now. Members of
the M.L. Federation are out at Waihi and
Reefton (mining districts), and a deter-
mined and hostile front is being shown by
both employees and employers. Two thou-
sand men are estimated to be on strike or
locked-out, and the official mouthpiece, the
M.L. Worker, sools them on thusly:—
Make the fight your fight, 0 mighty working class,
yourself part of it, your time and talents its servant.
Shout for victory! Work for victory! Give for
victory!... .Again, the curse of law-made unionism.
Again, arbitration is reaching out to strangle us.
Fight we must, and fight we shall. Give us fighters !
Give us Dantons (“dare and to dare, and again to
dare”). Give us a red-letter campaign.
It is a fine large hosannah, but there is a
lack of information as to what the battle
aims at, and what exactly the victory will
be if there is a victory.
Windsor (Vic.) Angling Club has made
the obvious discovery that the practice of
depositing silt in Port Phillip Bay is not
only unhealthy to the barracouta and other
beasts therein, but is liable to prove fatal,
as it will ultimately leave them no water to
dwell in. The club wants to know how long
this foolishness is to continue. In reply, the
Public Works Department, which more or
less controls the matter, says that only
27,000,000 cubic yards of silt have been
dumped into the bay since the beginning of
things, and 27,000,000 yards means a mere
layer of Ift. thick spread over 20 square
miles. That is one way of looking at it.
Another is that all Tffie silt thus deposited,
as well as what is washed down by natural
causes, will gradually find its way to the
deepest part of the bay, and that is the
channel which is continually being kept
dredged from the city to the Heads, and
27,000,000 cubic yards, swept into a channel
a quarter of a mile wide from Yarra to ocean
means a layer of mullock sft. thick. So as
fast as that channel is cleaned out it begins
to fill up again with the scourings just
taken out. The fool department says it
would cost too much to spread the stuff over
the low-lying reaches along the lower Yarra,
but when the department has finally put so
much in the bay that it is full, and Mel-
bourne is an inland city with only the shal-
low Yarra meandering from it to the ocean,
it will dawn on a lot of people that the first
cost would have been the least cost, and that
to eternally scoop out mullock and put it
where it will immediately start to wash
back again is about as intellectual a feat as
that of Sisyphus. Sisyphus, however, had
one excuse —he had no option.
“Chasuble”: Primate Wright’s visit to
Melbourne puts another wedge into the rift
between the Highs and the Lows, though the
split does not gape so wide as it does in
Sydney. The Lows of Victoria call upon the
Primate to be Primate, while the Highs
say, “We have no King but Caesar,” mean-
ing him of Melbourne. The Primate came
specially for a missionary meeting held in the
Town Hall. Archbishop Clarke did not
attend; but Norton and Nash were excuse
enough for that. It is not long since the
Primate was last in Melbourne, yet he looks
aged in even this brief time. It would not be
surprising to hear of his giving up, like
Barry. Clarke has nothing like the worries,
even with Nash thrown in; Melbourne
churchmen who visit Sydney find the bitter-
ness there inconceivable. Besides, Wright has
the misfortune to be a man of stronger con-
victions in matters of detail than the other
Arch., who paddles his canoe comfortably
enough alongside the Evangelicals.

LINES TO A SPLENDID EFFORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Liberal platform of the Melbourne Conference
lias given great dissatisfaction to many supporters of
the party. —Daily paper.
Strange as the moon emerging from eclipse,
Seen through an overhanging haze of smoke
Blent with white fog, and breathed on by the lips
Of some vast universe of throbbing beer,
I saw the wondrous thing that sizzling broke
Last week upon Australia’s atmosphere.
as when some soaring aeroplane is caught
Amid the barbed-ivire fences of the sky,
And all the merry winds in sport have taught
Peculiar lessons to its various wings,
Such was the thing that challenged me to try
To understand its weird imaginings.
Thin as the chill airs moving in the morn,
Scarce rustling the faint leaves upon the tree,
And tenderer than the sigh of the unborn
Young raindrop that shall gather e’er the eve,
Frail as Wragge’s drought, and sad as memory,
The thing in which they ask me to believe.
Soft as the suds of over-lathered soap,
Pale as affrighted ghosts of unhatched eggs,
Tepid as summer ink, or fainting hope,
And vague as chaos or eternity,
Uncertain as intoxicated legs,
The platform of Confusion’s policy.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

Baden Powell, having viewed the situa-
tion in Australia, seems to understand that
although he may have come here to Scout,
lie remains to be scouted. His interest in the
kids who play the obsolete game has hardly
a pretence of sincerity in it. In Melbourne
the other day the chagrined “Chief” chal-
lenged disrespectful comment by escaping
in a motor-car from some silly function,
whilst a. lot of damp infant “scouts” were
waiting in the pouring rain for his blessing.
Baden Powell is looking for glorious advt.
all the time. Seeing that the compulsory
cadet system has swamped his scout craft at
this end of the Hempire, he acts like a show-
man down on his luck. Probably, also, he
feels that the yarns which passed for gospel
in Britain are getting sceptical criticism
from the Australian boy. Applause is limited
when he spouts from the platform absurd
stories of his ready-witted subterfuges at
Mafeking; as, for instance, the anecdote of
how he scared a party of Boers who came
upon him suddenly when he was out on his
own by motioning with his hands to a non-
existent brigade of followers, as though he
had them hiding in a trench prepared to
charge the foe when he gave the signal. The
Boers, in this lovely fiction, no sooner saw
him perform the “lie-low-and-wait” move-
ment with liis hands, than they turned and
fled like hunted devils. Which wasn’t like
their tactics. How could a surprise packet
of Boers have got near enough to the wan-
dering Baden Pool to spot his antics without
being within shooting distance, and the Boer
is a mighty prompt shooter. The yarn is so
thin that even a 12-year-old scout can see
through it at 20 paces.
A GENUINE WORSHIPPER.
Miss Sybil Arundale expresses the opinion that
dancing has a good deal to do with physical develop-
ment, and the strengthening of the limbs and feet.
“Dancing,” she says, “gives the feet proper exercise.
You can strengthen them until they become almost
prehensile. I can stand on the tips of my bare
toes, and jump off them, landing on them again,
with not the slightest inconvenience. The girl who
has not been trained as a dancer would howl with
agony if she did this. I’ve heard them do this when
they’ve stubbed a toe. I could almost kick a ward-
robe with my boots off without feeling any unpleas-
ant result.” — Theatrical item.
J OHNNIE: "My word! Kick a wardrobe with
your bare feet and feel no inconvenience!
I wish I was a wardrobe, don'tcherknow."
Premier McGowen, of the Ma State, lost
the support of certain believers in cleanli-
ness by breaking his Government’s promise
to put £5OOO on the estimates to pay for a
crematorium or corpse furnace. Yet the
movement goes on. A few weeks ago the
Bishop of Truro (England) joined those
whose work in the vineyard is finished. He
threw up the pastoral crook, and left the
sheep shepherdless and the palace empty.
His earthly tenement was burned at Holder’s
Green, in North London, and his cinders
were enclosed in an urn and placed in a
niche of Truro Cathedral. To return to
McGowen —that great ’Bus-Misser takes up
exactly the same attitude towards cremation
and tlie public demand for it that he assumes
towards the totalisator and the public de-
mand for that. He “doesn’t believe in it” ;
therefore, the public—like a kid that doesn’t
know what is good for it —shan’t have it!
Yet the old man would probably feel hurt if
anyone said in his hearing that he is a stodgy
and unprogressive Premier and a Tory of a
very backward kind. He is progressive
where the' party programme lays it down
that he must progress or lose his job. Wher-
ever he is left to himself he about ranks with
Bruce Smith.
“Squills”: A man passed out at Wallal
(W.A.) the other day from diver’s paralysis,
after being down in 19 fathoms of water for
an hour or so. Diver’s paralysis, alias
caisson disease, is common enough, though
rarely fatal, and it needn’t be either as com-
mon ’or as fatal as it is. To work
under water it is necessary to compress the
air so that the external water pressure may
be counterbalanced. Ordinarily the atmo-
sphere hits us at about 151 b.
to the square inch, and it is
not usual to strike trouble
till this has been trebled.
The unpleasantness begins
when the surface is reached.
Under high pressure the
blood absorbs nitrogen in ex-
cessive quantities, and if de-
compression occurs too quick-
ly, the nitrogen bubbles are
suddenly released, like the
gas in a soda-water bottle
when the cork is taken out.
The released bubbles, flying
around, block the small ves-
sels in the spinal cord, and
pretty soon unpleasant
things happen. The only
way to prevent it is to care-
fully graduate the pressure
as the diver is coming up,
by “staging” or “locking
out.” The rule during the
building of the Mississippi
bridge at St. Louis was to
take at least one minute for
every 61b. reduction, and the
results were excellent. When
a mistake has been made, and
the diver has been brought
up too quickly, re-compres-
sion, followed by slow exit,
is the best means of reliev-
ing the fierce pains of para-
lysis.
From the Old-
est Inhabitant:
Apropos Dr.
Lang’s old house in
Kent - street, Syd-
ney (B. 13/6/’l2).
There is a yet
older house of
some note in this
once-swell street.
It was built in the
’2o’s, and its first
tenant was Jus-
tice (afterwards
Chief Justice)
Dowling. He lived
there until he got
a grant in Dar-
linghurst and built
Brougham Hall.
In the ’so’s the
Kent-street house
was occupied first
by John Anderson,
M.D., and then by
Thomas Henry Neale, who made a pot as a carcase
butcher, and another pot as a moneylender, and
died one of the wealthiest men in Old Sydney. The
year 1866 saw the aged domicile a vinegar factory;
then, in 1868, certain philanthropic people bought
the property and turned it into a Soup Kitchen and
Night Refuge, and Sydney’s derelicts still doss in
what Old Sydney reckoned the mansion of its Chief
Justice.

Last tveek, the morning paper described
with pride and enthusiasm the wonderful
new strong room which is being built at the
neiv Treasury Buildings, in Melbourne, to
hold Andrew Fisher’s gold reserve. The
odds against Fisher ever being cleaned out
by an ordinary masked Sikes are tremend-
ous. But for all the use it is against the
banks, the massive contraption, with its
enormously heavy door, combination locks
and so forth, might as well be made of brown
paper. If Andrew’s unlucky time arrives, he
will be cleaned out in a perfectly legal way
by three or four polite and affable officials,
who will drop in during the garish hours of
day and do the business without a jemmy.
Apropos, many years ago, before the boom
and the burst, the head office of the Q. N.
Bank was rather famous as the possessor of
the most efficient series of burglar alarms
in Australia. And while the late T. Mcll-
wraith and his friends were getting away
with 16 overdrafts, these alarms didn’t ring
once.
COW V. CRICKET.
There is still another item to be added to the list of complaints laid at the door of the dairying
industry viz., the decadence in rural centres of the national outdoor game. In bygone days, before the
advent of the dairy herd, a local cricket match was the centre to which gravitated the youth, beauty
and age of almost any country district on every Saturday afternoon. — N.S.W. School Inspector.
A most important and momentous domestic trouble which leaves no time Jor consideration of
the smiling stranger without the gate.
The last time the Commonwealth asked
for power to control trusts, combines and
monopolies, Fat burbled that in Australia
there really weren’t any monopolies to con-
trol ; and the Cow-men of Victoria mostly
believed him and voted “No.” Next time
they will probably know better. At the
beginning of the 1910-T1 butter season the
Victorian proprietary firms of butterbox-
makers remarked, with astonishing unan-
imity, that “owing to the rise in wages, etc.,”
the price of boxes would have to be whooped
up from Is. 4-Jd. per box to Is. 7M. The
co-operative butter factories had perforce to
pay; but they took counsel together, and
proceeded to establish two factories, one at
Dandenong and one in the Western District;
whereupon Private Enterprise suddenly and
unanimously dropped its price to Is. 5d.,
and forgot all about the alleged increase in
wages.

AN EASY VICTORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As the Governor-General’s motor-car was passing
along Collins-street yesterday afternoon, William
Hudson, aged 19 years, attempted to pass in front of
it. Though every possible attempt to evade accident
was made, Hudson was knocked on the tram-line and
received a gash on the right arm. A tram was
pulled up just in time to prevent further injury to
Hudson. He was taken by Captain Wheatley,
A.D.C., who was in the car, to the Melbourne Hos-
pital, but after treatment by Dr. Wilson was allowed
to go to his home. The car was not damaged.
-—Melbourne Argus.
Whilst venturing to cross the street
(How reckless some boys are!)
Young Willie Hudson chanced to meet
The Gee-Gee’s inotah-car.
Despite its toot of wild alarm
The youth, who sought renown,
Attacked the motah with his arm
And tried to knock it down.
He suffered for his action rash:
The motah stopped his blow,
Inflicting on his arm a gash
And laying William low.
He fell before a tram that might
Have torn him limb from limb:
Yet, full of pity for his plight,
The tram stopped short of him.
Poor William, lying on the ground
Was splashed with blood and dirt,
But when they picked him up they found
The motah quite unhurt.
The beaten bruiser, looking blue
And shaken by his fall,
Was carried by the victor to
The Melbourne Hospital.
And there a surgeon put him right,
And gently greased his joints.
When William asked: “Who won the fight?”
They said: “The Car —on points.”
Bill’s dauntless arm is in a sling,
And damaged is his shirt.
But —this is the important thing—
The motah is unhurt.

O.K.
NO TIPPING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Church is, I think, called upon, too, to do
something towards suppressing this reprehensible
practice of tipping.— Melbourne parson.
The Common Man went into church,
And heard the lisping parson preach
Up in his lofty cedar perch
Of wickedness in those who reach
For worldly gain.
With periods so softly tripping,
Like little runs of molten dripping,
Did he arraign
The custom perilous of tipping.
He breathed the offertory then,
And sharp the Common Man arose,
Possessed with indignation, when
The platter lingered ’neath his nose.
“Avaunt!” he cried,
No votive thripny smugly slipping.
“Such servile blokes deserve a whipping.
1 can’t abide
This practice beggarly of tipping!”

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“The Triumphant Fisherman”: I have
been patiently reading through the plan descriptions
of the great and beautiful city of Yassomalley, or
Kingberra, or O’Kingfish, or whatever it is to be
called. As I understand it, the great feature of
Yassucan or Youeantfish will be the involved and
relentless gaoling of the half-pint of water available.
The mud-choked trout that dwells in that region
will have to transform himself into a plain vegetable
when the gaoler-engineers get to work with
their locks and start imprisoning the element which
a modern French poet describes as “so frightfully
anonymous,” because you never know where it was
working before you got it. Once water trickles into,
or falls on, Fishoking—or Fishchoking—it is never
going to escape again, but will remain there until it
it quite dead, or until all the inhabitants are dead
of smelling it. Or, maybe, when it has become old
and acquired a slimy green overcoat, desperate people
will cut openings and invite it to run off the prem-
ises and take its mosquitoes with it. And it will
find that it is too sluggish to run, and will subside
gently and start to die in three volumes, until the
unemployed are bribed to cart and shove great
stretches of the landscapes upon its wide dwelling-
place. The manufacture of lakes in a dry region
necessitates desperate experiments in water economy,
and I foresee an interesting time for Fishchoking—
or is it Kingrooster? (I never can remember the
name of that splendid place).

“Billy T.” : Danny Clarke was what you
might call a careful man. Thrift with him was auto-
matic. He was frugal in his sleep, and never moved
in bed, to save wear and tear on the blankets.
Danny was even sparing of his breath, for it was
noted at The Bunyip (Michael Bourke, licensed vic-
tualler) that he never shouted. But Clarke was not
deaf; he could hear another man shouting a mile
off. If any stranger happened to be wearing out a
cheque at the Bunyip, Daniel Clarke was invariably
one of the reception committee. Daniel’s total absti-
nence from strong drink of his own buying had been
noticed. It was frequently commented upon, and
some hints of popular reproach must have come to
his ears, with the result that he very nearly shouted
once. He started from home with the intention of
shouting. When he reached the bar door his splendid
resolution had not faltered. He marched to the
counter, and cried: “Well, boys, what is it going to
] )R i” Then repentance came with an overwhelm-
ing rush, and he added, hastily—“wet or dry?”
“A. 11.” :As nobody has asked me for my
testimony concerning the question of sharks and
colored bait, I butt in to say that I have seen Malays
diving from pearling luggers into waters simply
infested with the marine demon. The bathers
shout, sing, clap their hands and make as much
noise as possible as they reach the waters; but show
no fear. Possibly it is Malayan music which keeps
the shark at a distance ; anyway, as soon as it be-
gins he bolts out of earshot in the quickest time
possible. The Bingliis, too, all along the nor’-west
coast dive freely in popular shark resorts, though
they demur at entering the water singly. In com-
pany, they shout and make a great commotion be-
fore leaving the shallows, and will then swim and
dive freely together. The Malays have a theory
that even should a shark make an attack he can be
put out of action by a single blow, planted firmly
on the extreme tip of the upper jaw. They say
this paralyses the animal. At various ports in the
Solomons I have watched the islanders diving for
hours into water where I could see sharks cruising.
These brutes were of a tawny orange color, with
darker stripes on the back, and were smaller than
the species usually seen off the Australian coasts.
They made no attempt to attack the swimmers, but
scuttled off at every splash. On occasion a Euro-
pean has been tackled by the biggest specimens, but
they can hardly be regarded as maneaters. The
Malays on the pearling grounds love shark-catching,
and have some novel methods. Should a sheep be
killed on a schooner, the skin and waste parts are
baited on a strong hook and trailed through the
water. As brother shark makes a swoop for the
counter-lunch, it is lifted swiftly just beyond his
reach. This tantalises the customer till he comes
nearer and nearer. In his rage he leaps partly out
of the water, and lands in the arms of a whirling
lassoo. A bullet concludes the entertainment. At
certain seasons up North, a big trade is done in
shark blubber, which is rich in an oil that is
steadily increasing in commercial value.
* * * *
“Machete”: Do you know the Papuan
sling? The great western bow is well enough
known, likewise the curious “catching loop,” which
is like a long-handled tennis racket without the
strings, and furnished with a neat spike to jab into
your fleeing enemy’s cervical vertebrae once the loop
is over his head. But the sling, one of the most
famous and efficient of primitive missile weapons, was
used by few Papuan tribes, and has now almost
vanished. It was the principal weapon of the sturdy
little natives of Goodenough Island, a precipitious
mountain rising 7000 ft. from the sea. The Papuan
constabulary, who cared little for the Tugeri arrows
or the clubmen of the North, could hardly be got to
face the Island slingers. The implement is of the
ordinary pattern, but about Oft. long when doubled,
and the pouch for the stone is of hide. The projec-
tile used was about the size of a turkey’s egg, care-
fully ground from diorite to a double-ended shape.
When the sniper wished to let fly he dropped a stone
on the ground and slipped it into the sling with his
foot. Then with a strikingly graceful motion he be-
o-an his swing. On the released end of the sling was
fastened a cracker of pandamus fibre, which snapped
like a rifle shot. At any range up to 200yds. the
stone was much the same as a bullet, especially 1 1
struck bone. The people who had to tackle the
slingers in their fortified mountain villages had only
one consolation. The slinger had to fully expose
himself to “fire.” The time was then ripe for the
individual with the rifle.

“Tialti”: Don’t know how much “Paroa”
(B. 16/5/’l2) is prepared to punt on the kiwi as
the champion Jack Sheppard of the bird
world, but I'll cover it and back the Maori-
land kea (Nestor notabilis). This hefty par-
rot, which inhabits the mountain regions of
the South Island, is charged on the highest
authority with filing his way to liberty out
of a wire cage. Naturally Thomas Henry
Potts makes mention of this characteristic,
and in Hutton and Drummond’s book are
two specific accounts of remarkable escapes.
A captured kea was shoved under an inverted
iron bucket, the handle-tags of which would
not allow the rim to touch the ground all
round. The cooped-up kea shoved his bill
underneath, dug his feet well in the ground,
used his head as a lever, heaved up the
bucket, and sped the parting guest. Another
was immured in a wooden box-cage, but he
sawed his way through the timber like a rat
through a cardboard box. Caught young, the
kea is easily tamed, yet the settlers do not
seem to take to him kindly. Perhaps his
habit of mounting the backs of sheep and
sawing through wool, hide and mutton until
he gets the kidney fat has caused their anti-
pathy towards him. In a Lands Department
report it was once recorded that 200 sheep
were thus “kidneyed” in a single night at
Lake Wanaka. On account of this habit as
much as 10s. a head has been offered for the
thoroughly dead kea. The ancestry of the
bird is not accurately known to me, but I
fancy there is a circular saw in his pedigree,
also a swordfish.
“Snell”: The man on the fence is an Aus-
tralian character. Possibly he is conspicuous
amongst us for the reason that over millions of
square miles of our continent the fence is the only
safe and fairly comfortable thing for a man to sit
on. Have heard lately from a guard on a Victorian
line, whose attention had been attracted to a woolly
inhabitant who habitually perched on a post and rail
fence near the line out on a flat and arid waste in-
fested with a few sheep and one paralytic hut. The
man was there so often that the whole railway ser-
vice became infected with curiosity concerning him.
One day a mishap to the engine necessitated a stop
in the vicinity of the native on his perch, so the
guard sauntered over to make pertinent inquiries.
“Goo’ dayl” “Day, mate.” “Look here, 1 hope
you won’t mind my asking, but why do you spend
so much time on this fence?” The percher blushed
a little. “Oh, well, yeh see, Mister,” he said, “the
fact is, I’m looking for a wife.” Whiskers still sits
out on his long, lone fence by the road that goes no-
where and which nobody uses. The Railway De-
partment will supply further particulars to any en-
terprising spinster who may meditate passing that
way.

“Vagabond” : Just returned from stalking
deer on Maoriland’s highlands, and a real strenuous
time it was. The job of getting to the deer-grounds
used up the best part of two days, two horses, and
two bottles of whisky, and included a long drive on
roads that roamed recklessly over and through
boulder-strewn river beds, and along hillsides with
one wheel of the conveyance hovering on the brink
of Eternity and the other flying round in the Bank-
ruptcy Court. When the road petered out we had
to pack supplies on to camp over tracks a respectable
goat would require a bonus to tackle. My chief ob-
jection to deer-stalking is that there’s too much stalk-
ing and not enough deer, and that the pleasures of the
chase crowd out any other simple pleasures, like
smoking, eating and sleeping. You must crawl out of
the blankets in the darkest (and coldest) hour before
the dawn, and most of the morning you keep on
clambering over scenery which appears to have been
put in a bag and shaken out by a birthday party of
intoxicated devils. The arranged “long halt” at mid-
day usually cuts down to 15 minutes to boil the billy
and swallow stale bread and corned beef, because you
must hurry up to get to some gullies where the
chance is good, etc. When it is too dark to shoot
you stagger 1000 miles back to camp, wolf a feed,
light a pipe, and are just thinking of removing your
wet boots when the moon lifts, and it is time to look
down by the river in case a herd or two of deer
are holding a reception. For hours you crouch in
a damp (you can spell it without the “p” if you
like), dismal swamp, but nothing comes of it except
severe cramps and frozen trotters. It’s a glorious
pastime. I’ll say nothing of the reward of your
labors when the noble stag falls before your unerring
rifle. Let others tell of the fierce rapture at the
sight of the frenzied leap and the crashing fall of
the quarry, of the joy of posing for the camera with
one foot on the corpse, and the satisfaction of carry-
ing the fine head into camp. I could tell it myself
(I’ve a splendid imagination), but I’ll refrain. The
guides who take parties into the hills and pilot them
to where there’s a good chance, etc., demand anything
from £1 a day up. They earn it.
» * * *
“Move-on” : There is a fragment of the
whaling industry that has been hanging on in M.L.
now for a good many years, and is still going. Up
near the Bay of Islands, north of Auckland, there is
a little chunk of rock, about the toss of a pair of
boots from the mainland, and between the island and
the shore has always been a favorite promenade for
the swimming oil-tanks. A huge net is stretched
across the channel, and, when a visiting whale gets
snarled up in it, the local hunters gather up their
guns and fish knives, and proceed to prod him full of
holes and drag his carcase ashore and boil it down.
The net is made of heavy wire rope and steel chains,
the average whale being too boisterous for the health
of anything less strong. Just why the blowers take
this particular short cut is a mystery, but they have
kept on doing it, or trying to do it, as far back as
go the records of the white man or the Oldest Brown
Inhabitant, and the hoariest old Big Fish Lie in
the district relates how the speaker or the speaker’s
father or grandfather just missed a fine 110-ton
“fish” coming in at the submarine cellar door.
P’raps there’s a special “Little Pub We Know around
the Corner” of the whales there, or it may be that
the rocky island is “The Block,” and the lady and
lady-killer whales are in the habit of strolling round
to show off their latest barnacle wear. There is
another local theory that the whales come there to
scrape off their barnacles, but it doesn’t attempt to
explain just why they should call regularly at that
one particular spot, when they have thousands of
miles of rocky coastline to rub up against. Anyhow
they come, and a lot of ’em stay—or their bones do,
while the rest of them depart peacefully and smell-
fully in barrels.
“Dynamo” : Your friend “Jinnnie Panni-
kin” is correct in his statement (B. 23/5/'l2) that
the shell parrot is one of our most beautiful rrteat-
and-feather aeroplanes. But I marvel that James
has only met one talking specimen of the bird. I
owned a voluble sample myself, years ago, at
Broken Hill, what time Mr. Pannikin came along
and examined us in reading, hydrostatics, Venetian
history, Greek roots and eonchology ; and there
were, to my knowledge, a dozen similar talkative
fowl on the Barrier. And I once met a perverted
shell parrot in Melbourne which used to denounce
the Pope good and hard in a public house, and de-
clare that the whole College of Cardinals drank. Its
owner was a Roman Catholic. He kept the bird as
an evidence of the straits to which Protestants were
reduced to disseminate their slanders.
THE TWO THINGS NEEDFUL.
DEBUTANTE : "And what is the ‘ glass of fashion
and the mould of your form in society ?"
VETERAN OF FORTY SEASONS: “ Champagne ,
my dear, and corsets?”
Gunnar Landtman, Professor of Sociology
in the University of Helsingfors, Finland, has been
contributing to the old argument concerning mor-
ality as Brown Brother sees it in Papua. For two
years Landtman has been studying the natives near
the mouth of the Fly River, and he has arrived at
this conclusion: “Morality as understood by us is un-
known among the New Guinea tribes. On the coast
the natives have more touches of civilisation than
those of the interior, owing to contact with Euro-
pean traders, but the great majority are simply un-
moral. At certain seasons of the year they ha.ve
gluttonous and sensual orgies, with promiscuous in-
dulgence in polygamy. Unnatural practices are in-
dulged in by a number of interior tribes, while cer-
tain of the customs could scarcely be described. They
spring from sexual excesses. While these festivals
are not in progress the tribes are monogamous, and
the germ of a crude: common law is seen in the
penalty visited on those who depart from the custom
of this time. The male offender is invariably
killed.’’
TOLPODDLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tolpoddle, in Dorsetshire, England, lias erected a
to six convicts transported to Australia, in
1833, for conspiring to raise the rate of wages, whic
was then 7s. a week. —Daily publication.
What shame is this, that convicts vile,
At vicious plots detected,
Are honored with a granite pile
In public square erected,
While that sublime old English gent
Of rectitude the model,
Who o’er the ocean had them sent,
Receives no grand acknowledgment
In faded old Tolpoddle?
Is lawlessness become correct,
Conspiracy a virtue?
Tolpoddlers, when did high respect
For Property desert you?
These scamps sought eighteen pence a day
Their passions vile to coddle;
They worked for nix in Bot’ny Bay,
And now they get a martyr’s pay
In radical Tolpoddle.
’Twere well when strikers fill the street,
And leaders on a barrel
Demand for Hodge enough to eat
And second-hand apparel,
If Genius sculped most semblably
That fine old Tory’s noddle,
Who formed with chains in ’Thirty-Three
The first trades-union sent o’er sea
From glorious Tolpoddle.
