“Pick”: “Wang” (B. 23/5/T2) asks, “Do
rabbits eat birds? or do birds leave because they don’t
like the smell of rabbits?” Neither is the case.
What happens is this. When rabbits invade a dis-
trict, the Pastures Protection Board enforces the
fool decree that phosphorus or strychnine shall
be spread broadcast, with the natural result
that, in time, all the birds are poisoned.
I have seen flocks of pigeons, parrots and
magpies wiped off the landscape by this means in a
single afternoon. Incidentally, “Wang” mentions
that there are plenty of insects. Of course. The
, birds that formerly ate the insects are all destroyed,
and the delighted insects increase and multiply until,
in many country parts, they are a worse menace to
the farmer than the rabbits ever were.
THE
DARLING
OF THE
GODS.
“Iyles I.”: I don’t bear him any grudge
about it, for I am a Hayseed myself; but I must
confess that I derive a whole hogful of inexpensive
amusement from Bill’s visit to, the city. He tries
hard to disguise the fact (I don’t know why he
should) that the fragrance of gum-leaves is on him.
He has enveloped himself in a new tailor-made suit,
got under a new hat—a bit wide in the brim—and
his feet are surrounded by new boots. But, some-
how, the whole blessed arrangement puts him away.
His clothes fit him everywhere but where they ought
to; the umbrageous brim of his headgear looks twice
as wide as usual, and his boots squeak agonisingly
every time they collide with the pavement. He also
purchases an umbrella, because he thinks a “brolly”
is the hall-mark of the city man. And he wears it
wherever he goes, irrespective of whether the day is
wet or fine. But it is a terrifying possession, and
hangs like a necklace of grindstones on his conscience.
He trips over it in the street; pushes it into every
second person’s eye, and eventually loses it in a tram-
car. That represents the young and up-to-date
cousin from the country. But there is the old bush
battler, long-haired and be-wrinkled, who doesn’t at-
tempt to hide the obviosity of his residential whence-
ness. Rather, he glories in the fact. He has wheat
in his whiskers, grass-seeds in his hair, and the odor
of sheep is all over him. It is a shocking waste of
good produce. But, all things considered, the bush-
man isn’t very surprised 'at the wonders of the city.
Jim Bunyip, from Wantalaggi, was the exception.
It was a wet day, and at the races the ladies held
their dresses well aloft to escape the destructive
dampness. Jim beheld the blaze of colored stockings.
He told us about it when he returned. “It was a
rare good show o’ fillies,” he said, “with rainbows
round their hocks. An’, my word, yer oughter jus’
seen their trottin’ straps!”
In 1906 there were no special provisions for
safety, the pay roll of an American steel plant num-
bered 6000. The fatalities were 47. In 1909, after
the introduction of a safety Committee, out of a pay
roll of 7000 only 12 fatalities occurred. Of this
number one death was caused by a man inadvertently
stepping before a running loco. Five other deaths
happened from causes for which no possible explana-
tion could be offered—in other words real accidents.
Phillips Ormonde & Co., patent attorneys, 266 Flin-
ders St., Melbourne.*
THE IRRESISTIBLE FORCE.
T. TOPER: " No, sir , ye're wrong. I can resist the drink easy enough; it's the barmaid's
lure what's draggin ’ me down."
North Coast (N.S.W.) cow-cockies are
mostly in a state of great distress because of th<
Board of Health’s wickedness in forcing farmers to
boil all pig-food with a view to check the spread oi
tuberculosis. The habit of the cow-cocky is to feed
the pigs with the milk of cows he considers diseased,
and when the said diseased Bluey and Strawberry give
up the ghost their flesh is fed to the grunters. The
men who object to the stoppage of this practice have
also been “resolving” against “State interference be-
tween a man and his children,” and altogether they
are first-class representatives of the Tory party to
which —as members of the Farmers and Settlers’
Association—they are firmly attached.
“Kobbi”: Re “Dinkum’s” query, “Can
animals smell water?” There is little or no doubt
about it. Watch a horse just unsaddled in country
that is absolutely new to him, and note how quickly
he gets the lay of the land, and, after a bite or two,
moves off to water, which may be the merest puddle
half a mile off. Not only has Edwin the powerful
sense of smell, but the instinct also to pick up the
“fall of the country,” so that when he starts out,
there is no aimless walking from place to place in
search of a pub. Besides, the general “feel” of the
country assists; for trees in well-watered or soaked
ground give off a different “atmosphere” from their
comrades of the dry hills. Apropos something of the
sort, I have noticed that iguanas, when foraging
round the apparently deserted camp, move the long
tongue from side to side constantly. Wouldn’t care
to say that the beast smells with his tongue, but it
has often struck me that his long taster catches the
wireless perfume-gram. The little fence lizard has
the same habit when searching for his dinner on the
splintered panel. Either he uses his tongue to assist
in locating the counter-lunch, or else he is merely
smacking his lips in anticipation of fly-steak.
STOPPED HOURS OF MISERY.
“The excruciating pains of rheumatism have
caused me many hours of misery,” says Mrs. Jane
Pierce, 1340 Sturt Street, Ballarat, Victoria. “It
seemed as though I would never get relief, then I
started to use Chamberlain’s Pain Balm. The first
bottle showed the merits of the Balm. Now I apply
hot fomentations and have the Balm well rubbed in
at the first appearance of pain, and it wards off what
used to be days of misery for me.”*
“R. McK.” : Let me add to “Leo Lear’s”
description (B. 23/5/’ 12) of the Devil’s playground
in Western Victoria, that at Alvic, 11 miles from
Colac, quite a number of bungholes similar to those
he mentions can be seen. Standing on Red Rock
11 small lakes are visible, besides Lake Colac (the
largest sheet of fresh water in the State) and Lake
Corangamite (the largest expanse of salt water).
There is only about two miles of land between these
two lakes, and the saltness of Corangamite has long
been a puzzle. AH the small lakes are salt, which
seems to bear out “Leo’s” contention that they go
right through the earth to the other side.
“Leo Lear”: “Wang” (B. 23/5/’12) is off
the track when he suggests that birds leave thick
scrub localities because they object to the perfume of
rabbit. As a matter of fact, few birds inhabit thick
scrub. Those that do are rare and shy specimens,
such as regent birds, bristle birds, pilot birds and
oof birds. Most aerial fowl prefer the fringe of
scrubs. But once a clearing is made bird life imme-
diately appears; and if “Wang” had spent his holiday
smiting down timber with his razor, the rush of
magpies, mudlarks, flycatchers, etc., to the spot,
would have caused a whirlwind like that which
dragged Elijah up to Paradise.
The Army Let Loose.
An Ancient Soldier writes:—
What arrant guff these British Socialists do ladle
out for the benefit of “the masses,” to be sure! The
other day H. M. Hynuman, M.A., the wealthy
founder of the Social Democratic Federation in
Britain, rejoiced heartily at the possibility of Eng-
lish soldiers “refusing to shoot down the sweated
and swindled workers of the United Kingdom.”
Keir H-ardie, Tom Mann, and the rest of this futile
crowd appear to spend a large part of their time,
these days, hurrying up and down the country,
openly or covertly urging the military not to pro-
tect the community against strikers, peaceful or
otherwise. And that is about the only contribution
they ever make to the alleviation of their country’s
woes. Their policy is purely negative. None of
them offers the vestige of a scheme for en-
abling the striker or potential striker to earn
enough to put a bit more food in his depleted stom-
ach. These political mountebanks are Freetraders to a
man; advocates, that is to say, of the fiscal idiocy
which has created Britain’s vast unemployed class
by removing so much of its collective steady job
to America, the European continent and elsewhere.
Hyndman and Co. don’t offer their working men
dupes a policy that will bring the latter remunerative
toil—which is the only thing on this earth
that . is going to do the vast majority of the
British “lower orders” any real good. Instead they
shout and orate and machinate and go to gaol in
the cause of a general strike. No civilised com-
munity can afford to permit a genuine, all-wool,
general strike, and an appeal to the military is in-
variably the first means adopted for stopping it.
The refusal of the soldiers to act would mean that
all laws were abrogated and that chaos had material-
ised. And when That happens the collection of taxes
ceases, and the unpaid, unfed army and police dis-
band, and everyone does what. is bad in his own
sight. Or if the army doesn’t disband—or if part of
it comes together again after disbanding-—it is
apt to be a force that means, under an ambitious
leader, to run the country for its own profit, as the
Praetorians once did in Rome, or the Janissaries in
Headache every now and then? Carry Steams’
Headache Cure—easy to carry, easy to take. Clears
Oie head in a few minutes.*
Turkey. It is a dangerous thing to teach an army
to break away from its old commanders. It may
then do one of two things—either dissolve and leave
the country to anarchy, or set up on its own account
as an oligarchy, ruling by the gun and bayonet for
its own benefit, fixing its own wages, selecting its
own leaders, and helping itself to girls and pro-
perty in a general sort of way. . Nothing but the
habit of obedience prevents it doing that now, and
Hyndman and Cos- want it to get rid of the habit
of obedience. Supposing it does! Why, then, with
the power to become rich and great should its mem-
bers step down to hustle for a crust among the
poorest of the poor ?
The Associated Chambers of Manufactures
of Australia have once more urged Prime
Minister Fisher to appoint a permanent,
non-political tariff commission, or board, and
the Inter-State Commission provided for in
the Constitution Act. As to this latter, which
Fisher said was a “constitutional power
which might be exercised sooner or later,” it
is notorious that inter-State Freetrade and
genuine Federation, as regards trade and
commerce, are only a farce so long as the
differential railway rates and port charges
now in force continue. The Commission
should, of course, have been appointed long
ago, and in saying it might be appointed
some day, Fisher is but fooling with the
laws he is sworn to administer. As for the
Permanent Tariff Board, that eminently sane
proposal should be welcomed, especially by a
party that has lived on its policy of sinking
the fiscal issue. One side of that issue, cer-
tainly, has been sunk permanently, and it
would be to the manifest advantage of Labor,
which can gain nothing by taking sides on the
matter, to hand over the ever-recurring ad-
justment of the details of a tariff to a small,
non-political body. As one deputationist
put it, a tariff stands on the same basis as
defence —it is really a weapon of industrial
defence, always there, but subject to such
increases or modifications as might prove
necessary. While Fisher is considering the
manufacturers’ requests, if he does consider
them, he would do well to ponder over one
suggestion offered him —that he should do
something to answer the imputation thrown
against his Government, that it is in favor
of the highest duties that will not protect.
Looking at Australia’s huge and ever-increas-
ing Customs revenue, that imputation ap-
pears well-founded, and if Fisher doesn’t
answer it, the electors may do so peremp-
torily next autumn.

“J.R.”: Defence-Minister Pearce an-
nounced the other day that he had written
to the British War Office people asking
them, in effect, when they had made up their
minds as to the type of flying-machine they
intended adopting, to send four of that sort
to Australia. He will not have to wait the
usual three months for a reply. The text of
the memorandum ordering the establishment
of a “Flying Corps” for Bull’s army arrived
by the last mail, and it appears that the
Bull War Office hasn’t the least idea what
type of machine it will use. It only knows
that it is going to buy 12 Henri Farman
hydro-aeroplanes and some loose floats for
the navy, and when it can make up its mind,
it is going to buy 25 aeroplanes for the mili-
tary service. Britain is lamentably behind
hand as regards aviation. The administra-
tion of its Flying Corps is a by-word. The
War Office has only £130,000 to spend on the
new science this year—a paltry sum when
compared, for instance, with the million
sterling that France proposes to lavish in
this direction during the same period. In
THE ABC OF SCIENTIFIC FARMING.
Pruning the crop.
the circumstances, it is absurd for Australia
to go to London for information on a ques-
tion in regard to which Britain is itself, ad-
mittedly, groping in the dark. A more ob-
vious procedure is for the Commonwealth to
encourage flying locally on machines which
experience has shown are fitted to Australian
conditions. There is, at least, one local fly-
ing school, aeroplane factory and skilled avia-
tor in the country to make a start with.

A childless couple, we sit here watching the fivers
sliding past;
For, all through a bit of feeble botching, our first
kid was the last;
It’s not our fault altogether, you see—and, cer-
tainly, not all mine,
For, under the law, polygamy is punished with more
than a fine!
And as for the missus—poor lass!—she dotes on
children, and always did ;
They talk about women demanding votes, but my
girl asks for a kid !
But medical science—they call it that—says, “Quite
impossible now!”
So, how can we manage a five-pound brat? Will
somebody tell me how?
There’s Smith and his missus with fourteen kids, and
all of ’em born out here!
There’s dozens I know —and some of ’em Yids—that
make their fiver a year!
Ay! some of the lucky ones make it ten with for-
tunate, thumping twins—
And wasn’t it one of the tramway men got fifteen
quid—for his sins?
And, look at the immigrants! Here they come, by
scores, with their new, young wives—
But what I always consider rum is that Baby never
arrives,
As he used to do, by the same good ship that carried
his parents out—
Not much! He comes this end o’ the trip—they
know what they’re about!
It’s up to Fisher to square this game, and level it
up for those
Who haven’t a baby to bless their name (unless it’s
under the rose!).
There’s all these fivers a-sliding past the married, but
childless, pair—
Two obsolete Brednoughts, hard and fast on the rock
of Sheer Despair!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
PEELING McGUIRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The circus came to Boolga town,
And reared its tents with stirring din,
Then set the famous talking clown
To drag the gaping natives in
The wondrous Riding Monks to see,
Likewise the grand menagerie.
In with the rest went Con McGuire.
He saw the lion in its cage,
He saw the ape upon a wire,
Beheld the Bengal tiger’s rage,
And then he stood, amazed and wary,
Beside the awkward dromedary.
McGuire looked close. His face was sad.
“ ’Tis sore afflicted,” murmured Mae.
He thought the swellings very bad
That showed upon the creature’s back.
“Sure, them’s terrific boils,” said Con.
“Why don’t yez put a poultice on?”

Australia’s most ancient milionaire is Ban-
Smith, of Adelaide, who is 88 years old. His
partner, Thomas Elder, died worth £900,000,
though he had previously distributed a heap
of money among South Australian public
bodies. Barr Smith has already given awav
a traceable £30,000, and as all his sons anil
daughters are wealthy, there may be more to
come later on.

taking Tyson’s old place as a sort of squat-
ter king. He has also the regal art of
getting solid with the press. Adelaide
Register (18/6/T2) gives these things as
facts: (1) Were it not that Mr. Kidman is
sending 400 cattle each week to Adelaide,
beef would be at famine prices.” (2)
“The ‘Cattle King’ desires the consumers to
be inconvenienced as little as possible bv the
extraordinary position which prevails,”’ and
has told his agents “not to endeavor to get
the last penny out of the butchers.” (3)
Tt was a common occurrence at the sale on
Monday for Mr. W. Will, Mr. Kidman’s
manager, to say ‘Knock them down,’ even
when more money was offering.” A regal
Crown will hardly cover this. One large-
sized saintly halo for Ividman, please!

Britain is full of small scuffling persons
of title these days—financially harried citi-
zens ever ready for a personal wrangle with
a dustman or other humble toiler on the sub-
ject of the latter's charges. The proud,
aloof, money-spurning aristocrat is going;
and in his place is to be found the worried
lordling with judgment summonses where
his ancestors used to carry gold pieces. To
cite a case in point, Baron Haldon (43), of
Beckenham, the other day, contested, in
Croydon County Court (here the newspaper
report begins) :
A claim by Messrs. Williamson, grocers, of South
Norwood, for £4 4s. Bd. for goods supplied.
Lord Haldon asserted that the goods were not de-
livered on the dates mentioned in the account, and,
after the evidence of two servants on this point, he
declared, “1 think that dishes it.”
r V hi J? evidence was being given for the plaintiffs.
Lord Haldon asked why this debt was not included
i'olT arrested for debt in January,
Judge Harington said there was not the least
ground for the suggestion that the account was
faked, and gave judgment for the plaintiffs.
Lord Haldon: “1 won t pay it. I am going to
America next week.”
His Honor: “Well, I think it is a verv good
thing.”
Imagine an old-time Baron threatening to
expatriate himself in connection with a small
grocery transaction, and being cheered on
his way by an assurance from the Bench
that it would be a good thing if he did!

William Hall Jones, the very white man
who has just ceased to represent Maoriland
in the Cold Country, and who, to the regret
of many, didn’t jump lou Ward’s claim for
the Maoriland Premiership when he had the
show, lias a political failing of the first mag-
nitude. ( Modesty is what is wrong with him.
When Seddon sent him a wire offering him
a portfolio, he wrote out a message declin-
ing the job on the ground that he didn’t
think he was capable. But some of his con-
stituents got on the scene in time, and held
down Jones, and tore up the message, and
declared that, the city of Timaru wasn't
going to lose its chance of being represented
in the Cabinet. Jones might think himself in-
competent, or sick, or dead or any old thing
he liked, but he was going to take that port-
folio. And Jones consented in a smothered
voice from under a heap of supporters.
“Rooney”: Died in Sydney re-
cently, Dave Kiss, an old Monaro
bushman. Such claim to immor-
tality as he possessed rested broad
based upon the hardness of his
skull. It was his pleasure to
amaze the simple bucolics of his
district by breaking into fragments
with his iron cranium a cheese
placed in a bag. Occasionally a
local wit would substitute for the
cheese a grindstone. It made no
difference to David. The only suf-
ferer was the owner of the grind-
stone. There was one circumstance
that Dave’s backers always feared
would ultimately beat their cham-
pion if it could be brought to bear
on him, viz., the Liberal party’s
luck in having C. G. Wade as a
leader. However, in the nature of
things, no one could get it into a
bag, and so the old man remained
undefeated to the end.

Until a few days ago the Vic. Eight-
Hours’ Pioneers’ Association numbered three
members. One of them, James Dalgaty, died
in a suburban train, and now there are two.
The old gentleman who has gone had been
the pioneers’ standard-bearer in the Eight-
Hours’ procession for many years. George
Launder and ■ John- McKay are now the
whole association.
Premier Watt, of Victoria, was lately
fined for refusing to have his child vaccin-
ated. This was his third falling in. Certain
fragments of an old clan of conscientious
objectors still linger around Melbourne, ex-
Federal Minister McCay being one and
Watt another. William believes in majority
rule till the rule is against him. Then he
believes in being the majority.

The appointment of W. H. Ifould, an
energetic young S’Australian, to be Sydney’s
Public Librarian, in succession to historian
Bladen, recalls that John Fairfax, practi-
cally the founder of the S. M. Herald,
served awhile in the same place as book-
duster and distributor. Young John, having
struck financial trouble in England and emi-
grated in consequence, got a job at the
library; but as there wasn’t much to do
there in the ’3o’s, he used to drop in at the
bi-weekly Herald office and look around for
a few extra coppers. Evidently he found
them, because he and Kemp, the paper’s re-
porter, bought the whole turn-out before
long, and made it a daily.

came, he says, through the Arehbish. of

Archbishop Redwood, of Maoriland.
John Logan Campbell, one of the best-
lmown of Maoriland’s settlers, capitulated to
the advance of Time a few days ago at
Auckland, where he had resided for the past
70-odd years. He was born at Edinburgh
in 1817, .and educated in that rocky town’s
University for the medical trade. In the
long, long ago, about the time when the
Homani Empire began to look shaky, he came
out to Sydney as surgeon on the Palmyra. A
very little while later (the Roman Empire
was getting shakier all the time) he moved
on to Maoriland. and there proceeded to haul
together a great fortune. Nearly all legiti-
mate trades, jobs, schemes or speculations
were alike to “Jock” Campbell, so long as the
siller looked handy to the surface. Yet he
was no mean grasper. He had a big heart,
and the arm with which he passed out money
was almost as long as the one with which,
lie dragged it in. He was at one time a
somewhat prominent figure in politics, and
wrote a book, and was knighted by little
Ueorge V.
Died in Sydney, a few days back, James
Baxter, a big contractor, known well in four
States. He made one fortune, years ago. in
Melbourne, and another lately in Sydney:
but the former pile was reputed to have all
been drowned at the bottom of the Adelaide
Outer Harbor, which he undertook, as con-
tractors do, to dredge for a lump sum. As
it happened, the mud was bigger than the
sum.

The S. M. Herald has had an interview
with a Colonel Graham, who brings baek
what the paper headlines “A Lesson from
Java.” ‘‘There are,” it is said, “30,000 vil-
lages in Java, which, though l-20th the size
of Australia, has a population of 32,000,000.”
That is the fact, buc the Herald forgot to
mention the lesson. Probably it was shy
about saying that, 110 years or so ago, the
Dutch rulers at Java, in order to prevent
the aggregation of private estates and the
growth of a horde of loafing landlords, shut
down absolutely on private property in land,
and that since then the population (mostly
rural) has grown ninefold —from about
3,500,000 to about 32,000,000.
Labor’s three in the next Senate election
in Victoria will be Russell, McKissock and
Barnes. Russell has already had six years
in the House, and McKissock used to sit for
Ballarat West in the State Assembly, so
Barnes is the only one with no sins to
answer for. The new man is one of the
products of the A.W.U. —successively a
shearer with a plain member’s ticket, then
a shed rep., afterwards an organiser, and
now secretary for Victoria and Riverina* with
headquarters at Ballarat. He was largely
responsible for the surprise that Deakin got
when, at last election, he found himself only
400 ahead of his almost-unknown opponent,
who was another Russell. This time Alta-
ble’s opponent iu Ballarat won’t be unknown
—it will probably be either McGrath, now
M.L.A. for Grenville, who stands solid with
the Ballarat mining crowd, or Hargreaves,
an eloquent Wesley College young man who
is the Labor party’s political organis-
ing. Whichever it is, Deakin will have to
do all he knows to keep that 400.
“J.R.”: The Sydney Telegraph wrote
lugubriously, the other day:—
It is probably the Caucus system which prevents
the development in the Labor party of men of the
first class in politics. A leader who has to be
elected and is frequently a compromise; a leader
who has to accept a policy, and is allowed none but
his own voice in formulating- it; a leader who man-
ages Parliament on instructions received before he
enters it, and does not guide his course by the con-
ditions as he finds them, is denied all right of initia-
tive, and all exercise of original power.
Has there ever been such a politician as this
writer desires? Certainly not in Aus-
tralia, and probably nowhere else unless yon
fall back on such an example as Francia of
Paraguay—the man who was called “The
Supreme” when he was alive because people
feared to mention his name, and was alluded
to as “The Defunct” when he was dead,
because people feared to speak of
his ghost. The two Pitts, father and
son, who were supposed to dominate British
politics in the 18th century, might be sup-
posed to fill the bill, but recent historical
research has put them out of the running.
William Pitt the younger is popularly be-
lieved to have crushed the French Revolu-
tion and the first Empire. The researches
of Dr. Rose, as disclosed in his three
recent books on the younger Pitt, dis-
prove these fallacies. When he came into
office at the end of the disastrous American
War he saw clearly that reform of Parlia-
ment was necessary, and that something must
be done for Ireland, but Geo. 111. and the
aristocracy, who regarded the old corrupt
House of Commons as necessary to maintain
their supremacy, would not have reform at
any price, and Pitt had to choose between
Ins policy and office. He chose office, like
any modern. He was fatuously ignorant of
the condition of the people he was supposed
to_ govern during the 23 years of his Pre-
miership. He carried on the war against
revolutionary France against his own better
judgment to please the King, without whose
support he could not have stayed in office,
and he carried it on by means of treaties of
subsidy—paying good British money for the
maintenance of continental armies. On one
occasion he just missed paying a million for
the equipment and maiutenace of a Prussian
army that was destined, not to fight the
Irench, but to occupy King George’s king-
dom of Hanover. That was the so-called
Heaven-born Minister” as he appeared in
real life. How many British Ministers since
i me h ave made a better showing? When
the_ fight between Disraeli and Gladstone was
at its height, two M’s.P., one of each party,
happened to be discussing their The
Tory said : “We are better off for a leader
than you. Ours is an unprincipled scoundrel;
but yours is a dangerous lunatic.”

THE UNKIND ARGUMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
?l r .' said: “Those who accuse us of
robbing God have vessels consecrated to the sanctu-
the nw sideboards, and meat dedicated to
the altar stocks their larders.
The Westminster Gazette suggests that these
arguments might now be dropped.—1 Cablegram.
When troglodvtic warrior Wo
oof forth to meet the mastodon
Or argue with his fellow-trog,
He either dropped his hairy foe
Or had to drop the argument.
And if he prayed when he was spent
Before his dinner bit the snow:
“Please drop thy club, O fellow-trog;
Shed thy fierce tusks, O mastodon!”
His prayer went up—but where went Wo?
When kings from monkeries took toll
With impious and unsparing hand,
The holy shavelings cursed them well
By bell and candle, book and bowl—
To hottest flames of deepest hell,
By book and candle, howl and bell,
Consigned each sacrilegious soul;
And prayers and logic used as well;
But all the arguments the band
Of robbers could not quell they stole.
And so, to-day, as ’twas in Greece
And Britain in the days of gore,
’Tis judged in execrable taste
To sauce the ganders as the geese ;
To fail to let old evils rest,
And learn that what is now is best.
“Why this disturbance of the peace?
The Past, dear friends, should be effaced
All exhumations we deplore,
The argument should forthwith cease!”
TASMANIA.
JOHN DRAYMAN.

At that time there were four private concerns selling
electric current in different parts of Sydney, and,
after a while, arrangements were made to end the
unprofitable competition and buy them out. The
first deal, made with the Empire Co., served as a
basis for the lot. The council, having* no use for
the private cos. plant, didn’t buy it, but offered a
nxed pnce for the business. The customers were
s4erre<4 4o council, and all revenue in excess
of Id. per unit is paid to the cos. in extinction of
the capital debt. The Empire Co. has been paid
off; progress with the other three has been slower
chiefly because interest was payable to them on the
unpaid principal. The following shows the amounts
1^1907^— an ° US dat6S ' thC flrSt P urchase was made
„ . . , 1908. 1910. 1911.

.Strand (ditto and interest).. 40,103 £32,248 £23 162
Imperial „ „ .. 17,074 17,422 14,329
Oxford-st. „ .. 10,086 7,286 5,202
The nearer the debts get to extinction less and less
or the payments will be called for to square the in-
terest bill, and more and more will thus be avail-
able to wipe out principal; so the tail of the debt
will go out much faster than the head and neck of
it did.
But though the table shows that, besides providing
enough to extinguish the goodwill, the undertaking
has accumulated reserves of £80,959, much of this
fund is of a very doubtful character. In fact, since
£91,420 is at the credit of depreciation and re-
newal funds, it is doubtful whether there are yet
any true reserves at all, since those funds are no
doubt merely a set-off against actual depreciation.
Even so, however, progress such as that shown in the
past four years will soon put the undertaking right,
provided no cuts are made at the revenue. The
capital expenditure as at December 31 last was
£941,504, so the past year’s profit was equal to
over p.c., after paying all interest charges,
taking the assets all round, probably 4 p.c. is
enough for depreciation, and this would allow 1 p.c.
for sinking fund on the debenture debt and nearly 2
p.c. for contingencies. That is a comfortable posi-
tion. But it is the first year in which the position
really has been comfortable.

Though this big overdraft has been put into plant,
etc., just the same as if it were debenture capital,
-no contribution to the sinking fund has been made
in respect to it. So the undertaking can apparently
dodge the sinking fund by the simple process of
raising money by casual overdraft instead of by the
more business-like debenture. This is a matter that
wants seeing to. The capital ought to carry sinking
fund no matter what brand of IO U it is raised
upon.

The New York Life Insurance Co. —
This was one of the three American concerns which
pursued the Australian at great expense a few years
ago, and begged him to take a policy about twice
as big as he could afford. It didn’t riot so extrava-
gantly as the Equitable, yet it got well over
£3,000,000 of Australian business, and it still has
about £2,250,000 in its books —very nearly half as
much as the plodding Widows’ Fund had when it
threw in its lot with the M.L. and Citizens’ after just
upon 40 years of toil. Unfortunately, the big
Yankee three didn’t confine their jamboree to Austra-
lia. In their competition to see more snakes than
all the rest of the Earth, they overdid things at last
m their own country; and then the Legislature took
a hand, and put the offices on an allowance. Among
other things they were restricted as to the volume
°* ,^. ew business they could write; and as new-business
getting in Australia was and promised always to be
expensive, owing to the good tendency of the Aus-
tralian to patronise the Australian office, the big
three almost deserted the southern field. A couple
of them still accept new business ; but thev probably
find it a hard row to hoe, and they are 'not likely
to find it any easier as endowment and other
policies fall in and small amounts are realised com-
pared with the extravagant estimates on which the
policies were mostly secured. Yet the policies
are honestly worth more now than they were
when the estimates were made; for greater
economy followed the clean-up, and in’ life
insurance economies mean bonuses. In the
days of boom and boast the expense rate was
usually somewhere around 25 p.c. of the income;
last year, on a total income of £23,152,124, the
expenses amounted to £2,508,823, or £lO 16s. 9d.
P j? C -i'T?o *?«*** . m °de r ate figure, seeing that
£1,522,726 of the income was from new premiums.
Charging renewal premiums with 7 p.c. and annuitv
purchase money with 3 p.c., and assuming that the
residue of the exes, went in securing new business, the
latter would cost 94 p.c. of the first year premiums,
rhere are Australian offices which are doing better
in this respect, but only two or three of them. The
prodigal has settled down to a steadv life at the
plough all right.
The position of these Yankee offices never was as
bad as it was made out to be for wire-pulling l pur-
poses. The agitation, of course, knocked business
endways for a time, and the income actually shrank
for a year or two ; but it is growing steadily again,
inis shows the New York Life’s movement in the last
five years:—

This co., run by men who are full of push and pull,
is probably doing much better than the published
Profit and Loss account suggests. Take this com-
parison of the balance-sheet items for a guide:—
Liabilities.
N0v.,’06. May,’ll. Nov.,’ll. May,’l2.

Debts .. .. 9,597 9,547 41,378 18,569
Country mills 3,825 12,035 13,575 18,535
Pyrm o n t
wharves, mill,
etc 15,900 6,000 5,000 5,000
Vessels .. 18,050 25,066 28,502 33.402
Book debts, etc. 25,517 42,767 50,074 49,399
Cash, etc. .. 1,010 9,197 25,161 14,038
Stocks.. .. 9,502 23,686 28,593 33,906
In 1906 the wharf and mill property and plant at
Pyrmont were valued at £15,900. It is highly im-
probable that any of this property was sold; on the
contrary, additions have certainly been made. Yet
five years afterwards the item stood at £5OOO, so
it looks as though not only had all additions been
made out of profits, but that £10,900 had also been
written off, ostensibly against depreciation. In
the past half-year, according to the directors’ report,
“additional wharfage accommodation has been pro-
vided at Pyrmont, at a cost of £1600” ; yet the Pyr-
mont assets stand in the books at the same figure
as they did before this money was spent. In other
words, this outlay was provided for out of undisclosed
profit. It is impossible to follow other items in the
same way. They may or they may not have been
dealt with similarly, though the chances are that they
have been. In addition to the cash mentioned in the
last table above, there is the insurance and reserve
fund, which now amounts to £17,000, and is in-
vested outside the business altogether: £1250 is in
coastal-shipping cos.’ shares (and that is probably a
very good investment indirectly, even if otherwise it
is only second-class), and the balance is in City
Council debentures, or on deposit. Most cos. depend
largely on their reserves for the extension of their
business; but this one puts practically all its little
lot into gilt-edged securities. Hence a 10 p.c. paid
by this co. is worth a trifle more than 10 p.c. paid
by the ordinary trading co.
On the other hand, the co. isn’t all precaution and
no risk. In the first place, the Allen Taylor mills
aren’t sausage mills. Good times or bad, boom or
slump, the sausage mill -rinds on, for the people
must eat. But they neei. generally speaking,
build new railways or offices or houses. Of late this
country has been having the biggest boom in building
and construction on record, and Allen Taylor and Co.
have stood very well with the people who have had
the money to spend. Later on there is going to be a
calm after the storm, and if timber mills, tramways,
etc., are not actually thrown idle, thev are not
going to get the prices that have ruled lately. But
whether mills, timber tramways, etc., are used or
not, and whether they earn much or little, they will
go on depreciating; hence the wisdom of writing
assets down hard while profits are plentiful. By
adopting that policy the co. may be able to maintain
its dividend when the present riot is over; but unless
the past history of Australian concerns that supply
building materials is a liar, it couldn’t do it other-
wise.

1912.. 4561 7„ ~ 3165 1396 21,869
The half-year’s profits were better than that, accord-
mg to the accounts; but tne co. had to pay land tax
for two years. It cheerfully confesses that it tried
to dodge the tax, on the plea that building societies
were exempted, but it discovered that “the exemp-
tion only applies to certain classes of building socie-
ties.” If it didn't it would be a scandal. Or,
rather, if the law called this a building society, that
would be a scandal. This is a co. whose job is to
make dividends for its shareholders by the process of
buying land wholesale and selling it retail. It isn’t
in any sense co-operative: it isn’t a mutual help
society, or even the beginnings of one. Besides city
and suburban land, it owns considerable country
property; in fact, country lands are no doubt by
far the biggest property asset. Some of these have
been cut up and sold; others are being cultivated by
the co. itself. Having so large a proportion of its
capital sunk in country lands, the co. has not been
able to go very far into the suburban subdivision
business and reap the rich harvest that has been
bagged by a few other concerns during the last four
or five years. When the boom started it was loaded
up with the wrong sort of goods; and, as those
goods had been very unprofitable for many years, the
shares were only marketable at a heavy discount,
and that probably stopped the co. from getting in
more capital. Even when the market said that the
shares weren't worth half their face value, the man-
agement took no steps to re-appraise the properties
and, if necessary, write off capital. Since then the
assets must have increased in value; but whether
they are worth 10s. or 15s. or 20s. in the £ now
this paper doesn’t know. The figures scarcely pro-
fessed to be serious for many years, and whether they
are to be taken as serious now the directors don’t
say.
This next list shows how the chief items have
moved: —
LIABILITIES.

Thus in 1900 the co.’s properties stood at £164,117
and to-day they stand at £131,199. Meantime
advances—which include unpaid balances on property
sold—have moved on from £42,003 to £110,918.
This looks as though the co. has sold a good many
properties; but if it has sold the old ones it has
accumulated a lot of new ones, seeing that the total
now held is only £32,918 less than it was in 1900.
This big property holding is the weak point; when
a land-subdivision co. hangs on to so much in these
recent times of high prices it looks as though the
properties stand at too high a figure in the books, and
suggests that the co. is afraid to face the loss that a
sale would show. Current business may be quite
good and profitable; but much confidence can never
be placed in the figures until it is announced that
the assets have been revalued. And from the in-
vestors’ point of view that is all the more necessary
because the co. owes £128,427, which means that the
co. will have to pay £128,427, no matter how
much or how little may be left for shareholders.

The reserves are wretchedly small for a concern
with a turnover of £1,349,403 for the past year
and a capital of only £17,206; but the society is
trying to improve the position by inviting the sup-
pliers to take some of their bonus in shares instead of
cash, and if they don’t do it, this paper trusts that
their boss cow Strawberry will dry off, and that Spot
will kick the milk-bucket over every morning for a
month. Apart from the fact that the co. offers as
safe a 6 p.c. as almost any trading concern in the
State, the supplier has this to remember —every £1
he leaves in the business is £1 added to the’ fund
which guarantees payment for the goods he supplies
to the co. He would in ordinary circumstances be
paid in any case; but it is obvious that there must
be risks of bad debts in a business with about
£125,000 worth of trade a month, most of this
trade being done with small shopkeepers who want
some credit. And if the suppliers don’t provide the
increasing capital necessary in an increasing busi-
ness, nobody else can do it without destroying the
co-operative _ character of the concern. The only
other plan is for the society to hang on to some
of the profits by reducing the bonus, and that would
be less to the advantage of the suppliers, inasmuch
as then they wouldn’t get any 6 p.c. dividend on the
money withheld. Apart from the disproportion of
capital and reserves to the turnover, the position is
good. The co. owes £97,709, practically all to con-
signors, and it has £44,367 in cash and £80,211
in other assets, apart from store plant and office
fittings. The bulk of this £80,211 is represented
by sundry debtors, and the total amounts to little
more than a fortnight’s turnover, for which reason
the outstanding debts, though they look large, are
really exceedingly moderate.

One or two entries in that table are subject to quali-
fication. As remarked above, the business was taken
over as from October, 1910. No accounts were, how-
ever, issued for the following six months; but in
June, 1911, a bonus or dividend at the rate of 10 p.c.
p.a. was paid, and £539 was also passed to the
credit of Profit and Loss. Assuming that the bonus
amounted to £3408, then the profit for the period
was £3947; but whether anything was written off
is not clear. The deficit with which proceedings
started was made up of goodwill £18,020, and pre-
liminary exes., £lO4B. In addition to these paper
assets, leaseholds stood in the books on October 31
at £4086. This latter amount is. of course, being
written off out of revenue. The capital is £75,000 in
£1 shares fully paid. This and the reserves as at
April 30 (before the half-year’s appropriations),
were represented by the following assets: —

There being no freehold or fixed property, the profits
should be large. A co. with any considerable propor-
tion of its capital invested in more or less gilt-edged
freehold might well be content with less. With most
of the capital in stock, plant and book debts, almost
everything depends on the stock-taking, and in this
matter one has to trust the management entirely.
amount written off “plant, machinery and type” is
10 p.c. p.a., and it is extremely doubtful whether it
is enough; still the directors say that, on a valua-
tion by experts, the plant was appraised at £3OOO
above book value. Six months ago the 10 p.c. div.
was here referred to as excessive, and, in view of the
struggle ahead, the dash into the excess wasn’t viewed
as promising. Apparently the directors have thought
better of it themselves, and they now pay at the rate
of only 4 p.c., making 7 p.c. for the year —in view
of “the advisability of building up reserves and fixing
a rate which can be readily maintained.” The 10
p.c. was an indiscretion, and smelt of share-booming ;
and the directors will have to do a bit more yet
to live it down. Meantime, one or two things may
as well be noted: (1) IV. E. Smith is no longer in
the business, in fact, he is dead. (2) The co. is
dependent on a landlord for its chief premises, and
the rent charge is equivalent to a first mortgage on
trading profits. (3) The co. is in keen competition
with houses which haven’t got a heavy loading of
goodwill capital to share the profits with. (4) The
co. proposes to borrow £15.000 on 6 p.c. debentures
for factory-building purposes, and that, though it
may increase the profits in good times, may be_ a
drag in poor ones. In any case, it means the creation
of another creditor entitled to a first bite at profits.
(5) Business has been booming in Sydney, largely
because of the riot of public borrowing, which must
end very soon. If the ordinary trading concern aver-
ages two-thirds of its recent profits, it will do very
well in normal times.
* * * *
Australian Marine Fibres, Ltd. —This
co. was formed early last year to acquire certain
leases in Spencer’s Gulf (S.A.), and to work them
for the marine fibre which is mixed with the sand
at the sea-bottom. The co. has a nominal capital
of £165,000, in 660,000 ss. shares, of which
U 40.000 were offered to the public and 310,000 were
issued in payment for the leases; but of the 240,000
only 122,144 were applied for, and another 110,000
are reserved. Of the paid capital of £97,691 no
less than £77,841 thus represents leases —-a very
heavy loading, seeing that the vendors of the leases
had not made a market for the product. The present
co. has bought a dredge, and the directors say that
it has begun its work all right. Now, as the
directors say, the thing is “to establish a market.”
They add that “this may take a little time,” though
they also affirm that “right throughout the year offers
of satisfactory prices have been coming to hand.”
Apparently the directors are not quite so sure about
immediate profits as they were when the prospectus
was issued; anyway, that document, to which their
names were attached, said: “Contracts can be made
at satisfactory prices as soon as the co. is ready to
give regular delivery.” If the co. can average the
estimated £lB 13s. 4d. per ton (2d. per lb.) for the
fibre, and if it can make the estimated profit of Jd.
per lb., and if three dredges can raise the estimated
20,000 tons a year, then it is plain enough that
the profit will be £140,000 a year. Also, if there
are the estimated 1,372,000 tons in the areas, the
profits will go on for 65 years, provided fresh licenses
can be got on the same terms at the end of the
current 21 years. But for the present (1) the co.
has only one dredge; (2) it has only £2873 of
cash left, though it has also £10,344 of uncalled
capital; (3) it apparently hasn’t sold so much as
lib. of fibre for 2d., and it merely, mentions “satis-
factory prices” in a vague way; and (4) costs have
yet to be determined. For the present, therefore,
profits are entirely in the clouds, though this paper
hopes that they will presently leave that uncertain
perch and settle in the shareholders’ pockets. Prob-
ably Australia wouldn’t get very much out of the
business for a while, but it would, at any rate, be
the start of a new industry, built up on one of this
country’s very numerous waste products.
* * * *
This is what happened with regard to
the much-discussed cash consideration paid by the
A.M.P. to Sir H. S. King, one of the society’s direc-
tors in London. For very many years—over a
quarter of a century—King had been the society’s
agent in Britain. When Chairman Meeks arrived in
London, three or four years ago, and announced that
he had come to open a branch A.M.P. office, King
said that, by English law and practice, he was en-
titkd to compensation. The society’s officers said
they didn’t think so, and King replied that if legal
arbitrators said he wasn’t, he would be quite satis-
fied. A settlement on those lines was at once agreed
to, with this provision—that the compensation, if
any, must be calculated as from the date of the
interview. That matter disposed of, King*, as the man
most conversant with the society’s business in Eng-
land, was presently asked to join the Board, and con-
sented. The arbitrators later on reported to the
society that King was entitled to compensation—or,
rather, to a lump sum in lieu of commission on
expected renewals—and the matter was then referred
to an actuary to discover the amount payable. The
negotiations were concluded before King was invited
to join the Board; and the fact that King had been
on the Board for about a couple of years before the
payment was actually made was due entirelv to the
slow progress oi the arbitrators and the actuary.
The A.M.P. soon found out also that, according to
English practice, other agents besides King had a
right to commissions on renewals, unless barred by
special arrangement. The society offered a fairlv
high commission for new business, but, following the
Australian practice, declined to pay anything on
renewals. The English insurance brokers’ reply was
a boycott of the A.M.P., which was only ended when
the society retreated, and fell in line with English
omces. That meant a somewhat reduced commis-
sion on new business, along with a pretty stiff re-
newal commission. The two together amount to con-
siderably more than the average cost in Australia —
another reason, of course, why Australian policy-
holders should be given the benefit of separate ac-
counts for the English business, so that thev mav
decide for themselves whether it is worth continuing
or not. If it costs more to get and keep the English
business, and if the English policy-holder doesn’t
live longer when he is captured (and it is prettv
certain that he doesn t), then obviously he can only
get the same bonuses as Australians at the expense
of Australians.

chamber of Commerce the other day on the ques-

professing anxiety as to how the maturing loans were
to be met. The best way was to increase popula-
tion, _ open up the country by railway development
and increase the exports and imports. The Govern-
ment had set itself an ambitious railway policv, and
nothing short of lack of supplies would curtail it.”

John Logan Campbell, the ‘father of
Auckland,” who died on Saturday, was the last sur-
vivor of the committee appointed at a small pre-
liminary meeting to draw up the prospectus of the
Bank of New Zealand. That was 51 years ago, and
even then John Logan was a citizen of credit and re-
nown. It was, by the way, the success of the young N.Z.
Insurance Co. that suggested the bank, and Campbell
and the other committee-men had the use of a corner
of the insurance co.’s premises in which to hammer
the prospectus together. ,

Golden Horseshoe’s reduced circumstances
were attributed at the annual meeting to causes com-
pletely outside the control of either the Board or the
officials at the mine. According to the chairman the
Door results have been mainly due to the continued
poorness of ore sent to the mill, coupled with exten-
sive slides, shortage of suitable miners, increased
cost of mining supplies, etc. All these setbacks may
have contributed towards the co.’s misfortunes; but
had it followed the example of its neighbor, the
Great Boulder, and been content with running on a
grade it could maintain, instead of shipping away
rich ore to the smelters, in order to make the mine
look more attractive on the market, it would be in
a much better position to-day. It is simply the old
story of the bad workman finding fault with his
tools. As usual, the directors gush over the future
and express astonishment in a veiled sort of way
that the shares are not a good deal higher; but on
the basis of 1911 results the divisible profit in sight,
with the debenture debt taken off, runs out at only
about 7s. or Ss. a share.
* * * *
As every little helps nowadays, Great
Boulder (W.A.) has arranged with the Government
Water Supply for a subsidiary service at a cut rate
for sluicing and re-treating, for the second or third
time, an accumulation of 300,000 to 400,000 tons of
old tailings. Recent work in the lower levels of the
Ijoulder has shown the shoots to be shorter and less
permanent than formerly. Also, graphite is becom-
ing more plentiful as depth increases, and where
there is more graphite in the mine there is less
gold ‘ *
Lake View and Oroya Exploration (W.A.)
has resolved to be up and doing. A Holman double-
head No. 1 stamp, with a capacity of 4000 tons a
month, has been ordered for the Queen of the Hills
at Meekatharra, and an amalgamation between the
Mountain Queen, in which it has a share, and the
Transvaal, which it owns, is being arranged. Why
the Mountain Queen wants to have anything to do
with the Transvaal is not clear. The Queen has a
big, low-grade ore body which is fairly amenable to
treatment by ordinary means, whereas the Transvaal,
which is many miles distant, is refractory enough for
anything. Two separate and entirely different methods
of treatment will be required, and what little the
Queen makes out of ore treatment for a while will
probably be swallowed up by the Transvaal. It
might be difficult to raise money to equip the Trans-
vaal just at present, and the Lake View and Explora-
tion folk probably think that, to increase the capital
of the Mountain Queen, and hitch the two together,
is as good a way out of their difficulty as any.

Golden Butterfly is fluttering along at its
Kookynie (W.A.) property, and is confident of smit-
ing its detractors hip and thigh when the mill starts
When the plant does get going the management will
probably find that it has made a few miscalculations,
especially as to costs.

In all probability, the Northern Mines at
Lawlers (W.A.) will never be worked again. When
known as the East Murchison United it produced
£600,000, and paid about one-tenth of that amount
in dividends. It then experienced what a local paper
terms “a period of unrealised expectations,” and sub-
sequently it reconstructed and under its present name
produced a lot of gold. Low recoveries and high
costs, however, drove it on the rocks, where it lies
in a critical position. „
Gold dividends amounting to £16,719 were
paid in the West during May, making the total for
the expired five months of this year £306,485.
* * * *
Last year, 59,373 tons of gold ore were
treated by the State batteries in the West for 56,265
fine oz. Working expenditure, including additions,
totalled £7490 in excess of revenue. The total value
of gold and tin recovered since the inception of the
system is £3,733,939, and the expenditure exceeds
this amount by £28,870. # #
For the first five months of this year
Charters Towers produced £168,830 from 61,411
tons. The most important producers were Brilliant
Extended, £27,974 from 15,930 tons; Mills United
£2l 940 from 8822 tons; New B. Freehold, £21,253
from’ 7685 tons. The best development during the
period has been at 2540 ft. in the Brilliant Deeps, a
crushing from the leading stope over this
level averaging £8 2s. 6d. from 168 ton®, with
gold contained in residues to come. No. 15 level in
Mills United is practically the same depth, but at the
moment the reef isn’t payable. Brilliant Extended,
a close neighbor, is sinking deeper, and at the bottom
of the shaft the two sections of the reef have con-
verged; but prospects are poor. Speculation m
Papuan Brilliant and Victoria has been active, be-
cause the Moonstone reef has improved at both ends
of No 2 level. These workings are shallow, and the
dip of the reef very flat. The Queen Cross has tim-
bered its prospecting shaft on the Moonstone line, and
should bottom on the reef in another 30ft. bo.
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, has put
through 168 tons for £1364. Still the share market
isn’t jubilant, for ore reserves don’t justify a market
value of £lOO,OOO. Certainly the New Brilliant
Freehold, after a struggle for 20 years with poverty,
gave its shareholders more than £lOO,OOO in divi-
dends ; but then the Freehold had three splendid levels
on the Day Dawn coming in from Mills United,
though not one of them was so good over a length
of 200 ft. as the Deeps No. 4 level at 2j4oft. Mills
United is now driving a level (No. 15) at 2540 ft.,
but its prospects are not bright, nor is ex^ e ?:
sion of No. 14 east into the New Brilliant Freehold
showing particularly good prospects. Still both the
Day Dawn and Brilliant reefs are very capricious, and
the man who buys when the stone looks white and
poor often scores’better than the punter who gets m
on top of a boom. # #
Th© Trafalgar lease, of 35 acres, at the
Springs, 50 miles west of Cloncurry (Queensland),
which was recently purchased by the Mount Elliott,
is a practically undeveloped property on a lode trace-
able on the surface for four miles. Two shallow
shafts have been sunk about 300 ft. apart; one shows
four to five feet of ore in the bottom, said to average
20 p.c. copper. From the other a parcel of 60 tons
—picked, of course —brought over £7OO. The ore is
said to carry 3dwt. gold and 6oz. silver per ton, but
these metal contents cannot be relied upon. Ihe
copper ore is mostly sulphide.
When is a mine “developed”? The ques-
tion has arisen over Mount Oxide. Last week’s Wild
Cat referred to the change of the Option Syndicate s
plans aid ascribed it to the undeveloped character
of the mine. “Undeveloped” is, of course, a relative
term A mine with a pot-hole or two might fairly
be called developed if £lOOO were asked for it
whereas it might reasonably be called undeveloped
if £lOO 000 were asked for it. In the Mount Oxide
case there has been 280 ft. of sinking, and some
driving, winzing and pot-holing, and 12,000 tons of
rich ore is showing, and 100,000 tons more may be
fairly assumed. The mine is developed so far; but
it isn’t developed to the degree necessary for plan-
ning reduction works. If it had been there would
have been no need for the co. which the Option Syn-
dicate is now forming in lieu of the one previously
nlanned. Gerard Williams, who has “done ’ most
of the Australian mining fields for the London Times,
has it is announced, been appointed general manager
of Oxide —whether on the strength of his press pull
or of his copper-mining experience has not been
mentioned. By the way, what is his copper-mining
experience ?
A Melbourne syndicate has been formed
to test the lodes in the Tartana leases by diamond
drill. The leases are on the road to O.li. about
15 miles from Mungana. Capital is £3OOO, in 300
shares of £lO each, which is considered sufficient to
pay expenses for 2000 ft. of boring. Area of leases is
about 100 acres. #
Mount Morgan pays a shilling dividend
in July, which will raise the total from this won-
derful mine to £8,033,000. For six months to May
31 the returns ran:—

For the first four months copper was valued at £5O
and for the last two months at £6O. The average
selling price was much higher, but Mount Morgan
prefers to value copper at a moderate figure, m order
to give an approximate idea of the net return from
bullion shipped to the refinery in N.S.W. The treat-
ment of sulnhide ore from the open cut is about to
undergo a change. Crushing, roasting, leaching and
chlorination will be tried, for the ore is not so well
adapted to the simple chlorination process as it used
to be. The increased production of copper precipi-
tates suggests why.
Burketown, on the Gulf of Carpentaria, is
busy drawing attention to itself as “the natural port
for Mount Oxide.” The direct road measures 120
miles, or, if the talked-of railway goes round by
Gregory Downs, 140 miles. Surveyor Phillips’s re-
port was full of praise of the country near Gregory
Downs, and there is certainly copper in the vicinity.
Neither Burketown nor Normanton is a good port,
and their natural defects have frozen out previous
railway schemes; but the increasing importance of
Gulf minerals and the proved extent of the good
country on the Barkly Tableland have made a rail-
way to the Gulf a more attractive proposition than
ever it was before.
* * * *
An application by the local Mine Owners’
Association for a £20,000 Government subsidy to
trv to prolong the life of declining Gympie recalls
an earlier proposition by a Glasgow co. This Scotch
concern wanted a lease of 100 acres, in return for
which it was to sink a shaft 4000 ft. deep, and dabble
in the bowels of the earth. But Gympie was healthier
in those times, so it boiled over respecting the cor-
pulence of the lease, and it kept on boiling so long
that the co. got tired and withdrew the offer.

No posing can now be done by the South
New Moon, Bendigo, as it has come down to about
the nimble-ninepenny dividend stage. The °°es
not crush tons, but sticks to the old-fashioned
These may mean a ton or a ton and a quarter, so far
as the outsider knows. Anyway, work is profitable,
and, as the co. was endowed with a modest share
register when it started life’s travail, £BOOO was
able to provide five Is. divs. during the past halt-
year. The Board has reached out a long arm and
raked in 400yds. of new ground on the Derby line,
and The Bulletin hopes the co. will be made to
work it or else be made to lose it again for some
brawnier toiler to tackle. Most of the quartz got of
late has been from the 440 ft., 580 ft and ,
and the two levels lying below. As W. H. Cundy re
marks, it takes a lot of work with a Bendigo battery
to eat up all the rock broken in a width of from
80ft to 90ft. Westralian practices applied to the
South Moon would eat up the reserves in a jw or
two. But Westralian speed would snuff out the Bee-
hive and all its brokers, and would clear out mine
managers and secretaries galore; and as these are
the factors in mining policy at Bendigo, the doT *ey
gait is retained despite the loss of interest involved
in letting gold remain unproductive undergound.

“High Street”: The Bulletin has kicked
now and then at the lack of “go” in the management
of the Moon group, Eaglehawk (Vic.). The half-
yearly meeting of the South Moon, just held, elected
one director (chairman) who is 80; the ex-chairman
is 83, and three others are 71, 64 and 62. Perhaps
that average of 72 explains things.
Out Beaufort (Vic.) way alluvial min-
ing is on the jig owing to the Hope lease having
got on to a shallow run of gold averaging 3oz. of
fold per fathom. This part of the. Cabbage Garden
has provided a good deal of the precious metal at odd
times, but there hasn’t been much ambition to poke
away into the wet ground. At, Corolulup, the Lod-
don Deep Leads is at a standstill owing to the fight
over the directorship in London, seemingly just at
the time when the co.’s chance of a lifetime was
near. Wash got, so far, has averaged about 17dwt.
per fathom, and divs. would come on that grade of
dirt to any hut over-capitalised concerns. Carters
is another Bull-owned deep-lead show about which
little is heard ; and it is said that some of the Black
Range identities are perforating the basalt with drill-
holes round Trawalla to locate deep ground. So the
long-deferred shake-up of the Avoca system and its
tributaries may be near at hand.* *
To date, New Jubilee, Scarsdale (Vic.),
has mined 78,855 tons, won 24,5000 z. gold, and paid
£1 to the shareholders for each ounce gotten. John
Till manager, has punched down to 910 ft. at the
main shaft, and has the No. 2 shaft down to 600 ft
Here he has an east crosscut out 670 ft., so that
development has not been spared to pay divs. lill
at present cuts up costs as follows: Mining and fall-
ing 7s Ofd. per ton; hauling, Is. 3id.; crushing,
Is Old.; total, 9s. 4 Id. On development work
2s Old. per ton also went. His trouble has been a
big fault at 800 ft., but he has picked up the shoot
of gold under the break and followed it along tor
120 ft. So he has an inducement to push down to
900ft’. to try his luck there. # *
The decease of W. McDonough, described
as the finder of Mount Lyell, has started William
Dixon, of Hawthorn (Vie.), breathing information
In disputing McDonough’s claim, Dixon alleges that
it was he who really discovered the mine, fired the
first shot, and dug out the first dirt being helped in
the iob by McDonough’s brother Mick. At that time
McDonough had sold his interest to Dixon, and Mick
subsequently did the same, the buyer in the latter
case being the late James Crotty. Crotty, Dixon an
one Karlson then became the owners of the hill, and
later on there were added F. O. Henry and Karlson s
two brothers, Henry holding five shares and the others
three each. Dixon’s grip on the mine is still sub-
stantial, and he is also an amasser of bricks and
mortar and a heaper-up of real estate in general.

Sticht, of Mount Lyell, must thank his
stars for the improvement in the copper market. In
the past three months he hasn’t been able to run a
2 p.c. ore, and the missing 4 p.c. means a lot on an
output of nearly 25,000 tons a month, since 2 p.c.
stuff takes just as much mining and smelting as 2*
p c The market has, however, evened up things, tor
2n c at £75 per ton is worth just as much as 24
n.c.’at £6O. What Sticht must he hoping for now
is that the market will work for him for a couple of
vears- by that time his new power scheme and
other economies should be well on the way to com-
pletion. » * *
Mount Farrell, in the wilds of Tasmania,
is still handicapped by high costs. Its half-year
closed with a net profit of £2145 from 00-O tons of
ore which is less than 6s. 3d. per ton. The 692 b
tons raised produced 803 tons of concentrates, assay-
ing 53.90 z. silver and 58.7 p.c. lead, in addition to
which 357 tons were hand-picked fpr an average of
790 z silver and 65 p.c. lead. A total of 1160 tons
of high-grade ore and concentrateswere shipped-
The cost of production averaged £8 16s. 7d. per ton,
equal to £1 9s. 7d. per ton of jirude ore.

“P.X.”: The “Australian who has been
knocking a crust out of London” (B. 13/6/’l2)
says the A.M.P. remains “obscure and unobserved
in “an office in Cornhill that offers no means of
advt ” The office isn’t in Cornhill, and it does offer
means of advt. The first statement is a matter of
fact, while the other is one of opinion; but as the
Australian’s fact is wrong, his opinion is probably
not worth much. Anyhow, the premises in Thread-
needle-street were chosen, out of about a dozen sites
that were under offer, mainly because they gave the
society a chance to hit the eye of the half-million
or so of business people who pass daily.

Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings of
flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply to the Australian Agents,
W. and J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. Hurst-
house and Co., of Wellington, the agents in New
Zealand.*

The undisputed benefit of asbestos covering for
steam-pipes and boilers has been exemplified by a re-
cent scientific treatise of the principles of steam
conveyance. Tests made have established the fact
that 100 square feet of pipe covered with this
material, at a pressure of 2001 b. per square inch, and
coal at 16s, per ton, will save £7 per year, ana
prevent loss of pressure equal to 13.84 B. Th. U. per
square foot per minute. Bell’s Asbestos Non-con-
ducting Composition, supplied in cwt. bags, or
applied by experienced workmen at contract rates.
Bell’s Asbestos, Ltd., 315 Kent Street, Sydney.*
After considerable discussion on the advantages and
disadvantages of the mechanical stonebreaking as
against the handbreaking, the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) decided in favor of the mechanical
method, and, on comparing the various makes of
machine, decided that the one most suited to their
needs was the Bigelow Patent Stone Crusher. They
purchased a 16in. x 9 in. portable machine, and from
their engineer’s various reports on the matter they
are more than satisfied with it.
From June to September the Council crushed and
spread 3000 cub. yards of road metal at an average
cost of 2/6 per cub. yard; and the cost of spalls per
cub. yard averaged 2/4, making a total cost of 4/10
per cub. yard broken and spread. Their last con-
tract under the hand-breaking system amounted to
6/3 per cub. yard, stacked on the roadside, and
spreading 1/-; total 7/3, as against the 4/10 of the
mechanical system. Saving, 2/5 per cub. yard. These
figures speak for themselves to those interested in
the matter. , , , .
The Bigelow breakers, which are also largely used
by the mining companies of Australia, are manu-
factured by the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, who will be glad to answer en-
quiries.* „ * * 13
