A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, June 24, 1912.
My Dear Arini, —
Unreliable rumors concerning the strained
relations between Major Quilter, of the
G.-G.’s staff, and the Queen's Club are warily
stalking through this town. They are ail
armed to the teeth. The least reliable
legend hints that the great altitude of the
Footman-stealer’s head above sea-level brings
it within hatpin range of the club windows
in Macquarie-street. The Brute who, in
cold blood, stole the Only Jeames from the
fashionable Bridge dames’ menagerie is now
alleged to be contemplating a complete suit
of armor. The helmet may consist of a
heavy soup tureen (inverted) neatly glued
on to a meat safe.
The veteran William Lyne and his com-
paratively new wife have taken the Hol-
mans’ flat-roofed house at Bellevue Hill,
with all its contents, for six months. The
recently announced Dame Lyne had her
eldest step-daughter as guide and friendly
adviser in her search for a furnished nest.
“Si\” writes me from Tasmania: —
You’ll be glad to know that something more is
being done for that young Tasmanian girl of Green
Ponds who is placidly rearing an orphaned family.
Besides the contributions that have dropped in through
the par. on the subject, the local Council has now
been prompted to vote a sum, to be paid weekly till
the kiddies grow up and can punen for themselves.
The polo Whites, from Muswellbrook’s
sheepy walks, are in town for the Dudley
Cup episode at Kensington. They are
housed under the Denman roof-tree.
John Bull’s Ambassador to the U.S., James
Bryce, who has been turning an intense glance
on Maoriland democracy, is said to be a
homely official of the unfrilled species. An
acquaintance, who once rubbed elbows with
Mr. Bryce in a U.S. crush, says lie might
easily be mistaken for a plain citizen in the
evening tram-hunt business—-the business
that includes having your back hair chewed
by a hurried cab-liorse, while a raging motor
pushes your feet into the adjacent gutter.
Artist B. S. Minns writes from London to
a Sydney friend : “Saw the R.A. on varnish-
ing day. I have a watercolor hung. Coates
has a portrait on the line, and Streeton a
landscape (small). I missed Lambert and
Roberts. Fox has three, and Fullwood one.”
Mr. Bob Minter, who rented the Spencer
Bruntons’ luxurious nest, Elystan (up Wool-
lahra way), with its furniture, its green-
house, its motor, its velvet lawns and other
simple necessaries of life, is anchored there
till the owners return from their scamper
through foreign customs. The auburn-haired
Miss Minter, who is going to marry barrister
Norman Pilcher, won’t leave the parental
hearthstone for some moons.
The probate of the Dr. Piers Hatton will
the other day, reminds one that this de-
parted Lancet lived for a time at Rose Bay.
His prize dogs, his motors, and his wife's
frocks gave that mild water-bubble locality
a varied choice of tea-table s-ossip. He once
exhibited, with noisy results, his prize barks
at some annual conference of dogs in the
Old Exhibition Building. If that venerable
pile could speak it would open its walls to
detail the length and breadth of the howl
that occurred when the exhibitor and his
groom forcibly removed his canine dukes
after the judging. The authorities tried
vainly to obstruct the exit of the howl, and
great was the row thereof.
The owner of the mansion whose water
front looks right into Sydney’s Circular
Quay filled in this week end by dying. Every-
body who uses the ferries knew the house,
with the green lawns sloping down to the
water, but few knew Frederic Hamilton, the
owner. He was a North Queenslander, and
something big in the cattle business. In the
old gold-lined days of Charters Towers he
ruled the local steak market, and when the
historic Thady O’Kane lacked a subject
Hamilton supplied it. Though he was the
son of a. Presbyterian parson, the Northerner
didn’t take to religion kindly himself, and
was just about as “hard a case” as ever came
to pass hereabouts.
A parson with the festive name of Cake-
bread presided over the farewell social flurry at
Balmain to Dr. Sinclair Finlay and his large
and handsome lady, the other day. The
popular Finlays are going to trip back to
Britannia’s crowded isle to see if that damp
old lady’s rainfall is where they left it years
ago.
Camden Park, the large, square house that
stands in a mile or two of Macarthur-
Onslow land, up Camden way, has some
kind of hereditary right to feed and lodge
Governors and Bishops. The late dowager,
Mrs. Mac-Onslow, had, I fancy, one special
throne bedroom, which was never inhabited
by anything of lower rank than a bishop.
No poor relation ever snuggled under its
capacious eiderdown in those days. The
present Mrs. Mac-Onslow is such a kindlv
soul that this stern decree has probably been
bustled out of the family locker. in the
early part of this week, Lady Chelmsford lent
the light of her countenance to the auaust
home that lies under the Onslow shingle.
Mrs. Anthony Hordern’s new baby was
christened in the odor of Government House,
about the middle of June, and met the win ry
blast in St. Mark’s Church with a dimnled
protest. Lady Barttelot and Chamberlain
Nevill, the recently-returned Lebbeus Hor-
dern, Miss Gladys Bowker and the Society
buttles (Mother and Miss) assisted at the
naming of Baby Hordern. The pram, in
which this wealthy infant takes the air, is
said to exceed all local competition in its
aesthetic splendor.
As I write (Monday) muddy polo is rushing
across the wet Kensington course in pur-
suit of the Dudley Cup. which is just
ahead. A conspirator who hopes to tear the
'•ag of victory from the Denman team, says
that Scone has sent a mighty man—one like
Tubal Cain, who was a hefty person in the
days when this world was young—to help
the Sydney side against his flying Excel-
lency. This polo smiter is named Davies.
He battles in A team with the sporting
Garvan brothers and C. Withycombe. Mauve
shirt, yellow sleeves and white breeches are
the. conspicuous features in the armor of this
Knight of the White Ball. Says my tame
conspirator in a hoarse whisper: “The Den-
man game is won by combination. N.S.AV.
plays for keeps with individual effort. On a
bad polo ground such as Kensington (which
is more or less like a ploughed field at the
end of a tournament), science is a sorry nag
to spur to victory. Individual ball-clicking
and reckoning with the chances of the
moment, a deep hole, or a ferocious wart on
the face of the earth, must be the game for
the Dudley Cup. Personally, I expect to
see the claret-colored shirts of the Denman
team disfigured with the mud of defeat.”
Walcha contributed i s Ohio polo team to
the Kensington fray. When the four Nivi-
sons, of the Ohio sheep-walk, chase the
Dudley Cup, their gorgeous livery of purple
waistcoats and yellow and black fixings,
not to mention their extra special riding-
breeches, will turn some of their plainer
rivals pale with envy. Walcha wags (they
have a pretty wit at Walcha, and when
that town gives way to a burst of humor
all the planets guffa®. and Space holds its
sides, and Time bursts its waistcoat with
laugh er) make a libellous sort of pun or
jest on “Ohio,” but this affluent paper de-
clines to mention it lest it should be struck
for 2d. damages. The place with the “O”
at each end (suggestive of the lady in the
song who demanded tombstones at her head
and feet) was billed to meet the royal blue
lingerie of Muswellbrook to-day (Monday).
The Denman Exes., surrounded by the blue
silk-faced upholstery of their staff, crept
warily into the southern gallery at the first
Cisneros concert. “God Save” bleated plain-
tively like a lost sheep on the platform,
while necks were craned in a hurried search
of the usual vice-regal patch on the ground
floor. This is beautifully situated between
two Town Hall draughts, and is so close to
the music that nervous artists are transfixed
with the glare of the Government House
diamonds. The mid-southern perch gives a
more remote retreat to the vice-regal table,
with its perpetual bouquet. Cisneros, by the
way, had a small cartload of flowers laid at
the toes of her sft. 9in. of curved beauty
the other night. The scarce wattle, which is
only beginning to pop its golden head above
the wintry blast, made a handsome hedge-
like trophy.
The white hull of the naval training ship
Tingira hugs the placid waters of Rose Bay,
and flaps holiday bunting for Royal birth-
davs over the revised shell of the ancient
Sobraon. In south-westerly gales of the Tan-
gira grows a heavy crop of canvas awning,
presumably to prevent our juvenile navy
catching cold in its sea-legs.
Suet is the latest tonic for frayed nerves.
The society skeleton who prances round in
lean wisps of silk and velvet will object with
violence to suet as fat-making. But the
precocious child who is stuffed with jam-roll
till its nerves become comatose will roll
heavily along the road to a phlegmatic calm.
Suet-dumpling, says C. H. Heydemann,
Ph.D., is ballast for the excitable tempera-
ment. He recently told the “Diet in
Schools’ ” Conference (London) this pick-
me-up story: “Our baby’s dead,” said one
youngster to another. “It died of eatin’
ices on top o’ jam tarts.” The other infant
caught her breath, and gasped with envy:
“What a ’eavingly death !” This shows the
natural depravity of the juvenile stomach,
which hankers for gaudy poisons when it
ought to consume spotted duff.
The Boys’ Brigade ball committee is lying
in wait to spring its tickets on the dancing
man. He belongs to a hard-to-snare tribe of
mammal that threatens to become extinct
in even silver-gilt circles. So he has to be'
lured to a charity ball with a very delicate
bait. This year it’s a poster, plain and
fancy-dress hop, with a supper of solid worth
in the right place. Sydney Town Hall is the
place. The date is July 2.
For two years or more the foreign archi-
tects of women’s frocks have said three
things, mostly in italics with a French
accent. They are: “Crinolines,” “bolster
skirts” and “paniers.” These Caesars of frills
threatened the return of the steel dress cage
till the London press went black in the face
with enraged letters from “Paterfamilias,”
“The Man who Pays,” “Father of Dozens”
and other old inhabitants. Then the crino-
line collapsed, and the great Flouncers took
another reef in the already skimpy skirt-
measurement, out of pure spite, I suppose.
Between the balloon and bolster conspiracies
came the panier threat. It rumbles nearer.
Donkeys have never abandoned paniers. Man-
nequins recently giggled over them at Long-
champs for the benefit of the illustrated
papers. The genuine panier—the heavy cur-
tain of thick material dragged up into huge
puffs—is as dead as the crinoline or Charles
1., and needs a smaller funeral. The stage
will grip the defunct pretender, however, as
at seized the ankle-watch, and the actress-
hunting summer shark who flaps his fins in
five or six inches of surf. Sweet are the
uses of advertisement. The only French
models of the alleged paniers I have seen in
this town, merely added an inch or so to the
size of the hips. They were thin clouds of
gauze, that fell down dead like a slightly-
gathered tunic cut away in front. The 1912
panier of Pai’is and Vienna is as much like
the original as a pancake resembles a para-
"chute ascent.
The N.S.W. Bookstall Co. has brought
forth a bright and cheeky shilling’s worth of
“Theatrical Caricatures” by Harry Julius.
They are served up with sparkling letter-
press—of the footlight blend —by Claude
McKay, press agent on the J.C.W. staff. No
press agent who knows his business as well
as Mr. McKay does could resist such a bril-
liant opportunity as this book of “Theatrical
Caricatures” for hitting back at hostile criti-
cism. Naturally, any press comment which
did not laud the J.C.W. productions to the
starry vault of Heaven would be regarded
as a sign of profound ignorance. That the
daily press has sufficient spirit to resist the
advertising pressure of a great theatrical
combine, and occasionally open its mouth in
mtspokeu objection to a blighting frost in
the person of an expensive importation, is
greatly to its credit. However, this is an
infrequent casualty, and Dorothea Baird
got much the same kind of notices as Ethel
Irving. McKay’s letterpress is so clev-
erly put together, in spite of the pin-sticking
pars, that the sneered-at kingdom of news-
paperdom has been very generous in its ap-
plause.
Harry Julius usually gets his first night
theatrical caricatures sitting on about two
inches of d.c. step, peering amid the fat and
fleshy shoulders of habitual first nighters.
Very often he is merely an alert atom with
a pencil, bounded on one side by the horsey
repartee of a race-night theatre party, on the
other side by the ospreys and bare shoulders
of the Least Dressed. When or how he sees
the stage is his funeral —not mine
Brass bands and other implements intended
for the welcome of Eva Mylott are now
rushing through their final rehearsals. The
Australian contralto will enter this city by
its blue and wet fi-ont gate on July 6, per
the Makura—assuming that all goes well
with her and the vessel.
Ida Scott, a young Californian soprano,
gave a song recital in the Y.M.C.A. Hall last
week. She’s a fine upstanding American
woman, apparently clothed in Paris. If
not, the foam of white lace with a flick of
apricot velvet under a saucy corsage of
filmy drapery is a handsome French
understudy. She wore this creation at her
song-burst. Miss Scott has been trained to
a hair, but her style of singing is cold and
artificial. A dull manner and a chilling
smile made the tender words of her opening
number —“Still wie die Nacht” —sit like a
hard frost on the emotions of the musical
crank. Ida’s voice is mostly pure and full
in tone, and much practice has made it flex-
ible. That enthusiastic Boston friend and
forerunner, Miss Maude Millard, was well
to the fore in the audience. Fiddler W. ,T.
Coad and the bright girl pianist, Mirrie
Solomon, supported the Californian voice.
Hungarian Thuness Kovarick and his
fiddles two made their first public appear-
ance here on Friday evening. The stamp-
ing soles of the galleryites kept their cold
feet a few degrees above zero —the Y.M.C.A.
Hall being as warm as a freezing chamber
with its icebergs in full blast. Kovarick is
not a Kubelik. His next offence is his youth.
Putting aside this crime, he is a delightful per-
former who gets a good deal of the Kubelik
singing tone into the music of his fiddle.
Extensive applause and a vigorous booting
of the floor brought Thuness of the fair
locks, back to the bleak platform, when he
ought to have been thawing his finger-tips
near an emei'gency stove. The youth carries a
second fiddle as excess luggage. He picked this
up for the Beethoven “Minuet,” a charming
performance. Musin’s familiar “Mazurka
de Concert” flowed from the Kovarick fiddle
with brilliant fireworks. It incited the gal-
lery to wriggle on its chairs, and agitated
the great gold dragons on the back of an
Exclusive Jap concert coat (worn by a
front-seat lady), till they squirmed to
mazurka time. Little Mrs. d’Aleria, an
accomplished pianist, who recently dropped
off the edge of Europe into this city, and
soprano Leah Myers added their little bit
to the Kovarick first-night.
John A. Singer (U.S.), who is managing
the world tour of youthful fiddler Kovarick,
suddenly unloaded a packet of Asiatic travel
reminiscences at a recent suburban tea-
snack. Item, one of the first flags of the
Chinese revolution. This was hoisted in
Shanghai, and is quite hideous enough in its
stripes of red, black, blue, white, etc., to
cause a revolution. It beats the intoxicated
cockroach on the Chinese imperial banner,
and even the old rattlesnake flag of South
Carolina. Item : Potted Chinese pigtail, neatly
braided with red ribbon, and packed (germ
proof) in a small tin covered with the hea-
then language of Ah Sin. Item: One Chi-
nese fiddle. This brought ’cellist Liebling to
his feet, in a wild attack on its wooden
music. A Chinese fiddle looks as if it were
sliced in a thin round stick off a deal butter
box. It has two or three lumpy bridges, and
exudes the se*~'' class of squeak as a day-old
kitten, when the boy from next door nulls its
tail. Other incidents included a Javanese
dagger, with a curly blade.
The French-Canadian tenor, Paul Dufault,
is the star of the Cisneros concert season.
On Saturday night his first effort, the fam-
ous aria from “Joseph in Egypt” (Mehul),
displayed a tenor almost faultless in its
purity, and perfect in the method of its pro-
duction from the silver-lined voice-box that
lurks behind the Dufault collar. The steady
twinkle of the Canadian constellation
blazed into the light of a first-class planet in
the thrilling music of the “Invictus.” “I
am the Captain of my Soul,” the final line,
captured the sensational success of the
night’s song-burst.
•‘lnvictus,” “Morning” and “Boat Song”
were grouped together. They are set to music
by three New York friends of tenor Paul, i.e.,
Bruno Huhn, Oley Speaks and Harriet Ware.
The latter, who is responsible for the softly-
flowing music of the “Boat Song,” is one of
N’York’s well-known writers of melodious
verse and song. The words of “Invictus”
are almost household in English-speaking
countries, but the name of the writer is apt
to dodge the memory because Henley’s out-
pouring, “I am the Master of my Fate,”
sounds so like the spirit of Emily Bronte
stamping across a Yorkshire moor, carrying
the banner of revolution and protesting for
all she was worth against the Society of
Tame Curates. So Henley and Emily Bronte
have a tendency to become mixed. Anyhow,
when Bruno Iluhn snared the stormy words,
he set them to wild and whirling music, and
passed the result on to friend Paul, with a
meek request that he’d kindly “try it on the
dog.” “Invictus” leapt into public favor in
U.S., and is one of Dufault’s notable tri-
umphs.
Eleonora de Cisneros is a magnificent,
warm-blooded creature, who ought to _ be
preserved for ever in her present physical
perfection of wind and limb. Just here the
longevity germ might be tested. It is
absurd waste for such a woman to grow old
when there are so many people who might
be buried and not missed. As a popular
idol, she appealed to the eye on Saturday
night in a heavenly creation (Paquin’s, I
fancy, seeing that that firm is dress-builder
to the angels) of blush pink. It was_ a
faint blush, heavily embroidered in silk
flowers. Across one majestic shoulder a
cunning dark velvet band outlined the white
flesh.
On the artistic side, Cisneros is not at her
best on the concert platform. Mentally, she
is feeling for the swish of her Delilah drap-
ery, the uproar of the Philistines, the thun-
der of the stage storm, and the support of
the orchestra. Mostly it’s the orchestra she
lacks. If this want could be supplied by
the Portus and Talbot combination, Cisneros
would blaze like an electric arc in French
and Italian extract of opera. German music
she ought to carve out of her concert season.
Perhaps this office might find an understudy
to George Washington’s aye, if Cisneros
would use it with violence on the “Ich grolle
nicht” group.
Cosmopolitan ’cellist Liebling, whose
father is the well-known German pianist
Eiebling, and whose mother is an accom-
plished Russian, is another brilliant alien on
these shores. Liebling played the ’cello in
public at the precocious age of 12. Since
then he has used no other. His Strad. has
the deep, sonorous bass, mellow with age and
good living, of a church organ. Its lighter
fantasies are like a breath of wind flirting
with its valuable strings.

Humblecrofts, were harbor-launched, and

One “Mallarapoonie,” well known to read-
ers of this paper, writes re the woman-doctor
in Central Queensland: “I often wondered
whether the lady medico was mostly profes-
sional person or just plain woman. The
other day I put her to the test. An infuri-
ated horse had left a beautiful blue imprint
of his hoof on one of my legs, also a deep
gash in the same place. Thirty miles away
there was a feminine medico, so we saddled
up the handiest petrol cart we could find,
and honked awav to find the Lady Cure. It
was the woman, not the medical person, who
asked me, straight off, if I could bear much
pam. I said I wasn’t Sure; she’d better
try me. With a pretty nurse at her elbow,
she started business. Her touch was deft
and strong but fairly heavy fox' a woman,
and she was quite a little Woman, too. Her
work was splendid, blit I own she turned me
up. Have diagnosed the weak place in hav-
mg a lady doctor—a man can’t say the
tilings lied hurl at the male medico who
fished gravel and loose threads of trouser out
ot an open wound. He’s got to suffer in
silence. It s the silence that hurts most, I
reckon.
At the bleak tail of another Royal birth-
day, I subscribe myself,
VANDORIAN.
“Fore Peak,” from the scupper:—
Most, if not all, of the Australian railways harry
the British ’warsman at half-rates. Often the same
railways also ferry the visiting foreign ’warsman
free over the face of the globe. But the plain
common, no-account bluejacket of the Australian
navy pays full fare every time. Now, he doesn’t
ob.ect to his Bull comrade getting a concession, but
he reckons that, as he will probably be the first
person called for when the time comes to defend
the lines he might at least get an occasional cheap
" de «°A then ?\ Sthl, knowing the boundless love of
the States which own the railways .for the Common-
wealth which owns the navy, Australian Jack is
rather timid about mentioning the subject too loudly,-
lest the local politicians might all break their necks'
in their hurry to hand out the concession.
A record of the strikes of the United King-
dom has been published. It shows their dur-
ation and how they ended- — i.e., how much
was added to or taken off the total yearly
wages paid in the trades involved. The last
11 years show seven years’ of decrease and
™ i y i ncrease - The decreases were
±18,144,412, and the increases £15,088,310.
Loss to the-employees on the 11 years—a*
trifle over ±3,000,000 per annum. Truly,
Bull’s is a Cheap Labor Freetrade land. If
the record is carried back to 1898-9-1900,
things can be made to look better for the
employees, but these days are a long way off.
It is the wage slump which began in 1901
that is the present cause of trouble.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 24, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Australian Parliament commenced
again last week with the usual overture by
postillions, powdered Jeames, carriage horses
and cocked hats. A rainbow of purple,
scarlet and gold surrounded the little G.-G.
when he dashed up to the grey steps. His
Ex. looked small, very much overlaced, and
rather cross-tempered. He had travelled all
the way from Sydney to take part in the
splendid harlequinade, and he may have been
afflicted with doubts as to the wisdom of the
proceeding. Two outsizes in Helps attended
him. Barttelot was one of them, and his
great, fat busby had a warm look alongside
the sharp cocked hat topped with feathers
that struggled to fly away on the wings of
the icy wind. The show was billed for two
o’clock, but did not commence to time —pos-
sibly because the chief actor had left his lady
in Sydney. In these cases it is the wife,
presumably, who worries the Ex. into punc-
tuality. An opening of Parliament is the
sort of function to which a woman would
naturally hurry. Military dignitaries faced
the wind and the gaze of the populace from
the top of the steps, while, in the Senate
Room, women crowded together and ruffled
one another’s dresses and tempers with their
struggles to get a better view. They waited
a long time before they got anything better
to look at than the back hair of the more
fortunate cat in front.
The Fuller lady swept in regally (with-
out her John) in a cloak of pink and mole-
color shot silk. A big, black Gainsborough
hat perched on her fair hair finished a good
costume picture. Alfred Deakin was in at-
tendance. (Mrs. Deakin is regrettably out
of the social searchlight for a time, owing to
the death of her mother.) Mrs. Andrew
Fisher collected a number of friends in an
inpromptu fashion, and drove them upstairs
for tea in one of the beautiful rooms that are
lost amongst the corridors. Some new scarlet
carpet showed the way up the marble stairs.
Eater ofl, some of the members left the cor-
roboree and wandered in with thirsty looks.
Mrs. Pearce, with pink plumes in her hat,
and Mrs. Thomas helped hostess the informal
proceedings. Maoriland Fowlds’ two daugh-
ters, who travelled to the almost-forgotten
Coronation with the Fisher couple, brought
their fresh complexions to the party. Mr.
and Mrs. Manson also helped to represent
the Great Loan Land. Senator Long, of
Tasmania, appeared to be getting a bad
time on account of some unappreciated mut-
ton-birds he had sent to a Ministerial home.
It transpires that there is so much oil in Tas-
manian mutton-birds that it is proposed to
drive steamships with them instead of coal.
Also, it is alleged that they are good for the
hair. This would seem to put them out of
the kitchen as table birds; but the hardy
Tasmanians have developed an Esquimaux
taste for the greasy creatures. An English
visitor, Mr. Esson, wandered round seeing
how Australians open a Parliament. Mrs.
Agar Wynne abducted a large motor load of
women, and oiled them off to a tea-party at
Mrs. Molesworth’s suburban home.
By way of retaliation for the dance the
Mayoral Hughes couple gave, St. Kilda is
arranging a return ditto for Wednesday,
July 17.
The Gallery students have fixed their car-
nival for July 4, and are arranging an
Egyptian set. An enthusiast is going round
plaintively reminding and warning the sweet
paint girls that silk was not known to
Pharaoh’s people; whereat the girls wail,
for, since the “Kismet” boom faded away,
silk has been going very cheap on the bar-
gain counters and Asiatic satins are hated
by every draper.
The Victorian Artists’ Society intends to
fnake a considerable splash later oh. For
that dance, the interior of the Town Hall
is to be arranged as a Norman castle, and
the. dancers will, of course, dress for the
period. I am acquainted with a lady help,
or Marion Annette, who alleges that her
people went to England with the Conqueror.
Don’t know if that gives her a claim for a
ticket, but someone might ask her if she has
a plan of the family tree about her. Per-
haps family trees might be accepted in lieu
of tickets at the door.
Statistician Knibbs held the interest of the
Repertory Theatre Club, at the last meeting,
with his address on Goethe’s conception of
Mephistopheles. The man of figures showed
a depth of imagination surprising in one
whose everyday work consists of piling up
and arranging concrete facts. The address,
so I gather, was compiled from a lifelong
study of the gentleman who reduced the
Devil to figures of speech, and reported vol-
uminously on the percentage of brimstone
and sin contained in him. Archibald Strong
introduced and bowed out the lecturer, and
led the subsequent discussion. Artist McCub-
bin sat thoughtfully through the proceedings,
but shook his head violently when Mr.
Knibbs,. who was all right in Greek, quoted
Burns in the original Scotch.
Mrs. Mayoress Wilks has been wrestling
with the problem of her Malvern dance
decorations. The councillors emitted cries of
anguish every time the word “drapes” was
mentioned, for the new and deliciously
cream-colored walls looked too beautiful to
be attacked by people with hammers. The
hostess, however, had an inspiration, and
the place is to be decorated with pink, white
and red native heath. There is plenty of it
about now, and, if the decorators do their
work capably, the premises will be good to
look at.
Lady Fuller, who is Presidente d’Honneur
of the Alliance Frangaise of Victoria, made
her first appearance last week at the occa-
sional soirees that society indulges in. Ill-
health and other detaining circumstances
have always foiled the vice-regal intentions
hitherto; but her Ex. made up for lost oppor-
tunities by motoring along to the carefully-
concealed Independent Hall two nights in
succession. On Wednesday, a concert drew
the Best People in gratifying quantities;
but the vice-regals left early. Fogged tram-
ltes caught a glimpse of the radiant blue
and gold lady and black-haired Auntie
Phipps—both laughing consumedly—and an
attendant Help carefully screwing an eye-
glass into his face with grimaces that looked
like laughter suppressed. Then the fog swal-
lowed up the motor, its occupants and the
.loke at one gulp, and the tramites were left
guessing.
By way of a change, on Thursday night,
a short French comedy, entitled Tin Fr6re,”
kept the invited English wondering where
the Point of the jokes lay. The beauteous
Ken 6 Vanderkelen came in as usual for the
heros limelight, and Alix Lamande and
Marie Boisgard were charming girls in fhe
cast. Some dainty French songs preceded
the playlet, and there were violin soli and
cups of coffee at suitable intervals.
The Fifth Australian Regiment dance
made a large splash of crimson in the dense
grey fog of last Friday night. High-tonedi
military dancers always engage the Prahran
lown Hall for their revels, and are invari-
ably pleased with it and its floor. But Fri-
day is Prahran’s late shopping night, and
there were some lively struggles on the pave-
ment between motor loads of delicately-
trocked women and swarms of curious shop-
pers armed with hatpins and knobby parcels,
lhe ambling trams were encumbered with
Hocks of expensively-clad aliens, who tucked
their pale-tinted evening shoes under their
dresses and sniffed fiercely when the battered
vegetable basket or the homing parcel of
sausages was pushed too close to their fin-
ery. At nine o’clock, people heavily uni-
formed and gold-laced danced a more or less
stately measure. Major-General Kirkpatrick
one of tbe Courtney colonel’s wives.
(Ihis statement should be carefully read •
there are two Colonels Courtney, and each
on £r Wife A No bi S a my is implied.) The
other Mrs. Courtney paced by the side of
Major Tivey. General Gordon and Colonel
Stanley towered like draped goal posts in
the set. Monash, another military man of
large calibre, brought his wife and daughter.
H was the daughter’s first big dance, and,
although she was not with the debutantes,
she wore the usual white frock. The buds
were a charming half-dozen gathered from
all parts. Some of them hailed from the coun-
try. 1 heir partners were military youths of
the regiment, and their red coats con-
trasted brilliantly with the white frocks. The
hilth made a merry night of it, for it was
the last regimental dance under the old
order. The new military regulations will
break up most of the regiments as they now
stand, and give most of the red coats to the
old-clothes man.
Lieutenant Henry, who secretaried the
show, pervaded the floor in brave attire and
a lull armory of smiles. Captain Maudsley,
not long a benedict, looked very proud of
M S iu young And Hennessey (of the
Melbourne City Council), who walks nowa-
days among whispers of future Gaud May-
oral honors, brought his lady in an ermine-
trimmed gown. Naval Commandant Tickell
was absent owing to the serious illness of his
helpmate, but sailor’s uniforms were dotted
about the room. A few kilts swirled pathe-
tically amid the trousered uniforms. The
little skirts, like the red coats, are doomed
by the decree of Defence Minister Pearce,
whose heart has never been touched bv the
catlike yell of the bagpipes.
A Governesses’ Club is about to material-
ise after months of patient work. It is
planting its tea cup, its bookcase and its
easy chair in the Centre Way—that brand
new block that makes an arcade between
Collins and Hinders streets. Miss Strick-
land, who has mothered governesses for
years, and helped them along their difficult
paths, is to be manager of the new institu-
tion. Miss Strickland says that the Centre
i a: L reall y her way, the rooms being
glorified reconstructions of her own apart-
ments in the same tram-sprinting avenue,
lhe short cut in older days led through
dark passages, around an overwhelming
smell of hair oil from a secluded barber’s
shop, and past heavy lorries, draught horses
and packing-cases full of softgoods for the
surrounding warehouses. The Club is
a comfortable place where the work-
ing guide of youth can spend a leisure
hour when in town, and where the workless
sister will find tea and information as to
possible jobs ; The subscription has been
fixed at a guinea a year, and the place is to
be opened early next month.
Laurence Messy, a Frenchman with a
flowing beard, who kept Melbourne girleens
fluttering awhile, has wedded the pretty
daughter of Jack Arthur, the well-known
grazier of the Geelong district.
Pearl Pardy, the little Melbourne girl who
married widower Baldock, is back in this
city from India, and Collins-street sees her
regularly at four o’clock tea. Baldock, who
has something resembling a monopoly of the
Indian horse trade, is a big, bluff, handsome
man and a fine sport. Before her marriage,
Pearl had a severe attack of stageitis or
boardenza in the early Australian days of
“The Arcadians.” She is a niece of Mrs.
Earle, who was the widow of Hugh Murray,
the wool man.
Widower Dr. Kenny is going to marry
again. Olga Woinarski, the clever violinist,
shares the numerous congratulations.
Commandant Lee and his lady had a tast£
of Melbourne military hospitality on their
way from Tasmania to Brisbane. A num-
ber of big guns and their wives uncorked lots
of champagne at a complimentary dinner.
A ext day, the hospitable people trooped down
to the station and waved handkerchiefs at
the departing Sydney express.
Edward Dyson has written a play for
which the Repertory Theatre management
require a particularly impetuous stage-lover.
Gregan McMahon says it is easy enough to
get girls to make love on the stage, but that
men stage-lovers are an entirely different
proposition. They are scarce at any time,
and when found are fearfully self-conscious
creatures even in the quiet love scenes
which demand no raptures. But the lover in
the Dyson play has to dash on to the stage
without any preliminaries and start to make
violent love to the girl before the audience
has recovered its breath. One or two in-
trepid amateur mummers have essayed a
rehearsal. They tremble in the wings, get
cold feet, hesitate, and—dramatically speak-
} ng are lost. Then the placard has been
hung out again: “Wanted a Stage-Lover.
Must be Tempestuous and Headlong. No
Sluggards need apply.”
, r?S h6m 1 e .” drew a heavily-gilt and top-
hatted audience to Her Majesty’s on Thurs-
day night. The station people have been
drawn to town out of season by the Quinlan
co., and they flocked along to stalls and
boxes ana talked rain points in the intervals.
A perfect swarm or blight of the dull, col-
lapsible opera hat that proclaims the trav-
elled social moth dotted the sea of well-
groomed heads that swirled round the exits
at motor time, and there was a general air
or boundless wealth very comforting to the
night loungers who always gather round to
hear the oflicial carriage-and-cab roarers
bellow at the street.
The orchestra reeled off many yards of
dream music and covered itself with critics’
praise; but Melba’s Mimi is still too fresh in
the opera haunter’s memory for a younger
star to be heard impartially. While the
musical critics rage furiously together, let
it be recorded that Lalla Miranda made up
unattractively as the tuneful consumptive
lor no apparent reason, she got under a
hideous, high-piled, fair wig. Her own
glossy black hair would have been much
better. In the not-so-far back days, Bassich
(that most artistic and beautiful Mimi) wore
her own blue-black hair, parted over her
forehead and softly coiled. Melba certainlv
wore a girlish fair wig; but Miranda need
not have followed her lead —that is, if she
was following anyone’s lead.
“Carmen,” on Saturday, had a record
house, according to the box office. The
boxes on one side were laden with society
‘fats,” while on the other side all the stylish
lightweights seemed to be collected. Two
pens were filled with ewe lambs in the hail-
bow stage,, and pink-and-white youths of the
college species. Auntie Phipps and a Help,
who is at least musical by sympathy, sat in
the ordinary stalls like a pair of suburban
music lovers.
Rosina Beynon’s Carmen was a cheeky
flapper whose chief characteristics were an
impudent swagger and an insinuating elbow.
Sad to relate, the skirt she swaggered in was
an ill-cut garment. Robert Parker made an
Escamillo good to look at, and the boxed
flappers wanted to cheer every time he ap-
peared.
Lily Brayton had a most prosaic experi-
ence during the last week of the Melbourne
season. She is very fond of sweets, and
occasionally indulges in a quiet feast, in spite
of the fact that the proper care of a star’s
figure forbids much sugar of the edible vari-
ety. She was happily chewing some Turkish
delight behind the scenes, when something
startled her. She swallowed hurriedly—
so hurriedly, in fact, that the gold crown of
a tooth went down with the mouthful. Dis-
mal thoughts of appendicitis accentuated
the gloom of the Virgin Goddess during her
last days here.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The members of the Victoria League proceeded en
masse to Montefiore on Tuesday afternoon, and were
offered a true Way welcome, also tea and cake.'
Mrs. Herbert Bymill helped her mother to disperse
the foodstuff and so on, and Lady Bosanquet, in blue
velvet, presided over the business part of the affair.
Mrs. Russell Booth (secretary) and Mrs. J. R. Ander-
son (treasurer) read their report and balance-sheet,
the same testifying that the wolf of insolvency was
still far from the League’s doorstep.
An Adelaide amateur operatic society has been
formed. Mrs. Herbert Walsh shares part of the
guilt. She is the secretary of the new body.
Mr. J. C. B. Moncrieff, brother of the Railways
Commissioner, has been appointed Chief Engineer of
Railways in the place of Mr. Rntt, who is retiring
The “Moncrieff Brothers” have been associated with
our trains for years. The feature of their regime is
freedom from accident and the extraordinarily long
distances that they carry you for a very short
amount of money.

just like a mere British cavalry regiment, and as’a
result a small boy is now in bed. There is a
rumor floating round that police-court proceedings
ants* 0 be taken against certain unspecified malign-
Mount Gambier has a new recreation ground
which will be known to posterity as Vansittart Park!

Died the other day at Ru Rua Hospital, Nicholas
O Halloran, aged 69. The O’Halloran clan is a large
one, and by getting married to a Giles a couple of
generations ago it definitely related itself to all
Adelaide.
The May Club was filled to bursting-point with
Brain and body fatigue are quickly relieved by a
dose of Stearns’ Wine of Cod Liver Extract. It adds
tohe to the system, and puts you where you belong.*
Germans on Wednesday evening. Dr. Palleine was in

Mrs. Julian Ayers is in Adelaide for a space. She
and her husband are “on the land” near Bordertown.
Mrs. Darby (she who was Miss Crozier) developed
smallpox on her way back from Japan the other day,
and is convalescing from it in Sydney. The Norman
Brookmans, who have been honeymooning in the pic-
turesque but insanitary land of the Geisha, are back
in our midst unscathed.
The Adelaide Hunt Club had its annual run to
Reynella on Saturday, and finished with afternoon
tea at the Reynells’ house, where Mr. Carew Reynell
did the honors in his father’s absence. The acting-
host was Master of the hounds a year or so back, but,
like most men, gave up the cheerful sport on his
marriage. Miss Reynell and Miss Waterhouse both
followed, and traps and motors were thick on the
road.
J. J. Duncan, the Tory leader in our Landlords’
House, who was further disfigured by a knighthood
the other day, is a nephew of W. W. Hughes, a rich
squatter who helped to set the Adelaide University
on its legs. Duncan’s father managed the Hughes
station, and when copper was found by accident on
it, young Duncan was sent off with the news to his
uncle. The copper-find turned into the great Moonta
and Wallaroo mines; and the wolf has never since
made much of a disturbance outside the doors of the
Hugheses and the Duncans.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

POLITIES
POINTS
A LOST SUPPOR TER.
Senator Walker informed a meeting of the Liberal
Women’s Literary and Debating Club last night that
he knew of a lady who wanted to get her husband—
a doctor in the British Navy—-transferred to the Aus-
tralian Navy. When inquiries were made, it was
found that, under the Government’s legislation, a
doctor could not be admitted into the Australian
Navy unless he had taken his degree in an Australian
University. (Cries of “Shame.”)
D.T.: “ Won't you give me friend's ’usband a
job in the Guvment? ”
FISHER: “Nope!"
D.T.: “All right! Well, we won't vote Labor
any more, so there! ”
The attempted General Strike in England
collapsed even more suddenly than the simi-
lar business in Brisbane. A General Strike
and a siege differ in only one important
item. In a siege the assailants are outside
the starving city, and are themselves well-
fed. In a general strike they are inside, and
are probably worse fed than the rest of the
inhabitants. It is Lazarus trying to starve
out Dives. If the strikers could start opera-
tions with 100,000,000 61b. tins of meat on
hand they might have a chance. But if they
had the price of all that meat about them
they probably wouldn’t strike.
“Iyles Innison”: Premier McGowen’s
Thanksgiving Sunday idea, as a means of
celebrating the breakage of the drought, was
characteristic of the tired pietist who leaves
pretty well everything to Providence. There
is no more sense in thanking Providence for
breaking the drought than there would be in
cursing it for creating the dashed thing. A
more profitable thing would have been
for McGowen to get a move on in the
direction of decentralisation, on the ground
that a Providence which believes in tlie pre-
sent congestion policy would have dropped
all the rain on Sydney.
THE ACCUMULATED BILLS.
The Prime Minister (Mr. A. McKenzie), in the
course-of an interview, said the loan of four and
a half millions being floated on the London market
was principally for the repayment of old loans now
maturing, for advances already received in anticipa-
tion of authorised loans, and for payment of Dread-
nought money.
MAORILAND : “I reckon the old gag about de-
veloping the natural resowces is about
played out, so there's nothing for it but to
tell the truth."
The bad habit: of doing Aus-
tralian business in London calls
for a brick or a ton of bricks.
A Melbourne firm, wishing to
"tender for some of the machinery
required in the proposed elec-
trification of the suburban rail-
ways, was referred to the Agent-
General in London. Another
firm, acting for a manufacturer
of aeroplanes, offered to supply
them to the Defence Depart-
ment in Melbourne. “Apply
to the High Commissioner in
London,” was the answer. All
the tenders for railwav mater-
ial required by the Vic. rail-
ways are practically settled in
London. Private firms are oc-
casionally guilty of this out-
break of stinking fish ; but the
worst offenders by far are Aus-
tralian Governments, which talk
about their love for the countrv at the very
moment they are busy kicking it downstairs.
The Maoriland anti-drink party has de-
cided to drive its water-cart into politics for
all it is worth. The situation is compli-
cated. As the law stands it requires a
three-fifths vote to abolish drink —that is to
say, 60 per cent, of the poll is sufficient to
carry No License. But 51 per cent, isn’t.
If a bare majority could prohibit the sale of
drink, Maoriland could be made dry. On the
other hand, a bare majority of teetotallers in
Parliament can enact that a bare majority
will suffice at the No-License poll, and can
abolish the three-fifths majority proviso. So
the bare majority in Parliament has resolved
to be up and doing, and, on the figures, there
should very shortly be not a single beer left
between Stewart Island and the Three
Kings.
At long last John Bull proposes to adopt
plain manhood suffrage. For many years
he has been expanding the franchise, but
always with an eye to the property qualifica-
tion. Seventy or SO or 100 years ago there
were cases in which a county only contained
six electors, and they were probably three
cripples, two drunks and an epileptic, all
nominated by a big landholder, and paid a
small annuity by him to vote as he directed.
There were cases where a big city had no
member, and a bare patch of grass had two.
Things gradually improved and expanded,
till at last it was almost sufficient if one
could prove six months’ continuous owner-
ship of two rags and a bug in order ro
qualify. But it was never the human being
who was entitled to the vote ; the qualifica-
tion always lay with the two rags and the
bug. The new departure will increase the
number of voters by about one-fourth, which
is considerable. But the great change lies
in the qualification. A man will now vote
because he is an Englishman—not because he
uwns some furniture that was made in Ger-
many.
After all, though London is the
heart of a mostly-nigger Empire, it is
liable to be a cold city for the man
who wants to slap the tar-brush in the
white man’s face. When old Dr.
Creed, of Sydney, mumbled his pro-
nigger paper at the Colonial Institute
in the Big Fog, he couldn’t get a voice
to support him. As speaker after
speaker banged his arguments and de-
plored his piebald sentiments, the old
man grunted worse and worse. Finally
he ambled out with the safe challenge
to fight the lot of them in the news-
papers.
S. Aus. knows now why the State
Governments were so desperately an-
xious to block the Commonwealth Sav-
ings Bank. Of the S. Aus. Bank’s
eight millions, the State Government
has already borrowed five millions;
yet Premier Peake quoted the parable
of the Unjust Steward, the other day,
because the trustees kept a poor little
£200,000 in gold, instead of lending
that also to the Government. The
trustees replied, in effect, that they
didn’t know they were supposed to be
working the confidence trick;
somehow they had got the idea
into their heads, that they ought
to be prepared to pay depositors
back their money when it was
asked for, and pay it in gold,
too, and not in a State Govern-
ment’s indefinite lOU.
Extract from the Federal Parliamentary
report:—
Mr. Bennett : An attempt had been made to raise
alarm by saying that a rush was to be made by the
banks to get gold for notes.
The Prime Minister: Let them send up five lor-
ries for the gold, and they will get it.
Mr. Bennett : Nobody would give the statement
serious consideration.
It is a pity Bennett, M.H.R., didn’t give the
names of the persons who said that “ a rush
was to be made by the banks.” This paper,
at all events, has heard nothing of the asser-
tion, though it has heard a great many com-
ments on the fact that such a rush could be
made (it was done once in England out of
sheer political animosity, and the Bank of
England cracked up right there), and that it
is a very foolish banker who has all his
liabilities on demand as Fisher has. That
nolitician says that it won’t matter if note-
holders ‘‘send five lorries for the gold.” But
suppose they sent 10 lorries! And, any-
how. what kind of policy is it which quite
needlessly courts even a five-lorry rush?
There had been some alterations made to
the grounds of Parliament House (formerly
Government House) at Wellington, Maori-
land, and this is how the Dunedin Star felt
about it: —
The Goths and Vandals —a small army of muscu-
lar workers-—have transformed what was once a
vice-regal paradise into a reproduction of Sodom
and Gomorrah, Nineveh, Pompeii and other buried
cities.
That Greater Melbourne Council is com-
ing about the end of next century. The
other day the conference of municipalities
which is pondering over the matter, met for
the manyth time to draw up its report or
something. There are 47 delegates, and 19
were not too fired to attend. Only four
were favorable to the scheme propounded,
the rest either objected altogether, or wanted
amendments made, or opined that “the time
had not yet arrived,” or something. The City
Fathers said that owing to pressure of other
loafing, they had not been able to deal with
the scheme —they didn’t quite put it that
way, but that was the meaning. If Premier
Watt is going to make a genuine effort to
pass his Bill, he will have to ignore the ex-
isting tinpot councils altogether. But is
Watt game to fly in the face of Bumbledom?
A proposition by Prime Minister Fisher is
to the effect that all Federal ex-Ministers
should have free passes for life on all the
State railways of Australia. It would be a
poor sort of ex-Minister who couldn’t get some
kind of bagman’s job with his free pass to
help him keep down expenses ; and it would
be a hard-hearted party which wouldn’t let
a political friend hold a portfolio for a day
or two, or be an honorary Minister for a
month or two, in order to qualify as a life-
long deadhead. Quite a lot of unexpected
persons are already qualified if the scheme
comes off—Drake, Neil Lewis, J. W. McGay,
Dugald Thomson, Pumpkin Smith, Keating,
Best, Hume Cook and others whom the
nation doesn’t always delight to honor.
Fisher proposes, in return for the suggested
concession, to let all State ex-Ministers have
the free run of the Federal railways, so
traffic should be heavy. After all, there is
something unsatisfactory about this policy
of adding small unconsidered perquisites to
the open and visible salary. It leads to pen-
sions, free drinks, counter-lunch and all man-
ner of abuses.

PREMIER JIM : “Ana, by the way, while you’re
about it, you might mention to the Almighty

juck reservoir."
A cry of misery filled the air last week
in re that disloyal regulation which shuts
out from the Australian navy any doctor
who hasn’t taken an Australian degree, and
the air was filled with wild bellowings of
“Shame.” There isn’t really any such regu-
lation, of course, though when the man with
the English degree and the man with the
Australian one are equal the latter gets the
preference. The “loyal” push would, of
course, give the English medico the prefer-
ence—in fact it would probably shut the
Australian out of his own navy altogether.
A CANTICLE OF TRUSTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
To control by law the operations of trusts and com-
binations acting detrimentally to the interests of the
public.—- Plank 6 of Liberal platform.
The Liberals of every State were represented, and
the views of people living 3000 miles apart had to be
reconciled. —Joseph Cook on Liberal platform.
Let us control the trusts,
Let us make laws for them,
- Let us speak much of them upon balconies,
Let us make mention of the evil of their ways;
For in distrust of trusts is there hope,
And we shall have favor of the people.
Bring unto us a trust that we may see it,
Lo, if there be any such we shall smite it with
the jaw ;
For a trust is an abomination before the Lord,
And in our detestation of it there shall be many
votes.
The people cried aloud.
" Bread was up,
So was meat,
So was rent,
So were rags ;
(Also the prophecies of Wragge
As a kite),
And the people cried aloud and said:
“Who in the name of the profit hath caused all
things to ascend?
Yea, who the blazes has caused the price of living
to increase?”
And a still small voice made answer:
“The trusts.”
And the men of Belial stood afar off and said with
a loud voice, “Aha!
Bring ye us a trust that we may see it,
Yea, bring us all the trusts that have a name:
The Sugar trust,
The Salt trust,
The Beef trust,
The Bread trust,
The Tripe trust,
Yea, even the Beer trust, that is wet as the dews
upon a parched land;
For we be Liberals of every State,
And our views are the views of those who dwell
3000 miles apart.”
And one ran swiftly and brought a looking-glass,
And they that dwelt 3000 miles apart looked
therein and saw
The Sugar trust,
The Salt trust,
The Beef trust,
The Bread trust,
The Tripe trust,
Yea, even the Beer trust that is wet as the dew
upon a parched land.
And the men of Belial stood afar off and said with
a loud voice, “Aha!”

CURSE O' MOSES.
SCIATICA IS A
NERVOUS DISEASE.
Nerves Must Be Restored to a State of
Tone.
♦ —-
This Man, Crippled for Weeks and
Unable to Work—Cured by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills.
: +,
If you have sciatica or neuralgia, you
ought to know that the complaints are nerv-
ous diseases that can only be cured by re-
storing the affected nerves to a proper state
of tone. Sciatica and neuralgia are very
similar except that the pains in one case are
in the face; in the other, in the thigh. As
the nerves get all their nourishment through
the blood, a blood-making tonic like Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills is the correct treat-
ment.
Mr. Edward West, corner of Smythe and
McKellar Streets, Benalla, Victoria, was
crippled so badly by sciatica that he was
unable to work for weeks. He was finally
cured by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and he
gave the following account of his illness and
cure to a reporter :
“I was droving and sleeping out, and the
exposure at last told on me,” said Mr.
West. “A pain gradually came in the right
hip. It grew worse and worse till at last
I was in agony. For weeks I had to give
up work. Then the complaint might lull a
trifle, but the least change of weather laid
me up again. All the right leg from the hip
to the ankle was affected. I was rubbed
with weak turps or had flannels sprinkled
with it put on. I had the leg blistered from
hip to calf, and the skin was painted with
iodine, but nothing gave me lasting relief.
The leg was so bad that I could barely feel
the hot flannels. Many a time I could not
get in or out of bed by myself, and when
there I would lie quite unable to move and
racked with pain. It was impossible for me
to close mjr eyes till the paroxysms eased a
little. K’nife-like thrusts would dart
through the leg. I dreaded nightfall, as I
always felt worse. The leg felt stiff, and I
could nob put the full weight of the body on
it. I often had to knock off work, and per-
haps then I would be unable to go out for
weeks. One doctor injected morphia into the
calf to try and ease me. After reading a
little pamphlet through, I was so convinced
that Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills would do me
good that I told my son to go and buy me
a couple of boxes for a trial. I was in bed
at the time laid up. The two boxes acted
wonderfully. By the time the fourth box
was through I felt fit for anything. I went
back to work a cured man, and I’ve kept
free from sciatica ever since.”
When you ask for Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills don’t be put off with anything else.
They are 2s. 9d. a box ; six boxes 15s. 3d.,
and if necessary will be sent post paid, on
receipt of price by the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Co, of Australasia, Ltd., Sydney.*
