SPORTING
NOTIONS
Surrey county, whipped by Gregory and
Co. in May, to the tune of seven wickets, squeezed
home in last week’s return match by 24 runs. It was
the third defeat and second successive failure of the
Australians, but, if no bouquets can be handed them,
the occasion does not call for bricks. In spite of the
tricky wickets, resulting from the moist weather
conditions on the Murky Isles —conditions to which
seven of the Australians are unaccustomed —the team
gave a strong combination of seasoned battlers a
great run for the bun. Hobbs, Hitch and Strudwick,
all here last summer, were wound up for this en-
gagement, and had to fight for their lives. Strud-
wick, at the sticks, kept down Sundries. Hobbs,
one of the world’s best cover-points, performed a
diving contortion act in the last hour or two that
sent Queenslander Jennings pavilionwards, “run out”
for 82. And Hitch, the nuggety, express bowler,
who halts in a long run to do a giddy little side-
skip, bagged most of the remaining wickets with
much abruptness. Coming on top of the shock admin-
istered by Hobbs, the side-stepping of Hitch on the
last of a dirty wicket was too much for the Austra-
lian tail-enders, who, a little while before, had seen
Jennings and Smith apparently knocking off runs
with ease. Last week’s special mention of David
Smith proved prophetic. His third appearance dur-
ing the tour revealed him a rod in pickle with a
vengeance. Following a first innings 13, he
punched up an even century, much to the surprise of
Surrey. This, with the consistent performances of
Jennings during the last week or two, has done
something to reveal the actual batting strength of
the team. Off the bat, Surrey scored only 10 runs
to the. good, and Sundries, as in the case of Lan-
cashire, accounted for over half the winning margin.
It was a match with a “magic” or “fatal number.”
Tom Hayward began it with 21. In the same in-
nings Ducat made 21. The County’s second innings
sundries numbered 21. Australia’s sundries on both
innings aggregated 21. Australia’s skipper made 21.
The highest individual aggregates were: Jennings
114, Dave Smith 113, Hobbs 106. In the match
Jimmy Matthews, of Victoria, had easily the best
bowling figures on either side, with seven for 92.
All things considered, Hitch was fortunate in bagging
those tail-enders just when he did. After Surrey,
the expected soft snap was found in Somerset, where
victrov was achieved with a margin of 10 wickets;
Australia’s first victory since it downed S’Afriea on
May 28. Roy Minnett, of Sydney University,
smashed up the County’s first innings for 59, his fast
medium bumpers, shot from weirdly-waving arms,
bagging seven wickets for 29. His second innings
‘none for 32” still left him seven for 61—and'he
wanted them badly, for his five previous wickets had
cost 40 runs apiece, or thereabouts. Up to the end
of last week, Minnett’s best with the bat was his
second innings 64 against the M.C.C.; but he has
had few chances when runs were really wanted on a
wicket that suited him. Never a careful watcher of
the ball, the dashing Roy is not too sound in defence.
Tb e habit of going forward, practically on spec., to
the pitch of every ball, is no good on Fogland’s
uncertain wickets, even though it may bash up cen-
turies at times on the billiard-table pitches of Sun-
land.
* * * *
To-day, Thursday, the little man who for
some weeks past has been at No. 1 position in the
Australian batting and bowling averages, has a birth-
day. Charles Macartney, respectfully styled “The
Governor-General,” and disrespectfully referred to as
“The Wee McGregor,” was born in West Maitland
(Ma State), on June 27, 1886; which makes him
26 not out against the bowling of Father Time. He
has had two trips to the Isles of Murk. When a
lad of 15 he amassed 177 for North Sydney’s Third
Grade team, and when 19 made his first appearance
for N.S.W. in _the Brisbane encounter with Queens-
land, scoring 57, and capturing three wickets for
80. In the same season he knocked up 70 against
S.A. at Adelaide, and for N.S.W., in the 1905 Aus-
tralian Eleven, scored 24 and 25 (twice run out),
and bagged five (Trumper, Duff, Noble, Darling and
Howell), for 123. The stereotyped and generally
misleading system of “averages” reckons “per in-
nings,” but it is “match figures” that should count.
Omitting the small-fry fixtures at Oxford and Cam-
bridge, Macartney’s contributions in the 11 others
prior to the current Test were 88, 127, 208, 148, 94,
21, 117, 4, 111, 66, 49—total 1033, or 93.90 runs
per match. And if we put him to a more severe
test, and take the five toughest matches of those
finished, namely, Notts, Surrey (first), M.C.C., Lan-
cashire and Surrey Return, which include three
Australian losses, his scores are 88, 148, 94, 111 and
66 ; aggregate 507, or an average of 101.404 per
match. So much for his right hand. With his left
hand, and bowling in only six of the aforesaid 11
engagements, he has bagged 14 wickets for 148, aver-
age 19.57 per wicket. Though his trundling re-
quires more or less trickiness in the turf, he made
his runs on all sorts and conditions of wickets. So,
whereas most Australians are fair-weather batsmen,
here’s to the health of the ambidexterous, amphibi-
ous, champion all-rounder.
* * * *
Syd Gregory is somewhat of a Jonah in
the matter of spinning the coin. Out of 14 tosses
he has called wrongly 12 times; and as the first
choice of the wicket depends upon successful calling,
the record of the team shines the brighter in the face
of this continued ill-luck. This serious set-back is,
without a doubt, due to the persecution that two-up
has been subjected to in the Ma State, and if it
should conduce to the loss of a Test Match there’ll
be a massacre of Wowsers for sure.
* * * *
Ernest Hume, who died last week while
sojourning with the Australian Eleven in England,
was a man to leave a gap. He was a
champion in the great fight for Law and
Order in the Control of International Cricket.
In the N.S.W. Cricket Association he waged a long
and splendid battle on behalf of New Blood ; and the
promotion of many of the younger brethren has been
due to his efforts. As a buttress to the Board of
Control, Hume was second only to Percy McElhone.
His presence in the Land of Slush was due sqlely to
his desire to spend a holiday helping the colts to
make a good showing for Australia and the Board
of Control.
In response to the request in last week’s
issue the Sporting Editor has received sufficient
copies of Frank Laver’s pamphlet to keep him going
for a week or two. It is an interesting brochure
which calls for a more detailed notice than space will
permit of this week.
By the kindness of a friend who doesn’t
wish his name mentioned, this paper has received
a copy of. the balance-sheet of the Australian cricket
team tour of England in 1909. Some of the figures
are interesting. The total receipts in England
amounted to £12,941 10s. lid., added to which
the £287 os. 3d. taken at the Sydney and Melbourne
matches, made £13,228 11s. 2d. Of this the
Board of Control received only £lOO3 11s. 4d. in
return for the risk of having to foot the bill had
there been a deficit. Dr. Poidevin, who did sterling
service in arranging the programme, got £lO5, and
the 16 players cut up among them £7359 6s. 6d.,
or, roughly, £459 each, for a pleasant six months
or so holiday and trip, with all exes. paid. It cost
£915 to get t*he team to England by steamer, equal
to £57 3s. 9d. each man, from which it is evident
that the gentlemen did not deny themselves any-
thing in the shape of frills. Laver, in his pamphlet,
published in reply to the Victorian Cricket Associa-
tion, dwells pathetically on the fact that, at the
end of the tour, he presented McAlister with an
album of views as a token of goodwill. Of course,
it may have been a private gift of photos taken,
developed and printed by the august Francis him-
self ; but it is an irritating fact that in the balance-
sheet appears an item of £BO, branded, “To man-
ager, for souvenir, entertaining, photos, etc.?’ The
writer wouldn’t do Mr. Laver an injustice for the
world: but one cannot help thinking that
Mr. Laver regarded the Board’s representative
with the team as an absolute outsider, and handed
him one of the official souvenirs as a matter of
friendship. Which was calculated to make McAlis-
ter about 16 times madder than before. It is also
alleged that McAlister paid for his photo, but re-
turned the one he received from Laver.
The heroes of the All-Black Rugby League
team faced the Ma State combination, as advertised,
on Sydney Agricultural Ground, last Saturday. They
were a hefty-looking lot. The skies were unusually soft,
and the ground was somewhat that way inclined also.
But it was hard enough for the N.S.W. team, accus-
tomed to rock-like surfaces, to keep its feet and run
with ease. The Maorilanders were at a disadvantage.
For four days they had tossed on and buffeted the
eternal sea. They had played only one match to-
gether, and had not had time to develop their com-
bination or evolve a decent back machine. But they
were full of sand. For 40 minutes they kept their
end of the log up with splendid bursts of individual
play and wild energy that staggered the great Kan-
garoo team to which they were opposed. And then
their condition gave out—the buffeting of the aged
and cantankerous sea got in its work. They went
to nieces, and the Kangaroos bounded all over them,
and finished up a hard-fought game with a glorious
victory—27 points to eight.
On Saturday night the windows of Heaven were
opened, and the celestial fountains were broken up,
and it rained upon the earth in great style. Sunday
saw more showers, so that, when the teams faced
each other again on Monday, the ground was not
ground, but soup, and hope beat high in the hearts
of the Maoriland football swimmers. Even while the
battle raged more rain came down, and made the
soup thinner still, and turned the bag of wind into
a slippery, slimy thing resembling a globe of beef-
tea, and impossible to hold. Two previous matches
had churned the ground all up, and when Maoriland
captain Francis looked at it he lifted his eyes to
Heaven and said solemnly, piously, “The Lord hath
delivered them into our hand! Get going, you
tigers!” They got going, with the wind and rain
in their faces, and the back machine of the N. S.
Wales team rushing down on them like a storm.
Talk about a stalwart defence! It was simply mag-
nificent. The slippery ground deprived the Kan-
garoos of the advantage of speed. Man after man
was hauled down, half smothered in the mud, and
the Cornstalk line hurled back. The Maorilanders
made no passing charges to speak of —but they
dribbled, dribbled, like a baby farm on strike. The
forwards spread themselves out on the front, and
charged along together, with the dirty, slimy ball
passing up and down the line in a marvellous man-
ner. The local barracker knew it was all up when
the first half, in which the home team had the
assistance of wind and rain, ended with a margin of
five to two. And yet it was during the second half,
with defeat staring them in the face, that the Corn-
stalks played the game of their lives, and had the
Blacks battling right on the line over and over
again. But the Kangaroos had no idea of dribbling.
They strove to pass with their hands when their feet
should have done the work. The Blacks hauled them
down one after another till, getting the ball on the
ground, it was carried at the toe right to the other
end of the premises. It was a hopeless struggle,
though as game as could be. And when the whistle
blew the Maorilanders had added seven points to the
score of the first half, while the Cornstalks had put
on five. Had the home team played half as well in
the first half as it did in the second, history might
have told another tale. As it was, Maoriland won by
12 points to seven.
* * * *
The Rugby Union having climbed down,
the usual round of competition matches pro-
ceeded in Sydney on Saturday, and gave the under-
studies the opportunity they yearned for. And, cer-
tainly, they made the most of it. The result was
that all the victories were big margin affairs, show-
ing that when any one team found it had an advan-
tage, it pushed it right home. Glebe, the combina-
tion with the meat, put a fearsome-looking crowd
into the field and over-awed South Sydney by its
general appearance alone. In play South was no
better, and only managed to score a miserable five to
the 27 of the bounding butcher team. University,
looking clean cut and somewhat fine, appeared to be
at a disadvantage with the heavier Newtown crowd—
till play began. And then the combination of the
Student team told its tale, and Newtown was downed
by 14 points to five. The surprise of the day was
the thumping win of St. George over Manly; and
when the whistle blew, with St. George on top by
16 points to nine, the victors were the most sur-
prised of all —which is saying a lot. Manly must
have been making a night of it on Friday. Eastern
Suburbs vindicated its outraged honor as premier
club by falling on Balmain by 25 points to three,
and Balmain hasn’t been seen since. Even the ferry-
boats can’t find the old suburb. Western Suburbs
had a soft snap in North Sydney, and kicked up 14
points to the Hillmen’s six. In the competition,
Glebe and University are neck and neck with 13
points each. Western Suburbs has 12, and Newtown
10. Below that are single figures only, which have
lost all interest. For the owners of these figures
the competition is over.
* * * *
Undisturbed by the mighty events happen-
ing around, the Australian Rules section continued
the even tenor of its road. The excitement was
clustered around the meeting of Paddington and
North Shore for the return match; and followers of
the section were considerably excited. They met on
Sydney Cricket Ground and put up a dizzy battle,
which saw the Paddington crowd on top at the end
of the first quarter, with 32 points to 7. At half-
time Paddington had 54 points to North’s 27, and
was still going strong. North’s goal shooting was
horrible, and splendid opportunities were missed, so
that the third term ended with Paddington 61 points
to 33. The final quarter was a hurried affair, but
the North Shore was out of it, and the game ended
with North underneath by 74 points to 40. For the
rest, Y.M.C.A. smothered Newtown by 135 to 22.
East Sydney suffocated South Sydney by 82 points
to 14. And Sydney managed to beat the - Rail-
ways by 81 to 53—not a very decisive victory under
Australian rules. The struggle resumed on Monday,
when Paddington fell on the Railways by 128 to
16 ; Newtown shifted Sydney off the map by 56 to
17 ; East Sydney mopped up North Shore by 45 to
40 ; and Y.M.C.A. put a tombstone over South Syd-
ney by 118 to 12. Paddington now leads the com-
petition by 40 points to Sydney’s 36, Y.M.C.A.’s 32,
and the 24 of East Sydney and North Shore. The
others don’t count.
# * * *
The Soccer inter-State scuffle, between
N.S.W. and Queensland, at Sydney, on Monday, was
played in the face of a sea of difficulties, and in the
middle of a sea of. mud. Despite the disadvantages
some very pretty football was seen; and the crowd of
enthusiasts which braved the rain and general discom-
fort to watch the game, got its money’s worth.
After a strenuous battle in the bottom Of the slush,
half-time found N.S.W. leading with one goal to
nil. and the name anybody’s. But during the second
half the Ma State team seemed to suddenly recover its
kicking accuracy, and a series of splendid shots at
goal landed it victor by three goals to nil.
* * * *
The Quid of Discord. Maoriland Rugby
Union recently blew up in a burst of fury when it
received the invitation of the N.S.W. Rugby Union
to send along a few representatives for the Austral-
asian team which is to visit ’Frisco. There has been
tnJhble between the two bodies over money ever since
the coming of the Yankee University team, each body
having a different version of its liabilities. So the
Maoriland body, deeming it could not be done with-
out, prefaced all negotiations as to the proposed
trip a couple of months hence with a demand for
instant settlement of its old account. The N.S.W.
Union rejected the demand, told the Maorilanders to
go to Hades, and communicated with Queensland,
which jumped at the chance of being represented in
an international team. So now Maoriland is out of
it, and the players who might have gone are declar-
ing that their idea of a real good afternoon’s sport
would be to play football with the Rugby Union
Council —the Council to be the ball.
* * * *
An ideal Melbourne winter afternoon —-
bright, crisp and cold—brought thousands to Satur-
day’s football matches, especially those in which
South and ’Roy and ’Wood and Melbourne were con-
cerned. The former, expected to provide a close
contest, turned out to be as one-sided as a Liberal
Leaguer, the Red and Whites making hacks of
the Maroons, and getting further and further ahead
the longer the disturbance lasted. At the finish South
had acquired 66 points, ’Roy 28, and the disgusted
spectators a dour and argumentative spirit. The
other big game was a gorgeous episode. Melbourne,
having last year captured a capable rover and a com-
petent goal-kicker, has trained them into champions,
and there are no other specialists playing who can
come near them. ,The team persistently plays to
these two, and the consequence of this was that at
half-time the Red Legs had got 42 points to the
Magpies’ 26. Knowing they must win this game or
be practically passed out of the competition, the
Magpies, while partaking of refreshments, considered
their case deeply, and, on resuming, told off their
smartest plavers to attend to Melbourne’s two stars.
The effect was at once apparent. The rover roved as
briskly as ever, the forward went for the “marks
as usual, but always beside each was an obstructing
and competing shadow, and, when the final bell rang,
Melbourne had added only three points to its 42,
while ’Wood had put on 27—53 to Melbourne s 45.
The Magpies’ goal-shooting was faulty, but they had
fought hard for their win, and deserved it. Down at
Geelong, Carlton, rushed into the field half an hour
late through an excessively gradual “special,” struck
instant trouble through getting a man injured, lost
20 points in the first few minutes, and never got
within reach of the Seasiders again—Geelong: 79,
Carlton 60. The top-notchers of the competition
’Don—pushed the whippers-in—’Varsity —further into
the soup with a score of 120 to 52, and St. Ivilda
cantered easily away from Richmond with 86 points
to 42.
The most notable Ass. outburst in and around Mel-
bourne was the capsizing of Essendon by the despised
Brighton 16. The ’Dons kept ahead through the first
three terms, but in the last the ex-Bentites swept all
before them, piled on 25 points, and won by 66 to
’Don’s 55. The chance of the latter to reach the final
four is now none too rosy. Brunswick revenged it-
self on ’Cote for its one-point defeat earlier m the
season, by heaping up 52 points in the final min-
utes and winning by 40 points—9B to 58. ’Town
handed Port a surprise in the shape of a score of 51
points to 48; North put P’ran through its facings
with 72 points to 45, and ’Scray mercilessly raked in
149 points to Melb. City’s 50. These Citizens have
lately sacked their committee and other officers, and
acquired new ones, and this writer hereby urges them
to follow suit as regards the players.
* « * *
As anticipated weeks ago in this page,
Homebush (Sydney) Municipal Council arose in
anger recently and felled a tree right on to the neck
of the cyclists who have made travelling dangerous
in that suburb for the last few Saturdays. The
strip of roadway between Homebush and Granville is
ideal for road racing—but there is no room on it
for the general traffic and the cyclists; and the
cyclists have to go in the interests of public safety.
So now League and Union are offering rewards for
particulars of a good road-racing stretch, where there
is a weak council.
* * * *
The rowing men of Westralia and South
Aust. are having a somewhat undignified squabble.
It all started from the habit athletes have of writing
or talking for the newspapers. Somebody in Ade-
laide said that the oarsmen had not been welcomed
and looked after in Perth as they should have been;
and someone on the other side said they had, and the
Adelaide man was merely trying to “get into the
limelight.” And then details of free lunches and
paid-for programmes began to be exchanged, and the
Rowing Associations have started to send each other
polite but sarcastic letters, and in general a lot of
fellows are tending to make themselves slightly
ridiculous.
* » * *
The first challenge round of the Davis
Oup tennis matches (England v. France) will be
played at Folkestone (Eng.) next month. The win-
ner is to play America, and the winning team in
that match will come to Melbourne to argue the
matter out with Australasia. At present there is a
hitch in the arrangements. France and America agree
that the final should be played in Melbourne in
November; but England prefers December. Either
month will do Australia, but the Tennis Association
wants to make definite arrangements about the
courts. France has a very strong team, and the
chances are it will beat England, and settle the
argument about the date ; while the Yanks, although
they will put up a good fight, don’t appear to have
much chance against the Frenchmen, Gobert (who
recently beat Wilding), Laurenz, De Cugis and Ger-
mot. Although the Australian team is not decided
on, Brookes is a certainty; and if Wilding is back
those two will defend the pannikin. If Wilding is
absent, the most likely team is Brookes and Dunlop,
doubles; and Brookes and A. B. Jones, who has
just won the N.S.W. championship, singles.
* * * *
Balmain’s defeat of Eastern Suburbs in
the eighth round of Sydney lacrosse struggle levelled
things up, and put fresh hope into the sinking hearts
of the teams underneath. It demonstrated, as one
correspondent put it, that it was still “possible to
knock the top dog off bis perch.” The result of
Saturday’s debacle leaves Eastern Suburbs and Peter-
sham level with 14 competition points. Balmain has
11 ; Sydney 10, and the other five are apparently out
of it.
* * * *
Williams, the Englishman, is having a
cold time in his long billiards bout with Lindrum, in
Melbourne. The champion played the “Gray stroke”
considerably last week, and, on Saturday afternoon, he
increased an unfinished 400-odd to a 700-odd break.
Williams sits like a Chinaman brooding on opium,
whilst Lindrum is doing these wicked things, and when
at last he gets a turn at the table, his hands and heart
are numbed and he fails miserably. The champion
makes the hazards with wonderful confidence and
accnracv since his return from England. He’ll hit the
pocket off the red a hundred times in succession,
without touching a cushion. Yet he is improving
also at the top of the table game all the time. A
player of Williams’s comparatively limited resources
couldn’t make an interesting long game with Lin-
drum this season, unless he took a very long start in
a match for money.
THE BATTLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Los Vegas, U.S.A., Sunday.—Jack Johnson, the
I champion, says he is willing to post a certified
cheque for £3200, and give odds, that he can beat
the fireman. Flynn s answer is that the result of the
r ourth of July battle will be a terrible knock to the
“big smoke shift.” —Cable item.
Round I.
The men stood within hearing at the sounding of the
gong,
And cheerfully Jack’s seconds chortled; ‘‘Now, we
shan’t be long!”
But general astonishment pervaded all the place
As Flynn got in the deadly jab—Tons Gahddam
black Disgrace !
It fairly staggered Johnson, who was now observed
to flinch,
And some of Flynn’s supporters yelled: “Jack hasn’t
got no cinch 1”
But then the darkie came again and ripped a snorter
in
With: “Say! Ah guess mah mammy’s more a man
than you, Jim Flynn!”
The fireman countered gamely, crying: “Shoo I you
black hog, Shoo 1”
And for the next half-minute deadly words and
phrases flew ;
But, just as Johnson swung with: “Jim ! you cain’t
fight worth a dime!”
The First Round was concluded to the usual call of
“Time!”
Round 11.
The nigger didn’t wait, this round. With his ap-
palling grin
He flashed his gold-and-diamond teeth upon t
dazzled Flynn ;
And, standing ten feet off, he dealt a pulverising
smash
Upon the fireman’s tympana with: “Hoo! you
Irish trash !
With consternation it was seen that Flynn seemed
badly hurt;
But, still, he countered bravely with: “Ah, cut it, yez
black Dir-rt!”
Though reeling from Jack’s frightful laugh, the
Irish lad was game,
And caustically stalled him off until the respite came,
For, just before the gong went, Flynn brought off a
fine harangue
With: “Jack, yous is as handsome as a bald orang-
outang !”
This fairly rattled Johnson, and before he could reply
The call of “Time!” proclaimed the opportunity
gone by!
Round III — and Last !
No sooner were the gags off than they stood up in a
trice I
And it was plain their blows would now be anything
but nice....
Now Johnson’s education and great dialectic strength
Showed out conspicuously as he used his weight and
length!
He changed his former tactics altogether; never
smiled,
But led with hard, jaw-breaking words that drove
the fireman wild!
He called Flynn a flamboyant freak —a dilettante
pug —
To which the fireman only said: “Nit! Hear the
Talking-bug !”
Then Johnson, warming to his work, got clean all
over Flynn
With: “Ah’ll shore desquamate you, Jim, you poah
podophyllin !”
And: “Say! but yore a molecule, you lock-jawed
Gothamite !”. .. .
Just here Flynn’s crowd threw in the towel—and
Johnson won the fight!

NEGATIVES with the
greatest range of tone
values are secured by
daylight development.
The darkroom is obsolete, since science made possible development by
daylight methods. As a result, photography has become a more enjoyable
pastime, as well as surer in its results, less costly, and more convenient.
Tank development ensures absolute Uniformity of results and a greater
percentage of perfect negatives than is possible by darkroom methods. It
saves time by enabling you to develop a roll of film or a dozen plates or flat
films at once. It saves space, as any one of the tank outfits can be stowed
handily in a corner ready for instant service.
The Tank method of development, as exemplified in the Kodak Daylight
Tank, the Eastman Plate Tank, and the Premo Film Pack Tank, must appeal
to both novice and expert.

HOW SORE THROATS ARE CAUGHT
And How to Cure and Prevent Them.
IN the -days of our grandparents,
there was a general idea that sore
throats were due to draughts and
wet or cold weather.
People who had a genius for catch-
ing sore throats (as so many of us
have to-day !) always took great care
to muffle up their throats, to avoid
draughts, and to wear a respirator
during cold, wet, or foggy weather.
Yet they still went on catching sore
throat !
Nowadays, we realise that sore
throat is an infectious ailment which
has very little to do with the weather.
Cold, damp weather may be one of
the causes of sore throat, but only
because it lowers our vitality, and
makes us susceptible to the microbes
of sore throat—that is all.
These microbes or germs are the
true active causes of sore throat, as
they are of those dread infectious
diseases diphtheria, consumption
of the lungs, measles, and scarlet
fever.
The Germs of Sore Throat.
These germs are only too plentiful,
especially at this time of the year.
We meet them every day, every
hour, in every public telephone, in
every public conveyance, in every
crowded, stuffy place where we may
breathe the germ-laden breath of
sufferers from sore throats, convales-
cents, and people sickening for some
infectious disease.
At last, however, science has given,
us a safeguard against these risks.
There has now been produced a
germ-killing throat tablet which
cleanses the mouth and throat from
disease germs as easily and as rapidly
as dirt is removed from the skin. It
is called Wulfing’s Formamint, and
can be tested bv anyone without ex-
pense. (See note at the end of this
article.) No one susceptible to sore
throat who has not already tried
Formamint should delay to do so ;
for Wulfing’s Formamint is a trust-
worthy cure for that complaint, as
well as a preventive of infectious
diseases.
To understand the value of this
discovery, we must remember that
all germs including the most
deadly ones, like
the Diphtheria
B a cill i—are
living particles
of vegetable life,
ext remely
minute, but visi-
ble under high
powers of the
microscope. For
their proper de-
velopment, these
seeds or germs
must find a
“suitable soil.”
This they have
in the soft lining
of the mouth and
throat, which
gives them the
warmt h and
moisture they
need.
In such a re-
ceptive soil,
germs will flou-
rish like the pro-
verbial green bay
tree, until they
multiply into
whole colonies.
The above picture shows how the germs which
cause tore throat and tonsillitis are destroyed by
means of the germ-killing throat tablet described in
this article. The round plate, coated with a sub-
stance on which disease germs qu ckly develop, was
inoculated with the germs of sore throat, which
immediately began to grow. The right half of the
plate was then treated with saliva from a person
who had sucked three tablets of Formamint —the
germ-killing throat tablet. The result was that all
the germs on it were destroyed, while those on the
left hand (not treated with Formamint) grew
luxuriantly. Exactly the same thing happens in
the mouth of people who suck Formamint Tablets—
the germs are quickly killed and sore throat is
easily cured aud prevented.
In the case of an ordinary sore throat,
they merely produce symptoms of
pain and discomfort, which quickly
disappear when a few Formamint
Tablets are sucked.
If, however, the germs are of a
more harmful kind, they produce
certain poisons which get in to the
blood and set up diseases like
diphtheria, scarlet fever, or
measles.
This is where the value of Forma-
mint lies as a diseas e-preventive, for
Formamint is the most effectual
method known to Science of killing
these germs in the mouth and throat
before they become dangerous.
It should be clearly understood,
however, that
Formamint is not
a cure for such
diseases as diph-
theria, when
once they have
established
themselves in
the system.
But there is
no doubt what-
ever that, if
taken in time,
Formamint will
certainly pre-
vent diphtheria
and kindred
germ - diseases,
even though one
has actually
been in close
contact with the
patient. For this
reason, doctors,
nurses and sani-
tary inspectors
always take
Formamint Tab-
lets when they
have a case of in-
fectious disease.
Many distinguished persons have
publicly testified to the value of
Formarnint. To take only a few
examples :
Remarkable Letters from Prominent
People.
Ford Glantawe writes: “I have
been using Formarnint for sore throat
with most satisfactory results.”
Madame Adelina Patti writes : <C I
have taken Formarnint Tablets for
some time past, and have found them
very beneficial to the throat.”
Mr. C. C. Hutchinson, K.C.,
writes : ‘ ‘ Through the great benefit
I have derived from Formarnint
Tablets, I have recommended them
for sore throat to hundreds of other
people.”
It is this personal recommendation
which has done so much to spread
Formarnint’s fame far and wide, and
none are more enthusiastic about it
than doctors and scientists them-
selves. Among the latter several
have made exhaustive laboratory
experiments with Formarnint, one of
the most recent of which is depicted
in the centre of this article.
It should be noted that there is no
duplicate or substitute for Forma-
mint, because Wulfing’s Formarnint
marks the discovery of a new
chemical compound, which is manu-
factured by a patented process and
can only be produced by the firm of
Messrs. A. Wu fing & Co. The
real thing is sold by all Chemists,
in bottles containing 50 tablets. To
prevent substitution, the purchaser
should specify Wulfing’s Formarnint.
(Note. —Any reader of this article who has not yet
tried Formarnint, and would like to do so, is requested
to write to the makers, Messrs. A. Wolfing & Co., 17
Bond Street, Sydney, N.S.W., who will be pleased to
send a free sample and an interesting handbook.
Kindly mention The Bulletin when writing, and
enclose two penny stamps towards postage.) -:

No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.

instead of leaning them against each other like
a dried fish loafing against an oil-painting?

When the ruffian with a frown
Restrained from brutal murder
And dropped the weapon down
Then he clasped her to his bosom
And tears from his eyes did start
Her love aroused old memories
Which touched the drunkard’s heart.

One of the American land seekers has
found himself an Australian hride. She was
one of the smartest tea-maidens in a Collins-
street foodery, and all the exclusive Johnnies
who were in the habit of feeding from her
fair hands gave her handsome presents, and
intimated that they doubted whether they
would ever eat heartily again. The Yankee
person who has deprived them of their idol
intends to return to Australia, after fixing
up his American interests.
“J.” : A woman visitor to Melbourne, who
knows China by sight and smell, remarked
in surprise at the number of mandarin coats
worn here by fashionable women to theatre
and bridge parties. In many Chinese homes
the elaborate wrap is a treasured possession
that outlasts many successive human owners
and many epidemics. When the family dies
out, the coat is always liable to be sold to
someone who knows nothing of its ancient
history. I wonder if the wearers of these
imported coverings ever read about the Lon-
don girl who contracted _ leprosy through
wearing a wig made of Chinese hair?

seemed to irritate the scalp. I was advised

from the chemist, and lam .pleased.to say

Cuticura Oiptment on the scalp to prevent

“Briner”: Four little grey whaling steamers, look-
ing like war sharks, popped into Hobson’s Bay early
in June, and, after taking in coal, left for Maonland.
They were the Hawk, Eagle, Whangaroo and Whang-
arei. The two former are only 65 tons, the others
45, yet they came out from Norway under their own
steam. In the hows of each is a harpoon gun; and
attached to the head of each harpoon is a couple of
pounds of gun cotton, whose business it is to explode
when the missile pierces the hide —-said to be some-
times 2ft. thick. The idea is that the whale won’t
then feel too fresh when the boats come along. When
the animal’s soul has departed a perforated and
pointed nozzle is driven into its head, and an oil
pump gets to work. The Hawk and Eagle will use
Campbell Island as a base; the other two will keep
further north. The crews have signed on for two
years, so there should be good prospecting in the
Southern Ocean.
* * * *
“Billy T.”: “Sir,” bleated the vinous-looking cat-
astrophe in human form that had lapelled me, a total
stranger, on the sidewalk, “for the love of Heaven
give me sixpence to get a whisky!” After I had
heard his tale of a recent desperate encounter with
black bottles, and had been handed a word-picture
of the pangs that assail a man suffering a recovery
without the means of breaking it gently, I parted.
But the next time his life required saving at my-'
hands, I grew testy, and roundly charged him with
making a business of it. He admitted it. Nay,
more; he owned that he had never been what you
might call really blithered, and would, in fact, as
lief drink tea as beer any day.. “Fact is, mister,”
he said, “I discovered that nine men out of 10 won’t
look at you if you ask for pennies for bread, but
every man Jack will stop and listen if you put in
a straight, barefaced demand for sixpence for beer.
Two will lecture you, three will confound your in-
fernal impudence, but the other five will part up.
They like your dashed honesty. So I made the tank’s
craving my strong suit. No, sir, I’m no bibber.
Even this comes off.” He laid a finger on his car-
nation nib, and rubbed away a few blossoms. When
he has made a bit more he intends purchasing a
milk run, so he told me.
* * * *
“Snell”: On a list published by the Age setting
forth the odds in favor of the criminal as against
the police, I see that the dog-stealer has the liveliest
chance of escaping detection, the odds on him being
nine to one. It. just happens that I know a profes-
sional dog-stealer by sight. He was pointed out to
me at a fight in the brick jug on Eastern Hill, Mel-
bourne, one night. My informant was the son of a
clergyman, graduating as a “gun,” and the expert
embezzler of faithful hounds looked like a neat-
handed Phyllis in the habiliments of man. “But,
surely,” said I incredulously, “there’s nothing in
dog-pinching as a profession?” “Isn’t there,
though!” said the young “gun.” “Wish I had the
trick of it. But that gooey has a fascination for
tykes. He could wheedle a mastiff through a brick
wall. He’s just got to toot to faithful Tray, and
Tray is his.” “But what are the rewards of his
honest art? Who buys prigged poodles as a steady
trade?” The “gun” grinned at my innocence.
“Garrt!” he said, “you don’t suppose dogs are
duffed for their face value! There’s a bit in it that
way, of course; but mainly Gussie there is special
dog-stealer to the profession, and the cracks pay
him well. When a burglar wants an inconvenient
dog removed, Gussie beguiles the honest watchdog
from home, and catches his fee like a gent. If he
can’t steal the dog he hangs round and entertains
him while the cracks clean up the home. It’s easy
to Gussie, and his terms are spot cash.”
* * * *
“Iyles I.”: Does advertising pay? Well, a gentle-
man who has been doing the N.S.W. country districts
says it does. He called on Yass recently, and re-
marked that he was * the advance agent for
a big circus due there within a few days. He.
ordered loaves of bread by the barrowful, and other
provender by the hundredweight, not to mention
various delicacies for the somersaulting elephant and
the three-card bear. Next he got out an artistic
programme for the local season. It was bubbling over
with blank spaces, whereon, for, a consideration, the
business men of the town were cajoled into advertis-
ing their wares. The agent then collected his half-
sov. or so from each victim —and struck out for the
skyline. He stepped across the Drylongago River and
the Daddylonglegs Creek, and hasn’t been seen since.
Possibly he has been devoured by the somersaulting
elephant.
“Don Q.”: Uncle Sam having tried State control
in the construction of the Panama Canal, and having
made such a good job of it, is preparing to experi-
ment in, some other directions. He reckons the. next
place to show Private Enterprise how to do it is in the
parcel post system, which the said Private Enterprise
operates to the great detriment of the public. He
reckons that with a charge of ss. 6d. for an lllb.
parcel (to quote one price) he will knock P.E. clean
off the ground floor. What sort of a price must
P.E. be charging? In Australia, the Government
will carry an lllb. package for 3s. within one State,
or 4s. beyond a State. In England the speetre at
the rich man’s feast does the same job for lid., and
in Germany for a mere sprat. Samuel also has his
■optic meditatively glued on the telephone systetns
run by Private Enterprise, and calculates that he
could give as good a service for a. heap sight less
than £22 10s. per annum per subscriber. In fact,
the man who guesses he is the cutest circumstance
on record is beginning to allow that it is time he
got sense.

and you can smoke five or six of them. If you

IN BANANA LAND
“N. Peck”: Here’s a coincidence. During
the “general” strike, Coyne, the leader, issued
food coupons, and called on union and other
sympathisers to see that funds were provided
to honor them. Then I came across this in
an ancient law-book: —
Coyne and Livery : The name given in Ireland to
an assumed right of the Anglo-Irish nobles to estab-
lish themselves and retainers free of cost on their
tenant’s estates, and any similar practice. The first
term signifies the exacting of food for men.
So it appears that J. Harry Coyne was
merely following the ancient practice from
which his name was derived.
Some South Australians propose to tackle
wheat-growing on the Dee River in Central
Queensland. The Dee is the watercourse on
which Mount Morgan huddles. The Dawson
Valley railroad was last heard of wandering
down that way towards the coalfield, but the
railway has got more or less mislaid, and it
is suspected of being a sort of lost iron child
sleeping out in the bush without sufficient
clothing, and an expedition is away looking
for it. The wheat men think that the Dee
waters will help their crops, but the big
mining co.’s chlorination works don’t improve
these waters. Also, they rely on the rail-
way—if it hasn’t died of hunger in the bush.
CooktoWn distinguished itself the other
day by holding a great banana-wolfing con-
test, wherein Michael Feinn, the local cham-
pion, was knocked out by a ceriain Saxby.
hitherto unknown to fame. Feinn, puffed
up with vainglory, gave Saxby a start of six
bananas, and was finally beaten by one. Ac-
cording to the Cooktown press, there are
grave suspicions of foul play, allegations
being not infrequent that Feinn was ho-
cussed with huge green bananas of unfair
size, whereby he was so embarrassed in “com-
ing up the straight” that Saxby, whose ban-
anas were of normal size, “waltzed in a
winner.” Cooktown has not been so excited
since the Endeavor was beached there.
Exactly 50 years ago John Connolly and
Michael Power sallied out from the coast and
founded the township! of Gayndah. No town
has seen more of the ups and downs of pion-
eering life. A tearing, swearing village in
its infancy, it forged until it came to
be regarded as the capital of the great sheep
country of the Upper Burnett. Then the
sheep industry shifted west, and Gayndah
went to sleep for over 30 years, until a rail-
way chanced alone the other day, opening up
possibilities, and incidentally
■yfjgj&lng the almost dead and buried Burnett
metropolis. To-day the old town is going
ahead fast, and Toowoomba may expect a
rival in the near future.
Queensland, with or without its amended
Liquor Bill, is on the highway to become
one of the most temperate countries in the
world. In 1885, Auditor-General Drew re-
ported that in the previous year the average
Queenslander had poured 25£ gallons of
liquor, or £l3 12s. of coin, down his
throat. In 1911, the average ran down to
Ilf gallons, or £3 7s. per head. It is
doubtful if any other State in the world
has put the peg in to such an extent in
the time.
A Commission having been appointed to
view the prickly-pear curse fore and aft, just
as though it hadn’t been viewed fore and aft
a million times already, the cockies in one
of the threatened districts met the other day
to knock their ideas into shape. Spraying
the pest was voted no good, and digging it
out was too big a job. So the meeting
was ripe for an excuse to just let things
slide. _ The ingenious man of the crowd then
supplied what was wanting. “Where would
any of us ha’ been,” he asked, “if it hadn’t
been for the pear last drought! Let it alone,
I say—it will help us next time!” Also,
since many a. cocky fed his familv and him-
self during that drought on pumpkin and
goat, this paper puts it to him that it is an
obviohs waste of time to breed anything ex-
cept pumpkin and goat.
THE RECRUIT.
At a meeting of unemployed in Hyde Park 40 men volunteered to assist in the work of unloading
food cargoes. Their terms arc 35/- a week.
SHIP OWNER: “My friend , Mr. Poverty!"
“Pol”: Why does ' Disrby Denham persist-
ently run up the Union Jack at various coun-
try school functions, where flag-hoisting is
part of the biz? Is there no such thing as
an Australian flag anywhere?
In Senior Police Magistrate Ranking, who
died a few days ago, Queensland lost a valu-
able official. Ranking came from Hastings
(England), and put in a few of his early
years on Cressbrook, with D. C. McConnell.
Subsequently he plunged into the sugar in-
dustry on the Logan, where he gained little
cash and much experience. He afterwards
entered the Public Service and discharged
the duties of Police Magistrate in fully half a
dozen plapfjk, always with tact and effici-
ency. Ranking also served on a number of
Commissions, one of the latest being the
famous Commission of Inquiry into the work-
ing of the Liquor Laws of Maoriland,
wherein his impartial, level-headed findings
intensely irritated Mr. and Mrs. Wowser,
without pleasing Mr. and Mrs. Bung, and
Miss Bung and Baby Bung.
The Debtor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The debtor is a diner who fares upon the best;
He travels by the liner from gaudy East to West;
He wears a pallid waistcoat, he hath a shining face,
You gaze upon his phizog. and find it full of Grace.’
The debtor is a gallant who meets you with a smile;
Not his the ways of lenders—a genus full of guile :
Ilis paunch is often portly, his cheek is often vast:
And if you want his Future you’ll find it in his Past.
The debtor’s coat is spotless, his hat is often new,
What time the lender trudgeth in garb of seedy blue ;
When men who lend out money are mown by Ruin’s
scythe,
The debtor stands unshaken—his bosom’s lord is
blithe.
The debtor is a diner when lenders go without;
Ilis hopes are always vernal, his fears are up the
spout;
’Tis not his borrowings harass or rob his soul of
peace—-
No, No! He’s only worried when borrowings must
cease.
The dun who bites the debtor, a craven lout is he;
His boots are often holey, his pants of low degree ;
His step is never jaunty; his eye is never clear;
He seldom hath the solace of gaiety and beer.
He scowleth and he growleth when squand’rers gaily
dine;
He grudgeth them such trifles as raiment superfine ;
But debt sleeps on serenely in comfy feather bed,
The debtor is a diner, and his table’s ever spread.
The debtor is a diner, the dun a hungry slave;
The creditor a whiner from cradle to the grave;
The man who owes the money shall see the lender
toil
To make his days be sunny and find him corn and oil;
When primal dawn was waking and Life was in the
bud;
When earliest spring was breaking in fever through
the blood,
Then rolled the mighty mandate above Creation’s
span—
“ The dun is but a bandit; the debtor is the man!”
QUEENSLAND.
E. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, June 22, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
At time of writing the local walking dele-
gate of the Australian Union of Superfluous
Governors was still absent from the city, but
I haven’t heard that any anxiety had been
occasioned thereby. He will not be lost.
Kings have been lost, but not Governors.
North Queensland is plunged in gloom.
Bishop Frodshapa has had to resign his see,
owing to ill-health. He fought hard for Aus-
tralian Church autonomy, during his visit
to England, but without success. His
more important work in founding the school
of Tropical Medicine will long be remem-
bered in these parts, although it brought him
much abuse and vilification from cranks and
extremists in Britain, who wrote him per-
sistent and often ungrammatical letters. Not
knowing what the tropics meant, they
concluded that he was aiming at wholesale
vivisection, or wanted to dismember
the empire. He is referred to affectionately
in the West as “a good bloke,” and his suc-
cessor will have to be a man of many parts
before he canj-oust George H. North Queens-
land from the hearts of his happy-go-lucky
flock.
J. A. Robertson, the new president of the
Brisbane Club, and his handsome wife, gave
a big At Home in the Club-rooms on (Thurs-
day. The hostess trailed silver-grey satin
draperies with her usual grace. Bridge was
played, and music of an excellent brand
supplied. _ Other goods of excellent brand
were obtainable in the usual place.
On Wednesday night, the Queensland Art
Society gathered itself into a mighty atom
and said good-bye to Evan Smith, who, as
secretary for five years, has been a pillar of
strength and a tower of comfort. The fact
was impressed upon him with some gold im-
plements for keeping the loose ends of his
shirt-sleeves from flying all over the place.
In future he will abide at Melbourne, where
the Australian Government sits pensively on
the unfinished tariff wall, watching the na-
tions heave across their cheap-labor goods.
On Wednesday the “Old Girls” of the
Brisbane Grammar School gathered in large
numbers at the Cafe Majestic, and glode
furiously on the light balloony toe. The list
of glodes did not include the turkey waddle
or the chimpanzee embrace. We are a slow-
moving people in these parts.
Last night was distinguished by the Corinda
private assembly. The little hall was a per-
fect bower of wattle, and the scent thereof
made me long more ardently than ever to
bring into collision the head of the “scent-
less Australian flowers” maniac and a hard-
boiled brickbat. Mrs. W. N. Robertson,
Mrs. T. B. Steele. Miss Grace Bell and Miss
Gladys Pollard all slogged in to make the
multitude highly delighted at the close of the
evening. Among the dancers I noticed
Macaulay Turner and Miss Beryl Spowers,
whose engagement is very new indeed.
To-night Miss - Porter will give her annual
dance party. On Thursday, Mrs. Hunter
Brown entertained a party of young folk
at a “small and early.” At Albion, Mrs. ,T.
Martin’s Pills are sold by all Chemists and
Stores in Australasia. Ladies recommend
them. Be sure you get the genuine with the
signature, Martin, Chemist, Southampton.*
A manly moustache not only improves a young
man’s appearance but brightens his prospects “to
get on” in the world. It makes him look older and
more confidence-inspiring. Capillarine is a moustache
grower with a successful reputation of 30 years
behind it. It goes right to-the roots of the hair
and forces strong, healthy growth. 10/6 jar, of
Messrs.' Craig and Aitken, 654 George-street,
Sydney.* Jfcfr
H. Flower did the same for the youngsters
of her acquaintance. These events were al-
leged to be highly satisfactory to all con-
cerned.
On Wednesday, Percy Carvosso, the well-
known footballer, and son of W. H. Car-
vosso, of the Supreme Court, married pretty
Joan Woodyatt, of Gympie, at the Wharf-
street Congregational Church. The bride
was encased in the usual satin and lace,
and was helped off the fence of spinsterdom
by her two sisters, Ivy and Tempe, and two
little maids, Joyce Hill and Beryl Carvosso
(the only daughter of Dr. and Mrs. Car-
vosso). Gordon Cramond and Cyril Carvosso
assisted the groom to toe the line. The moon
of rejoicing hangs in the sky above Tew-
antin.
Another wedding was that of Gladys Gor-
don, youngest daughter of Mr. P. R. Gordon,
for many years Inspector of Stock, and David
Crawford, of Maoriland.
Two more engagements are mentioned.
One is between Arnie Lavers, once of Bris-
bane. and Miss Ruby Wilcox, of Toowoomba.
Another is that of Gordon Cramond to fair
little Dorothy Chapman.
To-night the town is filled with joy be-
cause our Rugby Union team has defeated
the N.S.W. invaders. Thousands of stal-
wart men are fighting the great fight over
again in club and pub and street-wav- Mean-
while, we are training our national defence
team to meet the invaders from the North.
I know, because I saw 11 kids being drilled
the other day.
Our Health Commissioner, Dr. Elking-
ton, and his well-liked wife, are due from
Manila by Japanese boat to-morrow. Their
home will be at the Belle Vue Hotel for the
next few months.
Mayoress Raymond lost a little son of six
last week, in the midst of a special effort she
was making for the Children’s Hospital; I
think less of Fate the more I see the way it
runs its job.
Yours affectionately.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The deadly North has just killed another
bright youngster known around the Gulf
mostly as “Dad” Creffield. The kid had
been in the Horse Guards once and drank
his first rum in Burlcetown just 50 years ago.
That and the climate, and perhaps a few
subsequent rums, cut him down in the flower
of his youth, aged 80 or thereabouts. His
wife, another Gulf pioneer, was even more
unfortunate. She lived to be only 70-some-
thing.
It now transpires that N.S.W. Premier
McGowen was “misreported,” and that all
the times he was represented as clamoring
for Private Enterprise as a support for hos-
pitals a unanimous mistake was made. lie
didn’t say the thing which was so generally
ascribed to him, and which he was so slow
in contradicting. In fact, he said the other
thing. He cordially believes in the Labor
party’s policy of jnaking hospitals a State
concern, and he always said so, though by
reason of boundless misunderstanding and
the deafness of the press a report got around
that he never said so, and invariably said
something else. Consequently, matters be-
came upside down.
Says Mr. Parson Richards, of the Inde-
pendent Church, Collins-street, Melbourne,
abusing the Defence Act, more particularly
compulsory service
If democracy is to prove its fitness to supplant
the old-time autocracies and tyrannies in the task of
government, it can never be by erecting a new
tyranny, in the name of defence. A luster social
order can never be raised upon a foundation of in-
justice or by ignoring the legitimate prerogatives of
conscience.
Rut Mr. Parson Richards forgets to mention
that when the lamb has raised a juster social
order, the lion will eat it all the same. If
Mr. Parson Richards is really so anxious
about the “prerogatives of conscience,” etc.,
what he ought to do is to go and argue
with the lion.

BEST FOR AUS-
TRALIAN CAME
Some rifle makers are always
talking about the strength of
their .product. You want
strength, yes, but accuracy and
dependability are the prime
requisites of a rifle. The espec-
ially strong points about Win-
chester rifles are their reliability and accurate shooting
qualities, but in attaining these, strength and wear-
ability have not been lost sight of. There are thousands
of Winchester rifles giving satisfactory service today
that were in use twenty years ago. Winchester rifles
are made in calibers for all kinds of shooting. The
Model 1892, made in .25-20,
.32, .38 and .44 calibers, is
widely used for Australian
game. These rifles are not
expensive, although they
are made to get game
and stand hard usage.

Census Bulletin, No. 12, recently issued
by Statist G. H. Knibbs, reveals the fact
that of 4,455,005 persons accounted for in
the Commonwealth on census day, 1,469,022
were married, 2,783,543 unmarried, 191,743
widowed, 4500 divorced, and 5597 lay low
and said nuffin. N.S.W. headed the divorce
list, having over 1000 more legally-torn-
asunder folk than its next neighbor. Of the
married, three were boys 14 years old; eight
were boys of 15, 25 were boys of 16. Two
of the 14-yearlings were padding the long
road to the old age pension in N.S.W.; the
other was at it in Victoria. Ten married
men alleged to be aged 100 years or more
still survived on census day. The toughness
ot these old pretenders is wonderful. Most of
the centenarians are fully 85, yet they still
struggle along. On the femaie side there
was a wife in Queensland who was under
14 years. Scattered throughout Australia
there were 18 married girls aged 14 years,
92 of lt> years, 343 of 16, and three of 100
and bogus (that is to shy, 100 and
over). There were more married men
aged between 40 and 44 than at
any other similar period, and married
women were most plentiful betweep
the ages of 30 and 34. From 35 to 39 was
the time when the merry widow was most
abundant. Unfortunately, there was nt>
record of the average age of the merrv
widow s husband when he died and left her
an average relict of 37. Nor ife there any
news of the average number of years they
lived together before he gave up the job. '

£2O REWARD for Information Concerning the
Whereabouts of CORNELIUS (CON.) BOURKE.
‘HE TRUSTEES EXECUTORS AND ACFNCV
COMPANY, LIMITED, of No 4M ®S
S ’ n ( M r o r e i Victoria (the Trustee of a Settle-
ment of C. Bourke), is desirous of ascertaining the
whereabouts of one Cornelius Bourke (commonly
known as Con Bourke), formerly licensee of the
Prince of Wales Hotel, Hamilton, Victoria, and who
is supposed to have left Victoria about 22 years ago
and gone to New South Wales or South Australia.
He sometimes went under the name of McDonald.
The above reward will be paid to the first person
supplying the said Company with satisfactory in-
formation concerning the present whereabouts of the
said Cornelius Bourke if living, or if dead, the date
of his death, name of the place where he died,
whether he. was married, and if married, whether he
left a widow and children. Information must be
supplied within two months from the date hereof.
Dated the 18th day of June; 1912.
ERNEST W. POWLING, Princes Street, Port Fairy,
Solicitor for the said Company.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad
to hear of a simple and scientific device which will
restore your hearing, no matter how old you are.
There is no mystery or secrecy about it; we describe
it fully and explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and in the
same way you may use this sound magnifier as an
aid to weak hearing. But, unlike spectacles, the
l Wilson Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and
comfortable. You can’t tell you are wearing them.
No one else can, either, because they are not seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off
them—the strain of trying to hear dim sounds.
What a blessed relief to have done with that
tiring strain !
Being made of the purest and softest Para rubber
they cannot be felt, even when the head rests on a
pillow. For the same reason, a blow on the side of
the head will not hurt the ear. In fact, they pro-
tect the ear, the tender inner parts that are so
senstive, from cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing
sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why these
facts are true, and why the Sound Magnifier restores
the hearing, we give an illustration of one as it lies
in the ear. You will notice that the Sound Magni-
fier is shaped
like a trumpet,
and so con-
veys even the
faintest sounds
right to the
natural ear-
drum. Being
of the softest
rubber, it can-
not hurt the
ear. It fits the
orifice of the
ear with preci-
sion and com-
fort. The di-
vision that
runs across the
mouth is to al-
low the remo-
val and inser-
tion of the
Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape
of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has
been chosen
after years of
experience a s
flic best adapt
ed to carry the faintest sound right down to the
natural ear-drum.
'bo persons who are nearly deaf, it is not only
trying to have people shout at them, but it hurts
the ear-drum. When age comes the ear-drum some-
times gets thickened, or loses its tightness, and
shouting does not act properly on it. It only
makes a confused and tiring noise. But the
Sound Magnifiers concentrate the sound in the best
spot for perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-nerve
completely, or were bom deaf, we cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could do that, in spite
of some of the absurd claims made to-day. But if
you can hear the faintest sound, do not despair.
You will never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people in
Australia and New Zealand, who have been cured,
and you can be cured, too. You may wear the Ear-
Drums day and night, and take them out whenever
you wish. Children can wear them (we have spe-
cial sizes for children from six to twelve). Even
a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.

trouble. Neglected chafings, “chaps,”
chilblains, cold-sores, or cuts may
also cause more serious disease in

Moths.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOON as pleasure pierces night’s
Deep, pulsating shadowings.
Moths, with sad, bright broken wings,
Flutter round the pavement lights;
Moths, that once had beautiful
Velvet wings, and lips as cool
As anointed altar-flame
Moths whose wings are wings of shame.
Once desirable and sweet
Amaryallis butterflies.
Lifting Faith’s untroubled eyes
Loverwards; their fall complete.
Now scorched moths with eyes oi sin,
Wine-stained lips and cheeks akin,
Rouged, transfigured, shamelessly,
Flaunting love illusory .
Once enlarged with love’s delight,
Now bewitched, bewildering
Ghosts of beauty, hurrying
From defiling things of night
That pursue like livid flames,
Burning ever-shameless shames ,
Theirs the passion that imparts
Broken wings and broken hearts.
Hope does not desert them quite;
Sometimes swift and fiery love
Comes, an emanation of
Tenderness ; a single night
Stays beseeching, blotting out
Evil dreams ; remains to rout
Seasons of despair that are
Aftermath of love bizarre.
Round the lamps they poise and flit
Scorching broken wings the more ;
’Tis a part unbargained for —
Luring love, yet dreading it!
Brooding o’er the desolate
Wonderment of life and fate,
Dazed with pavement litanies,
Strange, delightless moths are these .
Moths, whose brittle, burning wings
Shame the wings of butterflies,
Moths whose tired, pathetic eyes
Blaze amid the shadowings,
Where they shrink in darkened doors,
Terrified of Minotaurs ;
The espousal of a beast!. ...
Hell would spare that shame at least.
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
A Bit of Diversion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rp W O old men were sitting in the scant
I shade of a myall, what time the heat
shimmered till it looked like a lake
of dancing water in the distance. Beyond
the mystical lake the timber seemed sus
pended on the horizon.
The old men had a kelpie lying panting
by, with four vigorous pups sucking susten-
ance. .
The tall old man was bothered with the
flies, and waved a small bunch of leaves irri-
tably, but the little old man treated them
with the utmost unconcern.
“Phy d’ye not brush the flies from ye,
Tommy?” said the tall man.
“Wot’s the good?” said Tommy. “They 11
on’y come back again.”
Tommy had arabesques of whisker, being
bald in spots. He was bullet-headed, and
had little marble-like eyes which couldn t give
vou the least idea what he was thinking
about. He just sat with his arms on his
spread knees and twiddled his fingers.
“Well, ye moight think av the falings av
other people,” said the tall man, turning his
head away. “And, anyway, ye moight kape
yer mouth shut to kape ’em from lukin m-
S °The conversation lapsed, and the only
things which did not seem inert to the heat
were the four pups. The kelpie didn t seem
to care what happened.
Then a crow flew languidly on to the dead
branch of a belar and gave a disheartened
caw, and cocked his head at the two swag-
men. Perhaps he thought, “nobody seems to
die ”
And at length the Irishman turned and
Sai ‘T)i’ve bin thinkin’, Tommy, me bhoy,
whoile we’ve bin sittin’ here that this is a
day that is av peculiar interest to us. As ye
know, Oi’m a genius for dates, and Oi re-
mimber that to-day is a sort av majority tor
us, if Oi moight put it that way. In plain
wurrds, to-day is the twinty-furst anniver-
sary av the day that we furst became cob-
b6“l don’t know, but if you says so—well,
we’ll say it is,” remarked Tommy, who did
not appear to be given at all to sentiment.
“Av course ut is, me ould shport; an to
show ye how good and strong me mimory is,
Oi’ll give ye another instance of a quare
coincidence. years ago to-day we sot in
this same shpot.” „
“All right, Paddy; let’s say so, ennyways.
“Do ye moind, Tommy, ut was on that
day a felly in advance av shape rode up on
a chestnut wid two whoite shtockings and
a shtar on his forehead?”
“I remember. The ’orse wuz branded
“C.R. in a triangle,” said Paddy.
“Ter wrong, Paddy. It wuz C.K.
“If ye bethink ye awhoile, Tommy, ye 11
remimber that ut wuz C.R. Oi renumber
the brand particularly bekase the telly
squinted.” , „
“’E didn’t squint: he wuz cross-eyed.
“Well, we’ll say, just to be agreeable, that
wan eye wuz lukin’ round the corner and
the other wuz expectin’ to-morrow; but
there wuz no mistakin’ that it wuz an R,
and it’s just loike ye, Tommy, to conthradict
whin ye know that Oi’m roight.
“When I knows yer are wrong, yer means,
yer old bog-trottin’ villain” —showing signs
of irritation. , , ~ .
Paddy reflectively stroked his clean-
shaven chin with one palm, and he raised
his eyes to the crow who still cocked his
head at,them from the belar.
“Do ye see that black crow up yonder,
Tommy?” he said. “Just cast your eyes
up, me bhoy. It’s a black crow, black as
the ace av spades, an’ Oi moight mintion be
way av a change in . the conversation that
ut’s the same crow that have been tollenn
ye up ever since I furst met ye twmty-wan
years ago, an’ if Oi ’adn’t been wid ye he’d
have pecked at your bones years ago. And
Oi can hear him say, as he sits up on that
dead limb, ‘Oi’ll have him yet, whin he
hasn’t that ould Oirishman to luk afther
him.’ But that is away from the point.
What Oi wanted to ask av ye is, what color
is that crow, Tommy? Is he a whoite
crow ?”
“Of course he’s black.
“Luk agin, Tommy. Make shure before
ye spake. Isn’t he rid or grane?”
“Have sense,” said Tommy.
“Well, begob, that’s just what Oi was
thryin’ to get at, ye obstinate, cantankerous
ould fossil, an’ Oi just asked ye the question
fully expectin’ ye’d say he was yaller, be
way av your religious thraining, and just to
be contrary. And it is all av a piece wid
yer saying it wuz C.K. in a thriangle whin
Oi said ut was C.R. But ye would rather
die than admit that Oi’m roight.”
- Tommy looked a bit tired, and raised Ins
shoulders slightly.
“Wot rl o you know about an orse: he
said. “W’y, I don’t believe yer ever see'd
an’ ’orse till yer came to ’Orsetralia forty
years ago.”
“And what do ye know about a horse l
“I knows an ’orse because I’m a ’Orse-
tralian. If us ’Orsetralians don’t know
’orses w’y did they call the country ’Orse-
tralia?”
“Are ye an Australian?
“Course I am. Borned here and brought
up.”
“Oi’m sorry, Tommy, rale sorry.
Tommy’s blood fired up, and he asked
fiercely: “Because I’m a ’Orsetralian?”
“No, bekase ye niver mintioned the fact
before.” Paddy shook his head dolefully.
“An’ phy did ye not mintion that ye was an
Australian before, Tommy, so as to deliver
me from a delusion Oi was laborin’ undher !
“It’s the first time I’ve ever knowed yer
to labor at anything, Paddy. And what did
yer take me to be?”
“Well, well, well,” said Paddy, more sor-
rowfully than ever. “And it do show how ye
can be mistaken.”
“ ’Ow mistaken?”
“And here Oi was always taking ye to be
a kangaroo rat wid de mange.”
This hurt Tommy’s feelings more than
words could express. He sprang to his feet
and whooped: “Ter ’ell with the Pope!”
Paddy gazed at him in mild astonishment.
“Do ye moind, Tommy, that we made an
agraymint two wakes ago. that we would
niver inthroduce religious discussions in our
arguments excepting, av course, on the 12th
av July and the Holy St. Pathrick’s Day t
Tommy whooped: “Ter ’ell with the
Pope!” with increased emphasis.
“If any other man but you, Tommy, had
behaved in such a disrespectful way to the
Pope, Oi’d ’ave had his liver.” Then he rose
ponderously to his feet, and started to
thump one of his palms with his fist. And,
by gob, sind the Pope to Hades again and
ye’ll think that ye have met the man who
shtruck Buckley.”
Tommy became frenzied, and yelled: ler
Hell with the Pope!” finding the aspirate in
pYoitpiripntv
Paddy took action according to promise.
When Tommy rose stiffly he said: “ler
’it me, Paddy.”
Paddy waved the fist he had just used tor
business purposes, and cried : “Oi did. And
sind the Pope to Hades agin, an’ be the holy
shmoke Oi’ll make ye bounce.” _
“I never thought ye would ’it me, and us
old cobbers for 20 years.” .
“An’ phat did ye think Oi would be doin
an’ ye usin’ the Pope as ye would a felly in
argument at Grogan’s shanty?”
“Well, this is about the limit, and we dis-
solves partnership, becos I won’t travel with
yer any more.”
“Roight, me son, roight. An which road
will ye take, an’ ye take yer choice. Here’s
the River-road, an’ there’s the road to
Cudgellico.”
“I’m for Cudgellico.”
“Roight, me son, roight. And now for a
fair division av rations.”
The tucker bags were spread and the ra-
tions shared amicably to a tea leaf, except-
ing a slight discussion about the fairness in
apportioning the baking powder, which
Paddy called the “dydnomite.”
“And now about Jennie?” said Tommy.
“Jennie is moine, but for the sake av
ould toimes, an’ so ye won’t give me the bad
name along the road, we’ll draw for her.”
And he broke a twig and Tommy drew the
shorter and lost. “An’ now take your pick
av the pups.”
Tommy took one, and Paddy took the
next, and Tommy picked another, and Paddy
had the one that was left. The men then
opened their shirts and put their two pups
inside against the bare skin. Then Paddy
swung up his swag, and, whistling for Jennie
to follow him, set off along the. river road
without even a “good-bye’ ’to his 21-years’
cobber.
Even when he topped the rise he did not
look back. Tommy still sat where the
track to Cudgellico branched off.
Two miles on Paddy dropped his swag and
sat down. Then he drew out his clay pipe
and started to smoke.
By-and-bye a young man dropped his
swag by him and passed the customary time
of day, and sat smoking. At last he got
up and knocked the ashes from his pipe, and
said he would have to be going.
“As ye get over the roise beyant, ye
moight do me a favor, me lad, an’ widout
anny throuble to yerself or Oi would not ax
ye. There’s a little ould man wid whoite
moleskins sitting be the tlirack for Cudgel-
lico, or he was sitting there an hour ago.
Oi’d take it as a favor if ye say him cornin’
this way to dhrop yer hat, be way av a
warning.”
“Righto. Who is he? Don’t you want
to see him?”
“Av coorse Oi want to say him, and me
his ould cobber for twinty-wan years, more
or less. To tell ye the trut’, me son, we’ve
just ’ad a bit av an argument about a horse
we saw a few years back, the exact toime
of which Oi cannot remimber. He said it
was branded C.K. in a thrinagle, and Oi
said ut was C.R. And the argument got
warrm, and Oi lost me timper, and Oi
knocked him down.”
“And was it C.R. ?”
“Sorra wan av me knows for sure,” said
Paddy, meditatively, stroking his chin. “Ye
say, the siccond letther was a bit indistinct,
and the wan moight as well be roight as the
other.”
