Safety!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CAPTAIN BOLLARD, as lie landed at
Circular Quay, could scarcely be-
lieve that he was at last free of the
Eastern Pacific —free with the liberty of a
favorite horse pensioned for ever. From the
wharf he had driven past (lie bisection of
Bridge-street by Pitt-street. Me had looked
oht oh South Sea Island Corner, Where from
dawn to dark it is scarcely possible to avoid
Collision with a man with a mate’s certifi-
cate held in a Cape Horn suit —and felt
afraid that arohhd that corner waited his
Old Life to drag him back to sea. Well, he
knew South Sea Corner and Macquarie
Biace, and the worn sandstone flags before
the entrance to Mrs. Purcell’s little hotel,
and its big willow tree. He knew equally
Well that the willow tree had gone with the
dead men—once the strong men whose swift
certain feet had worn the sandstone flags—
and that the little old public-house had even
replaced by a new, glaring and uncomfort-
able bar. Yet old imnressions were stronger
than his Hew knowledge. He felt South Sea
Corner for all its changes ; and the fact that
a new generation talked copra and snail-
shell and M.O.P. and tortoise-shell and ivory
huts in a new bar seemed to bring the East-
ern Pacific nearer than it had been since he
had joined the Australian-bound ship in
Gizo.
Then he di-ove to the bank and satisfied
himselt of the remittance of his credit —a
credit big enough to keep him until the ar-
rival of the time to die. And after that he
]l l?b e cted the “desirable seaside residence”
offered him by the agent he had instructed
by wire—so anxious was he to be quit of
the moving element that had for so long
)een his home, h ollowing the choice of one
ot these lie had a week of moving in. and
disposing of his new furniture, and opening
up the cases of island curios that w. r<. j-uc
to remind him of 30 years of hard battling- -
pme apple clubs, and arrows tipped with
human bone and cassowary claws, and in-
tricately carved paddles, and pot-bellied and
indecent gods, and murderous stilettos made
ot cassowary thigh bones —and in that work
he forgot ali about the conflicts and Maiaita
and San Ghristoval and Santa Cruz and the
human hand pointing skyward from the
cooking pots in the village of Atta, and all
the excursions and alarms of his stormy life
before the little despairing steamer swam
up to where he waited and took him back
to civilisation from Gizo.
On the eighth day, having his house in
order, he walked to the tram from his little
home by Parsley Bay. His left leg was
stiller than usual. He walked with an ap-
parentJy rheumatic gait—yet he knew that
his ill was not rheumatics, but a withering of
skin and tightening of sinew as the result of
a partial barbecue at Sumarango 20 years
betore. And at the reminder back went his
thoughts to the old life —to the life that he-
ft 111 at. South Sea Corner, and would end, in
the fullness of time, at Parsley Bay.
Thinking thus, he forgot the tram, and
walked Sydneywards—living his close calls
all over again.
“That -was the time ! The cobbera cook-
ill’ in the steamhouse, and the big Guadal-
canal- nigger clippin’ me over the head with
a cobbera knife, an’ I choked him with me
hands! He couldn’t weather old Tom Bol-
lard.
“It wasn’t bad. though—clear sea an’
pumice bottom below, so you could see 12
fathoms deep, and a dirty carpet-bag shark
cruisin’ among the painted fishes.
“An’ the time the missionary asked me to
read prayers while lie was awa v. an’ the
on’y book I had was Nares’s Sailin’ Direc-
tions, an’ I read the nigs all the soundin’s
in Boston Harbor, an’ they said ‘Amen’
whenever I stopped to haul my wind.
“San Christoval! That was good tradin’
—pretty place an’ bad nigs. Pine trees on
white sand patches an’ lazy surf an’ the
black canoes, an’ the long-tailed bo’sun
birds, an’ a little gin with hibiscus flowers
in her hair—pretty piece she was, too.
“Many a close call have I had —old Tom
Bollard. In (he eld days I bought black-
birds with old Brownbesses an’ cutlasses
and Snider rifles, an’ 20 years after they
near did for me with the same guns. An’
the second time I wasn’t blaek-birdin’ —I
was recruitin’. Same thing, says you—
same thing!
“But they wasn’t goin’ to wipe me out for
that but for savin’ iwo men and a woman
they had tied up for the cookin’ pots....
But I saved ’em —an’ I got £(> a head re-
cruitin’ fee for the men.... The times I’ve
seen!....l made a hole in that village with
two plugs o’ dynamite. Some o’ the nigs
that got away ain’t stopped runnin’ yet.
“But the time I was tied up for the cook-
io' pots meself! An’ the supercargo’s hand
stiekin’ out of the cookin’-pot among the
sago! That was near —nearer than ever I’ll
be to it again till my time comes to take in
my papers.”
He could see it all bad an-
chorage, the drifting schooner, the yelling
savages ashore armed with old muskets more
deadly to the armed man than to the target;
and later, his dead mates —one dismembered
for the cooking, the other slowly roasting,
and, to fill in the intervals of his screaming,
the sound of the sea rushing and retreating
over the coral-trash-laden flats and tinkling
fragments of coral against the stones. lie
remembered the only thought he had found
then —that of comparing the sound of the
disturbed dead coral with the noise of the
shingle by the groins between Hastings and
Brighton when the English Channel falls
for a moment from the shore.
He could still hear the yelling of the
dancers as they celebrated his defeat and the
capture of his ship ; still see the noisy moon-
lit beaches torn by the feet of Sooty-black,
swan-black, glossy-black, copper-red men—
breathless, sweat-glistering, but dancing as if
they could never be tired. He could smell yet
the stench of hot, oily flesh and disturbed
sand ; could see still the young bucks decked
with frangipanni and hibiscus, and the old
women enjoying the screams of the roasting
man—more cruel than death itself.
The night thick in the shadows, the moon-
light silver on the whiteness of the sand,
the fires crimson on the forest blackness;
flying foxes working at the cocoa nuts on
the palms: the roll of the surf making an
undersong for the wonderful harmony of the
dancers’ chant:—
Au niara. Au mara
La hala hala
Itoo wau.
And then the sudden rescue, the white men
mad with the lust of carnage, morning break-
ing over a slaughterhouse in all that beauty,
and two Captain Cook runts or island pigs
granting under an orchid-covered tree lean-
ing over porcelain blue water, and the chat-
ter of the satin birds in the papaw trees.
Also, he remembered two old chiefs lying
there dead —two old chiefs like grey and re-
verend monkeys.
“Yes, it was a close call —that was. Bad
nigs they are. Admiralty boys are bad, too.
Hive me those womanish, slender nigs in the
Carolines. Graceful, too, all them nigs
about the Pelews—as pretty as any boy I
ever saw at l r ap.
“Blackbirding at Santa Cruz. Dismasted
near Angaur. Fifteen hundred miles in an
open boat on a provision of seven tins of
bully beef eked out with hat linings and boot
leather. Yes, that was a hard time, too.
“But there was the good times! The
time I got half a hundredweight of tortoise-
shell for a pound o’ trade tobacco, and ten
ton o’ copra for a dozen Jews’ harps and a
Piece o’ turkey red. Y’ can’t do that now,
though ; the nigs know as much as a pawn-
broker. But I had the good times! Give a
chief an old pipe that was too strong for me
to smoke, and a stick o’ tobacco an’ a mouth-
organ, and he’d give me a boy, an’ I’d re-
cruit my ship’s capacity in a fortnight.
“I nearly went out that time they took
me for Dick Pentecost down in the Hebrides !
Some of Dick’s recruits had piccaninnies
aboard, an’ he got his hair off when the
piccaninnies cried, an’ he took ’em by the
legs an' knocked their heads against the
mast and then dropped ’em overboard. An’
they got me for him two years after, an’ it
was a good job there was a chief there who
was my pal and told ’em I wasn’t the man.
They got Dick Pentecost a couple of years
after that, an’ Dick died slow* an’ couldn’t
scream any more a long time before he
finished breathin'... But I’ve been lucky...
All my close calls —tied up for cookin’ pots
one time, an’ half roasted another, an’ many
a time clubbed blue an’ green an’ black an’
yeller... Never be so near to death again
without doin’ it complete... Near it scores
o’ times... But now I'm safe. And money
in the bank—l'm safe!”
It was at that moment that the driver of
the motor-car called out. But he was a
second too late. Crushed under the hea\.
wheels was the body of Captain Thoma
Bollard, who had had many close calls, an.
now was safe at last.
RAN DOLPH BEDFORD
The Resumption of Hell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OLD Satan was not feeling well;
He sat and gloomed within his 11<
Ilis drooping horns were glum
For his domain, once proud to view,
Was but a tenement or two
In Heaven’s remotest slum.
For in a modern Paradise
A hell is not looked on as nice ;
So Satan glowered and gloomed.
The City Council to improve
The streets had told him he must move ;
1101 l was to be resumed.
The trouble was not architects:
The scheme was due to Christian sects.
These tenements of pain
Were, so the new religions reckoned,
Upon Jerusalem the Second ' v
A most unsightly stain!
He’d taken an (denial lease
Where could torture souls in peace—
The Irony of it! —
Ilis lease was cancelled; he must go.
lie contemplated in his woe
A notice brief to quit.
Put as the newspapers he scanned
Within a sheet from Maoriland
He found this thrilling news:
“A Presbyterian defends
The worm whose gnashing never ends.
He has for Hell a use!”
And presently to him there came
A copper, haloed in pink flame.
And said: “Go, gnash in peace!
We’ve orders got from Maordand
That these old tenements we'd banned
Through all eternity shall stand!”
And gave him back his lease.
N.S.W.
A.H.A.
